THE YELLOW BOOK.
With its fifth number The Yellow Book com-
mences a second year of revolt. It has undeni-
ably strengthened its position since it sprang in
April, ’94, from the brains of a few English writers
and artists—mainly of minor status—who loathed
Cockaigne conventionality and longed for the
license of France. The Yellow Book has now a
considerable public of its own ; and, commercially,
it looks likely to succeed.
• • •
From a literary and artistic poind of view its
success is hardly so sure. It has fallen short, not
indeed of the current standard by which magazines
are judged—in brilliant originality The Yellow
Book has no rival among its kindred—but of the
permanent standard by which we judge literature.
So mnch, before its advent, was heard of the
mute, inglorious Miltons, who needed only sing-
ing-opportunity t 6 gain their everlasting crown ;
of the Shakespeares who, for lack of publicity and
laurels, plucked an inadequate wreath of haw-
thorn from the village hedge-rows; that there is
some slight sense of dissatisfaction at the achieve-
ments which Milton and Shakespeare redivivi
have paraded in the light of Yellow day. One is
not quite sure whether the new apostles have
made their title clear to mansions in the skies or
in the flies.
• • •
This ft) comparative regret, but it is not, or
should ftot bp, positive depreciation. The all-
round quality of The Yellow Book is noticeably
high and singularly uniform. The art is mostly
ugliness—a'thing of blots and scratches—but
there is more than one illustration with force and
charm. It is, of course, possible to contend that
ugliness is a form of beauty—a sub- or rather
supra-asp Pct which the profauum vulgus cannot
see for ithelf and cannot comprehend when shown
to it. Only, as appreciations are relative, and
impressions are subjective, and there is no final
criterion of what is most desirable in love or art
or anything else, it is obviously better for the
profammi Vulgus to be rather Philistinish than
hypocritical—efinoe,it never knows if its critics do
not themselves really live in Philistia,
• * •
The verse Of The Yellow Book is poor in com-
parison with the prose. There is one striking
piece—John Davidson's “Ballad of a Nun” (in
vol. 3), and two or three others pleasant and in-
uring—notably Alfred Hayes’ “My Study”
(v01..2), and “Two Songs” by Charles Sydney
(vol. 4). Even these do not take first rank ; and
the remainder is the kind of poetry easily com-
passed at a certain pitch of culture—smart, cor-
rect and barren. Poetry, as a whole, may be
divided into think-things and pretty-tliings;
kernel-verse and husk-verse; the class which
draws uS by its truth and strength, and the class
which interests us by its glitter and melody. The
poets of the day, the poets of The Yellow Book,
are chiefly manufacturers of pretty - things.
They are deft phrasemongers, and juggle
marvellously with jewelled verbiage; but the
divine fire of the elder bards is not theirs. The
note of the superficial, the ephemeral, is struck by
nearly all. Take William Watson’s “Hymn to
the Sea,” in The Yellow Book (vol. 5). Here are
the introductory lines
Oram, O regal in bounty, a subtle and delicate hrgesa ;
Grant an ethereal alms, out of the wealth of thy soul.
Stiff r a tarrying minstrel, who finds and not fashions bis
numbers— .
Who, from the commune of air, cages the volatile song—
Here to capture and prison some fugitive breath of thy
descant.
Thine and his own as thy roar lisped on the lips of a shell.
Now while the vernal impulsion makes lyrical all that
hath language,
While, through the veins of the Earth, riots the ichor of
Spring,
While, with throes, with raptures, with loosing of bonds
and unsealings—
Arrowy pangs of delight, piercing the core of the world—
Tremors and coy uufoldiugs, reluctances, sweet agita-
tions—
Vouth, irrepressibly fair, wakes like a wandering rose.
These lines have been praised for their beauty;
and that they have a certain beauty of form may
be conceded. But they do not help us; their mes-
is but an echo; the description of Spring
impresses us as showing dexterous trickery, not
superior insight. And for mere beauty of form
neither these lines nor any which follow, over-
loaded with imagery and epithet, can be compared
to many verses of Brunton Stephens’ “Convict
Once,” in a. measure similar, but rhymed.
# # *
The prose of the Yellow Book is generally vivid,
forcible, and original. It is interesting to note
that some of the most vigorous work, such as that
by “ C. 5.,” sets a style which corresponds very
closely with that sought by The Bulletin. The
ordinary English tale has a small, weak point at
the end of a long, weak introduction. Occasion-
ally the point is strong, but the introduction is
almost invariably weak. The Bulletin asks from
its contributors brevity, and interest which races
from strong beginning to strong climax. This is
what “C.S.” achieves in several Yellow Book
sketches, of which “For Ever and Ever” (in vol.
5) may stand as model.
* m m
Henry Harland, Marriott Watson, and Ella
D’Aicy, have all given The Yellow Book brilliant
tales, which are being reprinted; with additions, in
the “ Keynotes ” series. Certain essays by Max
Beerbohm and Dauphin Meunier, and tales by
Hubert Craekanthorpe, have been particularly
clever; and “ Victoria Cross ” contributed to the
fourth volume a tale, “Theodora,” very striking
and audacious, The fifth volume is of good
average merit, and has one remarkably fine tale,
“ The Pleasure Pilgrim,” by Ella D’Aroy,

Handy Language Books.
As a language is alive it is as difficult
to bag as a cat: it slips through pen
and tongue as quicksilver slips through
your fingers. Yet, though no writer or
speaker can hope to be absolutely correct,
it is plain to everyone with eyes and ears
that some are more correct than others.
Within the last few years a tribe of little
handy-books has sprung up to point the
way to linguistic precision, and some of
them will repay purchase and study.
“How to Talk Correctly” (Bd.) quotes a
large number of common errors in speak-
ing and writing, and supplies corrections.
“Don’t,” a similar manual (Bd.), carries
censorship into the domain of manners,
and gives a large number of hints which it
is pleasant to approve without heeding.
‘Just the Word Wanted” (Is. 2d.) is a
simple book of synonyms, easily come-at-
able.
“The Handy Guide to Correct Pronuncia-
tion ” (Is. 2d.) lists words commonly mis-
pronounced, and supplies the proper
pronunciation.
“The Secretary’s Assistant ”(ls. 2d.) teaches
how to address letters correctly to C.M.G.’s
and others of touchy dignity and indignity.
“The Dictionary of Blunders” (Is. 2d.) sets
one right upon common errors of epithet
and phrase.
“Familiar English Quotations ” (Is 2d) is a
vest-pocket book useful as far as it goes,
but going hardly far enough.
“Everybody’s Writing-Desk Book” (Is 3d)
is cordially recommended as the best of
the lot, covering nearly as much ground
as all the preceding together.
“Common Words Commonly Mispronouced”
(2s 4d) goes into the finer shades of pro-
nunciation, and appeals to professional
speakers rather than to the ordinary ama-
teur variety.

Gallegher, and other stories, by Richard

Balaam and his Master, and other sketches
and stories, by Joel Chandler Harris.
*** With the doubtful exception of “The
Odd Number,” the stories, in this series
are rather recommended by style and
sympathy than by plot or vivid interest.
Davis and Harris have considerable Ameri-
can vogue, justified by their drollery and
perception ; but the average reader finds
them tending to dullness. Verga, Suder-
mann, and Anatole France employ the
charm of simplicity, not the fascination of
strength and splendour. Bonrget’s stories
are lilies, not roses of literature—unless it
be white moss-roses. De Maupassant’s
striking book can be recommended for
general purchase : the others require some
“ acquired taste,” some welcome of appre-
ciative culture. Paper, 3s. each.

O URSELVHBI
We wish It to be understood that the character of the
business we do, and the absolutely inclusive variety of the
goods we sell, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
We are also not far out, considering the low prices we
ask for everything we sell, and the consequent blessings
we are continually showering upon the people, when we
assert, not in timorous tones, but with the trumpet voice
of confidence in the truth of what we say, when we dub
ourselves
PUBLIC BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life—honest
citizens in every community, whose deeds stamp them
for the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
from the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stands
out in bold relief against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the same thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between the few
disinterested friends of the people, and the many selfish
lookers after the big profits they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little band
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
who, in self-abnegation and contentedness, are willing to
accept small profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but if the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then surely the laborer is worthy of his hire,
and the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
nevercease from their toil; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun they work, oppressed by the cruel frost of
somewhile baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it. has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
it has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will do the
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.
ANTHONY HORDERN 8c SONS,
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.

A Poet of the Push.
LA US LI BERT ATIS.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN .]
I He all lonely in my silent cell;
The heavy strokes toll out a dead hour’s knell,
And from the summit of the wall there cries
A warder—“ Ten o’clock and all is welll”
Silence again : the thoughts come back to me
Of great deeds done in days when I was free ;
Yea, deeds of valour which now otherwise
Than in remembrance I shall never see,
I see the fan-tan shops in Sussex-street,
The lonely copper pacing on his beat.
The push assembled looking out for larks,
And blokes at corners waiting for their “meat.”
The bright, glad days at Chowder, and the night
wu fc ™ tra P s oufc > and ''■he glorious fight
W 'lv[arks irllo aSOn P* u ££ ec * young Sheeney
For dancing with his tart. It was all right.
And then I think of this, the last best thing
ihat made me to the push a crimson king,
111 tell you how it chanced, my great, bright
deed,
That raised me up and left me sorrowing.
There was a swine that gave the crowd away
■“■l™ got us three months each. Long time we lay
-c or him before we got him : for great need
Had we for caution ; but we made him pay.
At last, one night we caught him all alone—
-1 ifteen of us. I dropped him with a stone
Behind the ear ; I plugged him once again;
ihe push sailed in, for he was all our own.
How could we know he would cop out? We meant
Merely to stiffen him ; but ere we went
Homeward again they lumbered me, and then
1 got twelve years for wounding with intent.
Right glad to miss the gallows. Yet small cheer
.Remains for me who pay for joys too dear,
r blue-metal that I loved so well,
I hate now—set to break it prisoned here.
Yea, freedom now no good to me may bring;
The years that pass on black and tardy wing,
I know, will leave me—ah ! me, wretched cow !
A chewed-up, blinded piece of string.
R.C.H.
RONDEAU.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Weedin’ Chow come down our lane lars’ night
Out Bowrke-street way ; well, we was three parts
tight,
An’ ready for a lark. “ Good ni,” sea ’e ;
“ You wantee buy, me got ’em lettucee? ”
‘ Ga’rn, go to ’ell, ’’ ses I, ter make ’im bite.
“ Go ’ome,” I ses, “ain’t there no Japs ter fight?”
“You lalliken,” 'e ses,—l couldn’t sight
Sech crimson cheek, ’im sayin’ that ter me,
A Weedin' Chow !
An’ so I gets a gibber in me right,
An’ stoushes ’im, an’ Bill an’ Brummy White
They lands ’im on the ’ead. Yer never see
In all yer days sech crimson butchery ;
Yer might ’a called ’im then, by Gawd yer might,
A bleedin Chow!
R.C.H.

South Melbourne Bench the other day
told an unfortunate, who had attempted suicide,
that if he used a pistol against himself again and
did not die, he would be severely dealt with.
After this the victim will probably fall back upon
a cannon.

The latest news of a turn-out by Port
Darwin blacks goes to prove the correctness of the
doctrine that “you never can trust a black-
fellow.” The leaders in this Port Darwin out-
rage had worked for teamster John Mulligan for
15 years, and were considered safe and trust-
worthy ! Always make the nigger keep in front.
Criminal lunatics were lately removed
from Hobart Gaol, at considerable expenses, in
order to provide separate quarters for first
offenders. Nevertheless, first offenders and
“old hands” are indiscriminately mixed dur-
ing work, leisure and meal hours! The Hobart
first offender, committed for 24 hours, has a tough
time. He is measured, weighed, examined as to
marks, &0., dressed in prison “ uniform,” and
photo’d, and by the time the performance is all
over his sentence has about expired. Any unfor-
tunate drunk who can’t pay ss. fine has the satis-
faction of knowing that his photo, is duly in-
serted in the album along with those of thieves,
murderers, &c., and duly displayed to visitors. By-
the-by, if ever a big fire should occur in Hobart
Gaol, both warders and prisoners stand a good
show of blazing up to Heaven together, for cells
and gaol are all locked up at night, and the
almost innumerable keys are kept at a safe dis-
tance away, with apparently no provision made
for giving an alarm.

Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year. ,
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Maclkod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. P.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding tivo columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially, acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Governments on Interprovincial News-
papers, the annual subscription outside N.S. Wales is
now £l, with 3s. 3d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

SATURDAY, JULY 6, 1895.
The Bogan Bridge at Monkey and
Other Matters.
On the same day that the Reid Government
announced its intention of dissolving N.S. W.
Assembly and appealing to the electors for
another “mandate,” it also announced a
new loan of £3,000,000, and issued the loan
estimates for the financial year which com-
menced on Ist July. The haste with which
these have been produced naturally suggests
that they are intended to appeal to the soul
of the elector, and make him feel good
towards the Ministry at a time when material
support is urgently wanted. The nature of
the proposals confirms this impression. Out
of a total of £2,516,500, the sum of £977,400
is to be spent in repayment of old 5 per cent,
loans falling due a few months hence, and as
this conversion will save the country about
£14,000 per annum in interest, the transac-
tion is, so far, highly commendable. About
£450,000 is to be expended on railway works,
and as these will produce at least some kind
of an income, and will thus defray part of
the interest on the money borrowed, this
outlay is excusable according to the shiftless
methods of Australian finance. The rail-
ways, it is true, are all iu remote regions
where returns will probably be limited, and
a good deal of money goes in “trial sur-
veys,” which are not* reproductive works in
any lier J sense ; still, the railway expendi-
ture may, as a whole, be set down as a more or
lessreproductiveiuvestment. These two items
cover all that can, by any possibility, be
called honest finance. About £1,100,000
is to be fooled away on all manner of petty,
unproductive works throughout the length
and breadth of the country, and is appar-
ently intended for two purposes. One of
these is to create a bogus surplus and an
aspect of sham economy by charging to loans
£1,100,000 worth of current expenditure,
whereby the Reid Government will be able
to brag hereafter that it reduced the year’s
outgoings by a very large amount. The
other is to corruptly influence che, electors
by promising everybody all over the country
that his bridge, or his reclamation job, or
his artesian bore, will be forthcoming if the
present mean little Ministry gets back to
office.
• # #
The schedule of works to be effected
with this million pounds, or more, of bor-
rowed money, is a far-jreaching affair. It
covers more asylum accommodation for the
poor ; a reclamation job at Rushcutter s
Bay, Sydney, which work will prevent the
carcase of the dead and gas-inflated dog
floating up against the back-doors at Darling
Point ; dredging works, &c., at seven
different harbors ; additions to a number of
public offices in Sydney; a vast number of
small sewerage Avorks ; metallurgical works,
experimental farms, artesian bores ; a drain
at Mosman’s Bay (Sydney) ; and a grant
“for straightening the course of Styx and
Throsby Creeks.” Also, there are grants for
a new wharf at Manly ; for alterations to a
wharf at Woolloomooloo ; for a lighter ; for
alterations to some old dredges ; a number
of small sums, from £6OOO down to £6OO,
for country lock-ups, &c. ; a punt; about 30
bridges over various creeks in the back-
blocks, including one “ over the Bogan River
at Monkey ; ” repairs to two old bridges 5
four roads in different parts of the coTintry ;
a sum of £25,000 for “ thinning out
forests (!);” £lOO,OOO for “ clearing lands
and an especially shameless swindle where-
by two Sydney streets are to be wood-
blocked out of the loan-funds instead of —
as they ought to be—out of local rates.
Even the worst perverters of the vicious
borrowing system among past New South
Wales Treasurers never went quite so far in
the way of shunting their current obliga-
tions on to posterity, or “faked ’ the public
accounts quite so brazenly as this.
# * *
The Bulletin holds that foreign borrow-
ing is utterly thriftless, vicious and inex-
cusable under all circumstances, inasmuch as
the foreign mortgagee drains the country of
money in the shape of interest, and re-
presents the absentee curse in its worst
form. And it holds that local borrowing is
excusable only when the proceeds are in-
vested in public works of a directly repro-
ductive character, and which hold out
reasonable promise of paying, at no
very distant date, the entire interest
on their cost. The rule as to the
investment of loan money in reproductive
works has been so far recognised that all
Treasurers profess, in a general sort of
way, to follow it. That they do not live up
to their professions is evidenced by the fact
that barely four-fifths of the N. S.W. public
debt has been incurred for works of a directly
revenue-producing character, while rather
more than one-fifth has been expended on
non-productive works, which ought distinctly
to have been charged to current revenue. But
outof the£1,516,000 which represents the pro-
posed new loan expenditure of 1895-96, Pre-
mier Reid intends to spend over £1,000,000
(or more than two-thirds) on works which
yield no revenue whatever. That unspeakable
financier, whose whole political existence is a
hypocritical protest against the dishonesty of
his predecessors, economises on the revenue
by charging roads, bridges, punts, river im-
provements, harbor improvements, street
repairs, wharf repairs, bridge repairs, the
patching of superannuated dredges—almost
all the current Public Works expenditure,
in fact—to posterity. He passes on the
responsibility with a whole-souled unscrupu-
lousness of which not even the worst of
previous Treasurers was capable. Therefore,
his surplus, if he has one, is a political
swindle, and his deficit, if he has one, is
a political swindle likewise, inasmuch as
the surplus is arrived at by not paying
current liabilities out of current revenue,
and the deficit is represented at many hun-
dreds of thousands ,below its real amount by
the same process. Further, G. H. Reid’s
alleged reductions in the national expendi-
ture are a sham, t being effected, not by
economy or wise administration, but by the
clumsy expedient of leaving the 'bill to
posterity.
* * #
If the aforesaid £1,100,000 represented
some exceptional public works which required
to be done only once in the nation’s history,
then, even despite their unreproductive
character, there might be some semblance of
an excuse for this wholesale shunting of the
responsibility. But they stand on a wholly
different footing. The making of new roads
is a regular item of expenditure ; population
keeps increasing, and settlement expands
in hitherto roadless districts, and more new
roads must continue to be made every year
and all the year round for centuries to come.
England—a mere speck of ground by com-
parison—has been making roads for 1900
years, and is not finished even yet. Also,
the country must keep on building new
bridges constantly for 500 years or more ;
almost every new road, or new township, or
new area thrown open for selection involves
the erection of more bridges to furnish
means of access. Likewise the rivers that
are dredged this year will require to be
dredged again next year and every succeed-
year, or, if they don’t, other rivers will ;
and the demand for new gaols and lock-ups
and post-offices is perennial while population
keeps on growing larger ; and water con-
servation will be an annual charge on the
revenue for ages to come ; and the highway
which is wood-blocked to-day will want
the process repeated at regular intervals
so long as it remains a highway.
Also, the repaired dredge and the patched
bridge won’t last for ever, and the State
forests will require to be thinned regularly
so long as they are forests. Therefore, when
Premier Retd proposes to leave posterity to
pay its own bill for all these works, and to
pay his bill as well, he is guilty of a mean
evasion of his responsibilities and perpe-
trates a gross political swindle on the gener-
ation which is to come, in addition to the
many similar swindles of his predecessors.
# # #
The late Dibbs Government sinned in
the same fashion, and so also did the
Pakkes Government Vhich preceded it, and
many others. But Premier Reid professes
to have introduced an altogether new era of
economy and strict honesty, and he has
expatiated to utter weariness about the
frauds, iniquities, misrepresentations, and
financial jugglery of his predecessors ; there-
fore it is a truly lamentable thing to find
him following in their footsteps. It is still
more lamentable to find that he is much
worse in this respect than any of his dismal
forerunners, and his snuffle is an aggravation
of his offence, and his professions of radical
reform in financial matters are mere gratui-
tous hypocrisy. The record of Premier Reid,
so far as the Treasury is concerned, may be
summed up in a few sentences. He “ faked ’
his first financial statement by charging’
£216,000 of his expenditure to his prede-
cessors and dating the outlay back in order to
fix the responsibility on some one else.
He “faked” his second one by omitting
from the estimates over £200,000, which
must be provided, according to various acts
of Parliament, for charges on the public
debt ; and by taking credit in every case for
12 months’ revenue while only providing, in
many cases, for 11 months’ expenditure—
the last month’s expenditure being peace-
fully shunted over into next year. He
further manipulated the public balance-sheet
by chaiging to loan account over £300,000
which his predecessors had already paid out
of revenue, and taking that huge sum
out of loan funds, and including it in
current receipts. He produced a bogus and
artificial appearance of solvency by charging
expenditure for roads, bridges, repairs, &c.,
to loan account on a more reckless and
shameless system than that of all the shame-
less politicians who went before him. And
he has made hardly one genuine reduction
of expenditure, save by cutting miserable
sums off the miserably small votes for the
relief of destitute children, aged and sick
aboriginals, for hospital and asylum grants,
for maintenance of the sick poor in infir-
maries, for benevolent asylums, for food to
prisoners in lock-ups, and the like. All
these have been retrenched with a lavish
hand, and they are almost the only items
which have been retrenched. Also the small
allowance for the defence of penniless
prisoners charged with capital offences has
apparently been abolished. These things
practically make up the whole of the Reid
Ministry's economics up to date.
» # *
The visible cause of all this is the incapa-
city and political dishonesty of a feeble
Premier and a feeble scratch Ministry. But
part of the responsibility rests upon the
community, which is miserably ignorant and
stupidly indifferent in matters of political
economy, and part rests with the system of
Government by Contradiction. The first
duty of a Ministry is to keep itself in office.
To do this it must placate the country as a
whole by decreasing, or, at least, by not
increasing, the taxes ; and it must placate
the constituencies individually by giving
them more roads, bridges, gaols, post-offices,
court-houses, dredges, lunatic - asylums,
breakwaters, and wood-blocked highways
than did its predecessors. Also it has to
placate politicians and influential supporters
with billets in the over-grown Public Service,
and it must show a surplus, or explain that
the other fellow is alone responsible for the
absence of one. Therefore, the ordinary
politician cares not a straw whether his
accounts are honest or not—all he wants is
that they should look honest. Also he takes
no account whether the Treasury is solvent
or not, so long as it appears solvent. If
New South Wales ever raises a thoroughly
honest Treasurer, then it will hear some
new and very surprising truths. For
he will tell it that the demand for reduced
taxation is an absurdity, and that it is not
the business of a Treasurer to reduce taxes
—that it is necessary to save at least
£1,000,000 per annum off the ordinary ex-
penditure—and that, when that is accom-
plished, it is necessary to raise £500,000
more revenue each year to cover the outlay
on essential public works which, being not
directly productive of revenue, cannot, on
any honest principle, be executed with bor-
rowed money. Further, he will insist on
the establishment of a sinking-fund for the
extinction of the public debt, on the ground
that the Government is eating up the national
assets by turning the public lands into
revenue, and that to use up the national
assets to meet present expenditure is bad,
but to use them up and yet leave our present
expenditure as a debt upon posterity is abso-
lute ruin. And he will probably add that
the country has drivelled far too much
already about its magnificent resources,
which are nothing so extraordinary after all;
and about its splendid future, which has
been growing more and more remote for
seventy years and more ; that it has been
living all along in a fool’s paradise, regard-
less of the morrow ; and that it will require
twenty years of hard work and depression to
put things moderately straight. Wherei pun
the country will probably decide that that
honest financier is a lunatic, or a Russian
spy, or an anarchist, and cast him out ac-
cordingly. Considering all things, The
Bulletin is not at all sure that G. H. Reid
is not as good a leader as the people who
made him what he is deserve. And that is
about the hardest thing which has been said
about them this century.
The People and Business.
Why should not the people do their own busi-
ness? Why should great industries —such as
can be made to pay—always be reserved as
happy hunting-grounds for the enterprising
capitalist? Why should the State be expected
to put up with Fat Man’s leavings and be con-
tent to set up institutions and manage them
only when there is no profit to be expected
from them ? We are told that the State can’t
do business. Innumerable individuals, each
working alone, can do business and make a suc-
cess thereof. They band themselves together
into companies, and often do better still. Then
the companies coalesce into rings and trusts,
and the bigger they are the stronger they grow
and the more they make. Yet their friends
want us to believe that the strongest and
broadest company possible, a real Democratic
State, must fail in business. A truly Demo*
cratic State— i. e., the People, is the only
honest ring, the only trustworthy trust, the
only clean pool. It is the only big institution
which does not exist for the purpose of making
money out of the sweat of the workers.
The meaning of an ordinary big commer-
cial undertaking now is that a certain
number of more or less smarj; men try to make
a good thing out of the people’s works and
wants. Why shouldn’t the people make the
good thing for themselves? Why should the
giant majority for ever be led and bled by
pigmy minorities ?
* * •
Fat Man’s friends say that business, to be
successful, must be managed by Heaven-born
financiers. Where are they ? (Some who were
called such are in Darlinghurst or Pentridge,
and are much lonelier there than they ought to
be. The story of Australian Heaven-born
finance is written in panic, suspension and re-
construction. We are paying in calls and
losses, in depression and despair, for the fling
which that inspired person, the financial
genius, had at our expense a few years ago.
Could the State do worse than the Fat Man ?
Certainly not. Could the State do better ?
Why not try ? The modern bank is supposed
to be the choicest flower of private financial
genius. All the talent, all the knowledge, all
the thickness of skin and hardness of heart of
the nineteenth - century business man go to
build up these safe strongholds for capital,
these castles of commerce, flow their managers
anil directors have laughed at the crude
bungling efforts of young democracies to man-
age their own affairs ! There was nothing
democratic in a bank, anyway : the Fat Man
thanked God for that. We are not quite sure
how many banks there are in Australia which
did not reconstruct the year before last, but it
does not matter much. There are not enough
of them to waste time over. There would have
been fewer still had not that clumsy, pitiful,
despised thing, the State, kindly bridged the
gulf with Banking Acts. Capital had to
come to Democracy to be saved.
« * *
So it was, too, in Maoriland. There the
province’s biggest bank went down on its
knees to Dick Seddon and the Labor-
party last winter and wept and begged
and prayed for help—or else the shutters
would go up next Monday morning. And
burly, big-fisted Dick gave the help. But the
Maorilanders don’t mean to let their bank play
fast and loose with the country again. The
State appoints the bank’s chairman and audi-
tor now, and keeps a veto up the Trea-
surer’s sleeve. Thus the Maorilanders have let
their State go into banking. Well, they cannot
make more of a mess of it than their private
capitalists made. Moreover, the State is used
to doing business over there. Twenty-five
years ago it started a life-insurance office. In
spite of all that private enterprise can do for
outside insurance companies in touting, can-
vassing, puffing and blowing, the State beats
them at their own game. Maorilanders
for their numbers do more life - insur-
ing than any other nation in the world,
and their National office insures as many
of them as all their private offices put
together. It charges the lowest rates ; it gives
bigger bonuses than most; it beats every office
of its own age out of sight in economy of
working expenses. The office began in a small
way and much distrusted. Its policies now
come to nine millions sterling, and last year
3000 men and 253 women went on to its books.
All sorts and conditions in Maoriland pin their
faith to it. Its policy-holders range from a
chaff-cutter to a Chief Justice, and from
sausage-makers down to bank-managers. More-
over, in Maoriland, thanks to the State Trust
Office, you can leave your property in trust or
die intestate, with a certainty that your children
won’t be robbed by friendly trustees, or be
fleeced by respectable lawyers. • The province
will act as your trustee or administra-
trator, and unless you are pig-headed you will
choose no other. The Public Trust Office of
Maoriland has now the care of a million and a-
half of property. It manages cheaply and
does not steal, keeps ruin from pouncing on the
widow and the orphan, and won’t let even
lunatics be robbed. The office is 23 years old.
Property-owners looked shyly at it at first, but
they know better now. Why in such matters
as these must Australia always let the Maori-
landers get there first ? If the people can do
their own business on one side of the Tasman
Sea, why not on the other ? There is more to
do on our side, let us remember ? Our business
men have had a long trial. Their incompetency
is the talk of the world. Let the long-suffer-
ing people take a turn at the work. It is the
People’s own.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
Our Oath!
“A' ’broad-minded Persian lately tried to knock
a little common-sense into the administration of
ilie oath in'a Melb. police-court. It goes with-
out saying that the attempt failed. The Persian
witness was about to be sworn in the usual way
when the Bench objected, on the ground that
lie hadn’t been converted to the Christian faith.
As an enlightened Mahomeddan, “ but not the
same as a Hindoo,” the man saw no difference
between the Bible and the Koran for swearing
purposes. The Bench, with true Christian
fatuity, .persisted that a Persian conscience
could not accept the official Australian oath on
its native merits. The witness, who had already
been there on several occasions, and was “quite
used to it,” suggested a little religious tolera-
tion on the part of the pious beaks. “I have
been here living 25 years,” said the Oriental,
“and it’s all the same at my church, this
book —we tell truth before one great God.
Any book do.” But there remained the
solemn fact that Christian missionaries are
sent forth to expound Terewth to men who
associate God with more than one Book, conse-
quently the Bench called for the Koran and
ordered the rational foreigner to confine his
conscience to the oath of his ancestors. And
when a greasy Koran had been substituted for
a greasy Bible the clerk of courts remembered
ids own unfitness to administer an oath upon
an un-English volume, so the case was
adjourned for a few days, in order that a
qualified interpreter might swear the Persian
worshipper of one great cosmopolitan God
according to the only conscience which Anglo-
Australian law gives him credit for. Mean-
while, a mild agitation for church union is
understood to be occurring in these parts. The
bishops of rival Christian denominations discuss
the hopelessness of this agitation. They stand
on opposite shores, so to speak, and smugly con-
template the awful width of the gulf which
separates them. Also, they wrangle as to the
respective merits of their denominations in the
matter of carrying the Word into unbelieving
hearts. Likewise, they would rather die, or
debar themselves of several luxuries, than con-
sent to be sworn on the opposition creed’s book
of common prayer. But they agree in recog-
nising the precious divinity of the Jewish
Bible, and the majesty of the law which holds
that same Bible sacred to Jews and Christians
oidy, and, as for any simple Mahommedan who
believes in one great God above all other re-
ligious considerations—well, for a poor be-
nighted creature who brackets several works of
pious fiction together, the bishops feel a pro-
found, though decidedly unpleasant, pity.
Which is not surprising when we remember
that bishoprics are built on bigotries, and each
bigotry is built on its own particular book. To
say that any book written to the glory of God
will do is to blaspheme against the most in-
fluential recipients of holy orders.

Municipal Socialism.
Editor Bulletin, —The reason the democracy
does, not get control of the municipal bodies is
partly that it does not try, but mostly because
it cannot. The flying of the democratic, or, to
be more accurate, the labor flag, at Broken Hill,
where are the safest Parliamentary seats for
labor in New South Wales, at once settles a
municipal candidate’s chances. There R.
Sleath, now labor member for Wilcannia, five
times unsuccessfully contested municipal seats.
1% Bulletin would not have municipal one-
mah-one-vote (and I quite agree); but how
shall we equitably modify the system under
which capitalistic control is so nearly abso-
lute as it now is in municipal affairs? But,
anyway, no one but a perfectly rabid, factional
democrat would vote for certain typical
democratic candidates. Why is it that these
everywhere are so often unclean persons
(physically as well as morally) whom it
were a nightmare to conceive representing
one in any capacity: tactless people who be-
lieve that blunt utterance of their extreme per-
sonal likes arid dislikes and a brutal treading
upon corns shows sincerity and earnestness;
that stupid bombast is the highest form of in-
tellectual oratory ? The dull pork-butcher, the
befuddled publican, the cheap-jack auctioneer
—these as aldermen are bad enough, but they
shine by comparison with the typical would-be
labor alderman. Let me say that the men who
would best fill the aldermanic and Mayoral
offices seldom appear in municipal life.
Generally when they do the quasi-democratic
pack yelps at their heels because they
quietly work instead of striking dramatic
attitudes arid uttering windy speeches, or
making’ “ scenes ” in the council chamber—
until they retire in disgust. Or else they never
seek to sit in the council chamber, but rightly
understand without the need of experience how
hard and thankless is the work, and how false
the position of the honest alderman. They
know how very small is the municipal stage,
and Bow insignificant the figures which strut
upon it. They know these things, and wisely
prefer the eminence of private life to. the obscu-
rity of the municipal council chamber.—Yours,
Broken Hill.
The One-Eyed Law.
At Invercargill (M.L.), in 1887, a small farmer
named Meikle was convicted of sheep-stealing
on the evidence of one Lambert, and sent to
gaol for seven years. In these days an accused
person was not allowed to give evidence on his
own behalf, neither could he call his wife as a
witness for the defence, and Melkle’s case was
considerably discounted thereby. Also, the
matter was hustled through so rapidly that
there was insufficient time to collect evidence,
neither was there much money available, and
accused, being shut up in gaol, could do nothing
on his own account, and the police, as usual,
worked up their side of the case only. Meikle,
therefore, served his term in gaol, and came <• >i b
to find a new law in force whereby an ac'cu ed
person and his wife could both give evidence.
Also, he had now time to hunt up the informa-
tion which, was so badly wanted and so carefully
suppressed in 1887- As the result he hauled up
the informer, Lambert, for perjury, and proved
that that person was drunk in a neighboring
town on the day when he claimed to have seen
the crime committed. Also he proved a con-
clusive alibi for himself, and convinced the
court that his son, who was said to have been
an accomplice in the theft, was then confined to
bed with an illness from which he never re-
covered. And the court thereupon found
Lambert guilty, and sent him to hard labor for
four years. This is at least something, yet it
is a miserably unsatisfactory conclusion. Lam-
bert was a well-known resident, and it seems
hardly possible that he could be howlingly
drunk in a small country town without the
police being aware of it, and if they were aware
of it, then it would seem that the information
was suppressed on the good old theory that it
is the* business of the police to get convictions
—to convict somebody or anybody under
any kind of circumstances. The fact that
young Meikle was physically incapable of
committing the crime charged against him
also looks like a matter which the police
must or should have been more or less ac-
quainted with. Likewise, both these facts must
have been known to other people who were
apparently too tired to come forward. The
ridiculous ease with which Meikle was con-
victed on the thinnest kind of a case, merely
for want of some experienced person to hunt up
the evidence, which was lying round in all
directions, suggests that many other people
may be doing terms in gaol from exactly the
same cause. It likewise suggests that the
administration of justice will continue to be an
iniquitous farce until a Crown Defence Depart-
ment is created to counterbalance the one-sided
sinfulness of the Crown Prosecutor. The local
“Crown” might inquire into the Meikle case
seriously, and draw some valuable lessons there-
from. And when it is doing so, it might
also seriously consider its own position in the
matter. The Crown went to great expense to
convict Meikle on the evidence of the shady
Lambert, but when it became a matter of con-
victing Lambert on the evidence of Meikle
the latter had to stand all the trouble and ex-
pense himself, the Crown apparently being too
exhausted to interfere. These aspects of the
Meikle-Lambert case deserve the considera-
tion of every respectable person who wants to
keep out of gaol. Every person who is satis-
fied with the law as it now stands is liable to
discover any day, by personal experience, how
absurdly easy it is to convict almost any person
of almost anything, under the present one-sided
system. Also, he fully deserves to make that
discovery personally.
A Local Government Problem.
It was proposed, under the deceased N.S.W.
Local Government Bill, to restrict local taxation
to Is. 3d. in the £ on rental value, or three-
farthings in the £ on the unimproved capital
value. A recent valuation made at Invercargill
(M. L.) throws some light on the miserable in-
adequacy of the aforesaid three-farthings. The
local rate there equals 2s. in the £ of rental, and
a valuation recently made shows that a tax of
betWeeu 4d. and sd. in the £ of unimproved
capital value will be required to produce the
same income. Therefore, it would seem that
when the N.S.W. Local Government Bill is
again introduced, the proposed three-farthings
should be at least 2d. to give the unimproved
value tax anything like a fair show. It is
remarkable that in Maoriland, where local
bodies may levy rates either on annual
rental or on unimproved capital values, the
great majority of towns and boroughs levy on
annual rental, the reason assigned being that
this method of taxation gets at the rich man
much more effectually than the other. The
Invercargill valuation shows that under the
unimproved value tax the business and swell
residential portion of the town would pay
much less than it now does, while in the
suburban localities where workmen mostly have
their dwellings, therates would be increased from
200 to 500 per cent. The typical swell residence
consists of a £2OOO house planted on £5OO worth
of ground—total, £2500; the workman’s cottage
represents, perhaps, £lOO worth of house on a
little £lOO allotment—total, £2OO. Therefore,
under the rental valuation, the rich man is
taxed on £2500, and the poor man on £2OO, so
of their joint liability the latter pays less than
one-thirteenth. But if they are taxed on un-
improved land-value only, the capitalist pays
on £5OO and the workman on £lOO, so the latter
pays one-sixth of the total, or more than twice
as much as at present. That is how things pan
out in Invercargill, anyhow, and when next the
New South Wales Local Government Bill copies
along the Labor-party should look up this point
carefully. It is just possible that it has been
unconsciously endeavoring to lump the local
taxation chiefly on its own class, and to let the
capitalist go almost scot free.

Australia’s Bankruptcy Sale.
The Argus strikes half of a great truth ;
There is only one market in ttie world that is now open
to them (the Victorian farmers), viz, the English market,
and if that were closed the results would he exceedingly'
disastrous to them. The irony of the position is that Vic-
torian Protectionists, to he consistent, have to advocate
the closing of the English market to Victorian produce.
Or, in other words, they have to work for the ruin of the
colony.
The first half of this is a solemn and awful
truth. Victoria pays the interest on its ex-
ternal debt by exporting butter, cheese, wheat,
and wool to Great Britain. If Britain gave
Protection to its agriculturists, then it would
still buy the wool, as usual, for wool-growing
requires a vast area of laud, which the little
North Sea isles do not possess. But it would
probably so enlarge its own agricultural yield
that it could do without the butter and cheese
and wheat, and, if it didn’t take them, Victoria
would very likely be unable to pay its interest,
and would have to default. N.S.W., with
its huge wool-producing area, might pull
through under these circumstances, but the
position of the smaller province would be pre-
carious. This doesn’t show' that Protection is
bad, but it does show most conclusively that
foreign borrowing is a lamentable mistake.
Australia can’t export the interest on its foreign
debt in sovereigns, for it doesn’t produce enough
of them for the purpose, so it has to pay
in goods. Under the protective system so
many countries are almost self-supporting, that
for most of these goods there is only one
niarket left, and if Britain abandons Freetrade
then there will be practically no market at all.
Therefore Victorian Protectionists don’t advo-
cate the closing of the English market against
their goods, they only advocate the closing of
their market -against English goods. And as
Protection, like Freetrade, is a purely selfish
regard for our own interests, they are perfectly
consistent. But for her foreign borrowings,
Australia could get on about as well with the
English markets closed as with them open,
but because of these borrowings she is reduced
to a state of helpless dependence on an
English buyer, and if he fails the bottom falls
out of things in general. Foreign borrowing
lias reduced this country to the state of the
insolvent drapery man who is always selling off
at an alarming sacrifice to meet a bill that is
falling due. There is a huge ticket constantly
TSxed outside the front door of this continent:
“ These Goods to be Sold Regardless of Cost.”
And if some day they aren’t sold in time there
will be serious trouble.
Sydney Telegraph publishes a letter from a
.“well-known farmer”—whom he is well-known
to is unstated—showing that Protection doesn’t
help agriculture, and comments thereon in an
enthusiastic leading article. The farmer’s name
and his letter is one more proof that
figures can be made to prove anything if they
are turned the right way for the purpose. Says
the well-known farmer, speaking of New South
Wales:—
The following- figures, taken from published returns for
quinquennial periods dating from 1860, clearly show
this:—ln 1800 the acreage under cultivation was 260,798 ;
in 1865, 378,254; in 1870, 426,976 ; in 1875, 451,139; in
1880, 706,493 ; in 1883, 868,093. I have not before me the
return for 1890, but that for 1895 was 1,325,814 acres.
Now, if we take the decade from 1875 to 1885, we find the
increase in that period was 416,954 acres, and from 1885,t0
1895 the increase is 457,721 —a very small increase indeed,
and this with a largely increased population.
This shows clearly, of course, that agriculture
progressed as fast in New South Wales under
Foreign-trade as under Protection. But if Mr.
Clout hadn’t omitted the 1890 statistics—which
he could have got from exactly the same source
as the other ones ; and if he hadn’t garbled
his figures by including some 300,000 acres
of grass lands in his estimates for the Free-
trade period up to 1885, and not includ-
ing them in his estimates for the Protective
period in 1895, then the result wouldn’t have
been of a kind that the Telegraph would
care to publish. When the permanent grass-
lands are included all round, then the solemn
facts are that the increase in three years of
Protection was equal to the best record put up
in ten years of Foreign-trade. It is just
possible that Mr. Clout —if there really is such
a person—was ignorant of this discrepancy in
his own figures, but the Telegraph could
hardly share that ignorance, and its joyous
endorsement of Clout’s assertions, which are
utterly destroyed by one glance at Coghlan’s
1894 “Statistical Survey,” looks perilously like
hard lying. When the D.T. gets on fiscal
statistics it is about the most conscienceless
publication that ever existed.
♦
The estimated cost of administration in New
South Wales for the year June ’95 to June ’96
— i.e., the cost of the great aggregate Civil
Service dude—is .£4,671,348, or just <£20,000
below the estimates, which the present Premier
denounced as “wildly extravagant,” for 1894.
And yet this is the branch in which, above all
others, savings should be effected. The
.£700,000 or so which it is proposed to “save ”
on the total expenditure is all made up either
by charging a vast amount of the ordinary road
and bridge expenditure and casual repairs to
loans, or else by postponing a large part
of the unavoidable charges of the public
debt to next year, and these are not
savings. The administrative services are
now run in Victoria for a little over
two millions, and even allowing for the larger
area and slightly larger population of N.S.
Wales, the latter province should get through
with ease on three millions. But thisis just where
the great retrenchment idiocy comes in. No
Government ever retrenches seriously except in
times of utter insolvency. Then it cuts down
unprofitable expenditure to avoid making itself
unpopular by new taxation, but, when things
improve the bill at once starts to expand
again. The fact which New South Wales
wants to look at with a hard, cold,, brutal
eye is that by the appointment of a really
econo.nical Cabinet it could easily save a million
and three-quarters annually off the cost of
departmental administration, and with that
sum to spend it could have a perpetual blow-out
of new roads and bridges, and could revel in
new railways and water-conservation works,
without loading itself down with taxation to
pay interest on fresh loans. In fact, N.S. Wales
could have a gorge of public works without a
cent of extra cost—if it only had sense enough
to seize the opportunity. The kind of men who
will make this, saving can always be found in
times of dreadful scarcity— Atkinson, Bal-
lance, and Turner are evidences to that effect
—and they could also be found at other times if
the community would look for them. But the
community never does look for them at these
other times.
Down South the legislators are concerning
themselves with the alleged entertainments
given by music-hall performers on Sunday
nights. The churches’ feel that they alone have
the right to take up a collection after a pitiful
performance, and they naturally resent the
competition of the music-halls. The music-
halls do their best to encourage the Sunday-
evening sleep to which the population has
accustomed itself. The windy baritone sings
the pathetic ballad about “The Cast in Mother’s
Eye,” and you could hear a brick drop while he
sings. The favorite comique comes forward
with an air of being in mourning for the death
of humor, and he chants “ There’s a Monkey to
Spare at the Zoo,” and the orchestra, con-
sisting of two fiddles and the drums, gives
him an encore, which he takes with unspeak-
able courage. Only this, and a little more like
it, and the collection—yet the churches, and
the Honorable Balfour, object. The churches
likewise object to the opening of libraries and
art-galleries on Sunday : the rich man may look
at hia books and pictures on Sunday, but the
people may not look at theirs. The churches
object to Sunday trains: the rich man may
order out his conveyance—the people should
not be allowed to ride in theirs. All this is
excellent bluff, and the Fat Man smiles as he sees
the elfect. The population that is controlled
by Fat Man’s friends will not touch Fat Man’s
vested rights.
Dear Bulletin, —The “meejum of exchange”
is perhaps the most unfittest thing for a public
debate. In most social and political questions
the appeal to individual experience is possible,
and facts of common knowledge can be freely
used without creating a fog. But statistics
are not matters of common knowledge, and
statistics are absolute!}' essential to the most
elementary wrangle about the “meejum of
exchange.” Where a matter of feeling or sym-
pathy is concerned a mixed audience might be
expected to come to a reasonable conclusion
instinctively. But with statistics, one set of
figures is played off against another, and the
audience is asked to choose on the spur of the
moment between the two—when, as a matter
of fact, they probably are both cross-eyed.
Cross-eyed statistics have no perspective. They
look on man as if at some particular moment
he were isolated from every modifying influence
but one. It is the privilege of the faddist to
cut his statistics to fit his arguments —and the
wonder is that the public finds any merit
in the performance. A proper discussion of
the “ meejum of exchange ” would not go
through the post for a penny, as Paula
Tanqueray thought her life story would.
Such a discussion would include the philosophy
of war and industrialism and trade. The wars
of the present century that have affected certain
countries so vitally—the Napoleonic wars, the
Crimean war, the civil war in America, the
Indian “ mutiny,” the Franco-Prussian war—
all these have accentuated or modified the evils
or advantages which the “meejum of exchange”
is fondly represented to have been entirely re-
sponsible for. Thenthequestion of harvests must
be considered, and the relative importance of a
bad European season early in the century and
a bad European season now. The alternation
of boom and burst, in spite of all sorts of new
factors, is perhaps more important to the dis-
cussion than even the marvellous gold dis-
coveries in the fifties, and the marvellous silver
discoveries of latter days. The birth of inven-
tion, the progress of mechanical skill, the effect
of improved banking systems and practice, the
elasticity and breaking-point of credit, the
opening-up of new countries, the electric tele-
graph, trades unionism and public sentiment,
fiscal systems, political systems, social condi-
tions, &c., are likewise concerned. How can
such an array of problems be dishcd-up in a
speech lasting a few minutes ? The thing is
absurd, and Frewen and Max Hirsch are so
much wasted energy.—F.
The simple and effective Torrens Title almost
abolished the great conveyancing swindle in
Australia, and nearly wiped out the large
revenue which the legal profession used to
extract from the public for muddling learnedly
among confused deeds, grants, transfers and
other barren wastes of parchment concerning
the ownership of land. Another great saving
of time and money, and another great assist-
ance towards the extinction of a non-producing
class, might be effected if the State undertook
to prepare indefeasible wills as well as inde-
feasible land-titles. It would be a simple
matter to establish a public' office, where
every man could have his will prepared
cheaply by the best legal talent in the
country, overhauled, as a further guarantee
against errors, by two or three first-class
lawyers, and his heirs insured by payment of a
small sum against any possible loss of their
inheritance through flaws in the document.
The same department could take charge of the
will, obtain probate at a low rate when the
testator died, and hand over everything in
correct form to the executors. Further, it
might be enacted that the wills so prepared
could not, under any circumstances, be called
in question on mere technical grounds. Such a
department, as an adjunct to a Public Trust
office to take charge of the property and act as
trustee and executor, would reduce many useless
solicitors, barristers, &c., to contribute to the
public wealth by agriculture or some otherhonest
pursuit. Even saying nothing about the fact
that the entire property often goes in litigation,
no country is rich enough to support a tribe of
learned counsel, who do nothing in return for
their board and lodging and wig save move
occasionally for probate in the will of Jones,
deceased. That six or eight large, muscular
persons of mature age should be moving for
probate in as many miserable little £BO or £lOO
estates, while outside the court a feeble little
man is'trying to shovel up two tons of sand in
30 minutes, is a pitiable instance of wasted
energy. A clerk at £5 a week from the Govern-
ment department could move successfully for
all the probates in Melbourne or Sydney, and
do office work the other three days in the week,
and still be home by five every evening.

Dear Bulletin, —Re a press paragraph
stating that a woman in Milparinka (N.S. W.) has
got two months’ gaol for refusing to work as a
domestic servant after signing a contract. A
better example of the crass ignorance of country
magistrates could not be afforded. The conviction
was under the 3rd section of the “ Masters and
Servants Act cf 1857 ” (20 Vic. No. 2S). But their
“ Worships ” utterly ignored sec. 14 of same Act,
which provides: “Nothing in this Act shall
authorise the imprisonment of any female.” Short
and to the point. Counsel’s opinion unnecessary.
The woman ought to be released forthwith, and
the J.’sP. removed from the commission. When
this sadly-ill-used creature is released I trust she
will take action against her ignorant adjudicators
for false imprisonment. She cannot fail to recover
damages. I hope she will see this.' She - could
not get better advice, even if she paid Salomons,
Q.C., 100 guineas.—Q. 24th June, ’95.

Athenaeum Rev. Haskett Smith.
The Dacres made a new departure on
Saturday, when a large audience gathered at Her
Majesty’s to see Grundy’s “Bunch of Violets,”
and expressed its cordial and even enthu-
siastic approval of that honored vegetable all
through four acts. At the end of the third act
especially, when the full of the herb
had just dawned on the house, nothing less than
a double recall could express its feelings.
Grundy’s play is a fine production in every respect,
and is also a bitter satire on nearly everybody.
The gallery might have felt inclined to resent the
picture of the Federated Sons of Toil, who are
the hardest-looking set of Domain loafers on
record, if it were not that the capitalistic hero is
such a finished and unspeakable hypocrite that
things are about even. Grundy’s hero is a com-
pound of Jabez Balfour, Merdle, Law, the City of
Glasgow Bank directors, the South Sea Bubble,
the Australian Banking Co., Mirams’ umbrella,
the Mercantile Bank, and the men who
salted Taranganba. He was a Sunday-
school teacher in early life, and seduced
the nicest girl in his class. Then he rises to a,
baronetcy and to a giddy eminence on the summit
of a cronk bank and a huge insolvent insurance
00. He nearly gets into .Parliament on the Non-
conformist snuffle ticket; nearly sells a gravel-pit
as a diamond-mine to a Yorkshire brewer for
i 6100,000 ; nearly hoodwinks his saint-like wife—
nearly does everything, in fact. And then the
whole show goes down like a house of cards. The
Sunday-school girl, whom, it seems, he had rashly
married to prevent exposure, turns up and
shatters his domestic happiness by cackling, in a
brassy fashion, about “the past.” The diamond-
mine doesn’t sell; the election is lost; the bank
bursts ; the bottom falls out of the insurance co.;
the abject confidential clerk cleans out the fallen
saint; the trustees of his embezzled marriage
settlement send for the police; and he suicides
among the ruins like Sardanapalus when he burnt
himself on top of the grand piano in Nineveh
with all Assyria falling on his head.
* # •
According to all the moth-eaten conven-
tions, virtue ought to come uppermost in the end,
but Grundy sticks closely to the natural sequence
of things. The confidential clerk who rises on
the saint’s ruins is a bony, crawling being, much
wickeder and much cleverer than his master, and
he is left in a state of skinny exaltation gathering
up the property. The pious wife turns out
to be no wife, and the pious daughter is
illegitimate ; and they are both left penniless.
As for the brassy, cackling person who used to be
the good little Sunday-school girl, she is the
wicked wife of a millionaire and is rolling in
riches ; and the Federated Sons of Toil, who are a
bad, blotched, beery lot, are happy and drunk
when last heard from. Also, the banker doesn’t
die repentant, nor does he weep in his last
moments; he perishes dry like a bad old Roman
with his back up to the last. Therefore, The
Bulletin regards the “Bunch of Violets” as a
great play, and every depositor, who is living in
the hope that poetic justice will yet overtake the
manager and board of directors, should see it and
realise that poetic justice is a dreadfully bad shot,
and can’t hit an Alp with a brick at ten paces.
The conspicuously weak point in the drama is
that the banker is only a plausible ass after all;
with a little more brains and a little artistic lying
he might have flourished to the end of his days,
• * *
Mr. Dacre’s rendering of the banker is a
very powerful piece of work. His cold and saintly
wife, however—though Miss Amy Roselle plays
the part with great ability doesn’t appeal
strongly to the audience. The glacial saint is
always a depressing character, and though she
thaws on two memorable occasions, the general
effect is that of a very good woman out in a hard
winter. The silent, murky, soft-footed confiden-
tial clerk is done by Scot Inglis, who misses not
one single point in that person’s cat-like evolution.
Frank Harcourt, as the principal Federated Son
of Toil, and J. W. Sweeney, are two excellent
comedians. Miss Knights, however, is shrill and
indefinite, and the banker’s daughter—who, for-
tunately, doesn’t matter much—is a rather wooden
little girl and suggests a strictly moral and upright
doll. She has very little to do, however, and
might almost be represented by a telegram stating
that she is detained owing to a bad cold.
** * *
“A Gaiety Girl” is doing its third and
last week at Sydney Lyceum, and is still going
remarkably strong. All the johnnydom of the
city rolls up nightly to stare through an infatuated
eyeglass at Grace Palotta’s fair countenance, and
to worship the other houris, and, as every separate
female in the co, is an houri, more or less, this
great capacious reverence is a dreadful thing to
witness. The verdict up to date is that the pre-
sent 00. is the finest and most comprehensive
beauty-show which has yet visited Australia, and
every dude in Sydney would see his high collar
burnt at the stake before he went back on this
conviction. Some new and bewildering varia-
tions have been appended to the original high kick,
which was sufficiently dazzling in its former state,
and sundry new graces have been added every
now and then to the skirt dance. “ In Town,”
which, according to Melbourne’s general decision,
is even better than the “ Gaiety Girl,” is to be
staged on Saturday (July 6).
* * * «
Sydney Tivoli is still in splendid health,
and is crowded nightly almost to the doors. Very
much the same may be said of the York-Jones
combination at the Empire, and between the two
Sydney has got the variety fever very badly
indeed. The Lyceum may also be classed as
variety, though of a lofty and etherealised kind,
which bears the same relation to the ordinary
species that Beethoven does to a hand organ ; so
with the Royal and Cri. both closed, the Dacres
at Her Majesty’,s are the sole representatives of
the drama now in the city.
* * * *
Sydney Grundy’s play, “The New
Woman,” is another addition to theß. and B. list
of marriage-philosophy works. For this reason it
is unlikely to meet with a brisk demand at Melb.
Princess’s. The public, in the bulk, care little for
stories of domestic dissatisfaction founded on any-
thing less solid than ground for a divorce. They
are weary of the woman who leaves her husband
without serious intention of billeting herself upon
somebody else, or who, even when she has the
other fellow in view, hears a voice commanding
her to change her mind. The one live “ marriage
question” play of this fin-de-si&cle is “Mrs.
Tanqueray,” which deals solely with the woes
of a well - matched couple under peculiar cir-
cumstances. Tliere is cause and effect in
“Mrs Tanqueray,” and B. and B. might as
well revive that interesting production for a week
or two. There is more or less cause, but no effect
whatever, in the recent conflicts between soul and
conventionality, theory and fact, inclination and
“duty,” or whatever the trouble may be that
incites stage men and women to talk so much
about love and marriage without making the
world wiser.
* # •
Grundy’s story opens cheerfully and almost
promises to be farcical. Clever artificial types of
new females roam in and out of Cecil Ward’s
bachelor chambers remarking that Woman has
“arrived” and man’s authority is a shattered
phantom of the past. They deny the right of
any bridegroom to reek with infamy unless
his bride reeks with infamy likewise, and
they smoke cigarettes till they have to rush outside
and be sick of society’s shams. But these amusing
reformers fade out of the entertainment after
Cecil gets married, and a tragic new literary
woman tempts the young husband to love her
with his brains in preference to cherishing a base
sensual affection for his wife. The poor little
wife, having overheard some references to her
lack of divine afflatus, runs away to a rural spot
where apples grow on gum-trees and all is peace.
The literary woman’s husband runs after her and
proposes a double-barrelled bigamy, but the
respectable little lady bids him go back
to home and duty, which he does. Anyway
the rejected sinner returns to town, whereupon
Cecil comes slinking round the apple tree with
penitence writ large on his face, and the object of
his sensual love takes him back into favor, on the
pleasant supposition that his soul’s affinity is
about to start cordial relations with the fleshly
partner whom her brain abhors. A comely,
practical, and altogether charming old aunt acts
as kind fairy to the young people, also her brother,
the colonel, is a popular interloper who whispers
“ Courage!” in his nephew’s ear, preparatory
to beating a rapid retreat when he sees a storm
coming.
* # *
Miss Talbot and manager Boucicault are
very happy in the elderly characters aforesaid.
Next to them Miss Noble, as an ordinary-minded
wife, is the most apparent success, although her
performance is merely a suggestion of what a
stronger actress would do with the same brilliant
opportunities. Miss Olliffe and Cecil Ward have
no characters to speak of. Their intellectual
attachment is indefinite, and when last seen to-
gether they are apparently resigning themselves to
some fate which they haven’t begun to think
about. New actor Elwood represents the mundane
husband of the soulful lady in precisely the same
dirgey tones and gloomy manner which he used
for the astronomer in “ The Masqueraders,” and
the cheerfulness of several other artists is too inap-
propriate to be of much service. The strongest
point about “The New Woman ” is its up-to-date
title. ,

stancial evidence for murdering the old man.

** * *
Ada Crossley (writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent) made her first appearance
before a London audience under somewhat de-
pressing circumstances. Her concert was held in
the Queen’s Hall, a huge gazabo of a place, which
must be filled to look anything ; it wasn’t full on
this occasion, though the audience would in any
other hall have seemed large. Miss Crossley
came through the ordeal very well indeed, and
made quite a favorable impression, both by her
manner and her vocal skill. I did not care much
about the selection of her songs. She sang that
dismal thing, Beethoven’s “In questa Tomba,”
very well indeed, followed by “ Air de Sommeil,”
from Arabroise Thomas’s “Psyche.” Then she
sang Schumann’s “ Waldesgespracht,” which is
rather dull, but which was uncommonly well
rendered. In the second part, she gave “Out on
the Rocks” (for what contralto can resist it?),
which was vociferously encored ; and, in response
to loud applause, sang a rather mawkish ballad,
which met with such favor that the audience
wanted to hear it again, but the fair Ada said
No. However, Ada Crossley is not yet a Clara
Butt; and her voice, though fresher, has not quite
the fulness of Miss Helen Rowe’s. Undoubtedly,
the Gippsland girl has a good future before her on
the concert-platform, as her enunciation is quite
perfect, every word being distinctly heard at the
extreme end of the large hall. This, in itself, is a
rare merit. She was very tastefully costumed,
and looked exceedingly pretty —she has much
composure, and her method is of the best. The
one objectionable feature of the occasion was the
presentation of a huge basket of flowers —which
looked too much like bogus business to please
those who really wish the Australian contralto
well. It seems to be felt on all hands that Miss
Crossley made a terrible error in appearing under
Marchesi’s auspices instead of under Santley’s.

A Bulletin correspondent:—“John Murray-Williams,
an unassuming Tas. musician, possessed of more than
ordinary talents, died (aged 34) at Hobart Temperance
Hall on the evening of 18th ult. whilst performing at a
benefit entertainment in aid of, a distressed citizen. In
the middle of the playing he staggered from the piano,
fell on the stage in full sight of the audience, and died in
a few moments. Deceased was a genial, kindly spirit,
ever ready to lend himself to any charitable purpose. He
might have attained a better position in Hobart, hut for
his extreme modesty. It would have killed poor Wil-
liams long ago had he khown that be would have to die
in public.”
When Marshall Hall was arranging the first of his very
successful series of Saturday afternoon classical concerts,
he was advised that he would never succeed unless he
secured vice-regal patronage. This he persistently re-
fused to do, and although, as a consequence, Guv’raent
House has just as persistently sheered off the perform-
ances, they have been an unprecedented triumph. In the
same spirit, Mr. Hall never played the National Anthem
at the finish. At first the dazed audience sat still, waiting
for the old signal to go home to tea, and didn't stir until
the orchestra started to go. But it is broken in now, and
the discordant thing is not missed. Apparently Marshall
Hall is giving Melbournites something more than a musi-
cal education.
The Vic. Charity Commission invites Government to
impose an Amusement Tax for the benefit of Victorian

dation.
Clifford Hal 16, coming to Australia from India, bad the
bad luck to travel with a case of smallpox, wherefore
quarantine, vaccination and woe. The quarantine threw
all his-concert plans out of gear, and the vaccination
seriously affected Miss Ribbentrop’s health. It is under-
stood that the imposing young lady preferred to be
“took” on one of the limbs which are not her arms,
although a patient in such cases must dispense with
walking exercise for a time if the inflamed extremity is
to have a chance to heal. Arm-poisoning is very much
the less of two evils, but it doesn’t adapt itself to the
requirements of evening dress.
Clifford Hall 6 had several months of tiger-shooting in
India, under the auspices of Miss Ribbentrop’s colossal
papa. Mr. Rhbentrop, being permanent head of the
Forest D epartment of India, can lay his guests on to
beasts of prey as easily as he can offer them any other
sort of refreshment. He just sends an official note into
the jungle, desiring particulars as to the whereabouts of
the nearest tiger or panther family, and then arranges a
pi -nic in the vicinity. After luncheon a mob of pious
Hin ’oos tempt the wild beasts to chase them, whilst the
HalliS of the occasion lets fly. If the new chum sports-
man, perchance, misses his tiger he probably pots a
Hindoo, and so there’s a funeral anyway.
Journalistic johnnies who dodge round after theatrical
“ tips” are supplied, in most cases, with the same sort of
reliable information that sporting johnnies get from hone-
trainers. When actors, or actresses, have a particular
market purpose to accomplish they breathe a Action into
the ears of some irresponsible news-gatherer, by Whom It
is passed into print as a fact “ straight from the stable."
Nine out of ten theatrical rumors are crafty suggestions,
or malicious lies, scattered broadcast for the hungry spar-
rows of the press to pick up and swallow. The sparrow
who comes tapping at a pro.’s window with his little beak
is generally liand-ft d by a morsel of misstatement, and the
bird who comes right in seeking an interview gels about
as much truth as might be expected, soaked in vanity
sauce. It is all swallowed. An interviewer gravely
assures you that his information about Sock and Buskin
is correct, because they told him everything themselves.
A famous professional beauty actress still shows signs
of the punishment received from a wealthy admirer, now
dead. He used to dissemble his love by giving the lady
beans. Money is power. Possessed of sufficient coin a
man may mingle with the crowd outside a photographer’s
shop, enjoy the expressions of admiration for the latest
portraits of the most fashionable Venus, and then stroll
home to black Venus’s eye with impunity.

“Which reads like money.”
Baron Carington has been created an earl
and will henceforth be known (in England) as
Lord Wendover. In Australia he will be known
as Lord Mount Rennie—as the man with the soft
bead and the hard heart who, in defiance Of every
tradition of modern law and despite remonstrances
which no truly civilised man could have disre-
garded, sent to the scaffold four 18-year-old
boys condemned to death after a trial so
brutal that “to find a parallel for it one
must turn,” as Dailey said, “to the darkest
and bloodiest days of Irish history.” The
Bulletin congratulates Charles Robert Baron
Carington on having changed his name. For it is
a name which, after the unholy slaughter of that
January morning, can, even after eight years, be
smelt across twelve thousand miles of sea.

Nay,.parson dear, you sway the soul
And other matters psychic,
*lis Satan tempts you to control
The doctrine of the high kick.
How do you know “ the truth, the way,”
That seems to you Aspasian ?
The stocking’d stripes the girls display?
The charms Herodiasian ?
Say, have you seen it with your eyes—
Your own two eyes so bold ?
Or is it titillant surmise ?
Or merely what you’re told?
Nay, parson dear, don’t dribble so;
Get off your moral shelf;
Take good advice, good man, and go
And see the girls yourself.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to seientistr that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback-the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of tins imperfection; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.
The Wild-Cat Column .
Treasurer Reid closed the half-year on June 30
' with a shortage ot £366,000, or at the rate of £732,000 per
annum to the bad. This is the worst record for N.S.W.
since 1887, and it is about time that the daily papers
began barracking about the “ Keid deficit."
- 1 r • • • •
It has been definitely announced that the amount
of the next N.S.W. loan (variously reported at £2,000,000
and £3,000,000) is to be £2,516,500. This will make the
total debt of the province about £61,000,000.
• • • •
The Royal Bank of Queensland has got on so
far with its conversion scheme that £150,000 worth of 4
per cent, debentures are announced as having already
been sold in London. They are issued at £lOO, and are
redeemable ten years hence at £lO3, so that the interest
is roughly £4 6s. per cent, instead of the nominal four.
After allowing for expenses the difference between this
and the original 4J will hardly be worth mentioning, and
in the present uncertain state of things the bank which
fixes such a load on its shoulders for ten years is doing a
remarkably foolish thing.
