i jt.• * » •
The Insurance and Banking Record draws
attention to a number of weak points in the actuarial
report of the City Mutual Lite Assurance Society of
Sydney, which are calculated to make policy-holders in
that institution think hard. The City Mutual, it points
; out, reckons that the whole of the first year’s premium
On every policy is absorbed by commission and other ex-
penses incurred in obtaining the business. Therefore, if
the policy is not renewed after the first year the
office has taken that year’s risk for absolutely nil,
according to its own calculations. The amount of
interest which it is estimated that the funds will
yield in the future is set down at 4 per cent,
in the case of whole-life policies, while in the case
of endowments it is set down at 4J per cent. net.
If the 4J per cent, is not realised, then the office is likely
to be in an awkward situation. As the gross interest
realised has fallen from £7 6s. 9d. per cent, in ’92 to
£5 9s. 6d. per cent, in ’94, and the net interest, according
to the Record’s estimate, has dropped in the same period
from £6 15s. 6d. to £4 17s. sd. per cent, there seems just
a chance that it may get below the required £4 10s. per
cent, by-and-bye. Anyhow, the A.M.P. Society evidently
thinks so, and is gradually reducing its estimated rate of
interest to £3 10s. per cent. From this standpoint it
would look as if the City Mutual, especially in view of its
high expense rate, was cutting things a good deal too fine
in more ways than one, and taking some rather reckless
chances.
• • • •
Daring the last ten years about 18 Australasian
fire and marine insurance companies have vanished out
of the business world, and the number of offices in exist-
ence has been reduced one-third. Also, despite the large
increase in population, the total premium income of the
local companies has dropped in the same decade by over
£600,000, or a good deal more than a-third. A good many
Of the best among the vanished institutions have been
bought up by English companies. The business ot those
that died a natural death also seems to have drifted to
English companies, and the same insatiable J. Bull
appears to have also made some inroads on the business
of the survivors. Altogether the curse of foreign capital
sticks very closely to this miserable country. The A.N.A.,
as a patriotic Australian institution, might set a good,
though small, example just here by swearing all its mem-
bers to insure their lives and their belongings in local
institutions only, and to keep their accounts-those that
have any—in local banks. Even the smallest kind of
example is worth something.
r * • • •
The following statement, by Mr. Teece, of the
A.M.P. Society, of the disgraceful condition of N.S.W.
insurance law, should be pasted in the hat ot every legis-
lator until further orders;—“As our legislation stands
at the present time,” says Secretary Teece, “there is
nothing whatever to prevent the biggest vagabond that
walks the earth sticking up a brass plate on a door in
George or Pitt-street, collecting premiums from the
people, putting them in his pocket, and walking away
with them.” Now that we come to think of it, one
tolerably capacious and well known vagabond did do some-
thing very like that. But he didn’t even take the trouble
to walk away. He remained, and remains to this day.

Dunedin (M.L.) corporation has tried the
experiment of selling 5 per cent, municipal debentures
locally, and has realised a higher price than that at which

linies ” should be gradually losing their veneration for
the British money-market as a good place to borrow in.

• * * *
Building society business is a tolerably solemn
thing nowadays, so far as the smaller institutions are con-
cerned. The Argus Permanent B. and I. Society (Melb.)
announces that it has got through its last year with a
loss of £123. The chief results of the year are that the
liability to depositors has been reduced by £l2OO, and the
bank overdraft increased by £l3OO. The Federal Build-
ing Society (Brisbane) has made a profit of £144, and
probably feels good as a consequence. The St. James
Building Society (Melb.)has written off £11,205, and made
a loss for the half-year of. £2lll. United Victoria Per-
manent B. and I. Society (Melb.) reports a profit of £276.
The small building society lives mostly by eating its own
tail these times.
« • * *
It is not nice to laugh at another’s infirmities,
bub when a big Melb. bank-director would refer to his
institution’s statements of accounts as the " balance
cheats," there was scarcely a smileless visage in the
whole assemblage of broken depositors.
* * • •
Melb. tea season (’95-6) begins with the arrival
of B.s. Tsinan (due July 12). In past years the auction
irootns, where the initial cargo is annually offered, proved
a happy hunting-ground for many needy persons, who
would jauntily saunter in, fill their bags or pockets with
a “sample” from the muster packages—and depart.
Now, the tea-sampling system has been checked, all
brokers’ and merchants’ representatives being required
to sign the sample-book. With the improved method
oven, the loss of “ samples ” to ohe importer recently was
equivalent to 1 per cent. In London tea-trade, for every
sample drawn, equal weight is exchanged.
* ♦ ♦ •
The Mount Lyell Mining Go. is having a big
row with itself. The directors recently resolved to raise
£160,000 by the issue of 6 per cent, debentures. In addi-
tion to bearing interest at this decidedly high rate the
debentures were to be redeemable in 1905 at £lO5, which
additional concession makes the interest practically 6J
per cent. And over and above all this, the debenture-
holders were to have the option, at any time up to 30th
June, 1897, of exchanging each £lOO debenture for 34
shares, the shares being thus valued at £2 18s. lOd.
Mount Lyell shares have risen, within a very few weeks,
from £2 to £2 175., and with present prospects it is
possible that they may be worth £lO or more before the
date specified. In that case, the debenture-holders would
certainly take shares in exchange, and the transaction
would be equivalent to redeeming each £lOO debenture
at £340 or over. If shares go down, on the other hand,
the debenture people would stick to their original
security, consisting of a first mortgage over everything
and a big sinkingfund. The Mount Lyell directors there-
fore proposed to pawn ail the shareholders property for
a loan of £150,000 at 6 per cent., said loan to he redeem-
able by the payment of anything from £157,500 up to
(say) £600,000.
The transaction looked like such incredible
foolishness, or worse, that some of trie people in-
terested obtained an Injunction to prevent the issue
of the debentures unless sanctioned by a general
meeting of the Mount Lyell shareholders. The meet-
ing is to be held on 26th July, and the board will
probably be able then to eloquently explain away the
awkward appearance of things. Meanwhile, certain low-
minded shareholders are inquiring whether any of the
directors or their friends or relatives are among the people
who subscribed for the debentures, and, if so, how many
of them were in it, and what number of debentures they
subscribed for; and they invite the board by circular to
make a statutory declaration to the effect that it has no
interest whatever in the matter.
But the latest impression is that the injunc-
tion to restrain the directors will not be carried through.
The point in favor of the directors is that they own half
of the mine among them, and, consequently, saem bound
to have the interests of shareholders at heart.

Broken Hill mining information, as handed
over mine-office counters to the press, is sometimes
minutely explicit, but more often it leans to vagueness.
Block 10, it was reported lately, had sold 2000 tons more
of its hig-dnmp sulphides for shipment abroad at “a
satisfactory figure.” What is “a satisfactory figure,”
anyway, and would its satisfactoriness wither if expos'd
to public view? Shareholders’ interests, it is_ logical to
deduce, would be on the highroad to perdition if this
thing got out. Bat. it does shareholders no harm to know
the number of odd cwt., quarters and pounds in their
weekly batch of concentrates and in the lead contained ;
these particulars are, therefore, handed to the press.
It is a pity, anyway, that the information is
not forthcoming, for shareholders might have compared
the figure with the 15s. per ton at which the big-dump
ore was valued in last balance-sheet. But, of course,
the price must have been both high and satisfactory,
since the ore could have been retained for treatment in
the concentrators 1

British B.H. Co. only just squeezed through in
the action brought in the Victorian courts by the Dry
Creek Smelting Co. to recover £lO,OOO for alleged breach
of contract. In ’B9 the co. contracted to supply 10,000
tons of carbonates in 12 months ; but only a few tons of
ore were actually delivered. The smelting co., having got
over some technicalities successfully, presented an absurd
basis for estimating the damages ; and Hodges, J.,
brushing it aside, said he was left in the dark, and so
could only safelv give a little more than merely nominal
damages. Verdict for £2O; and a lesson to claimants.

B.H. Proprietary puts up a sulphide plant
(crushing and concentrating) capable of dealing with 1400
tons a week. It will be the first on the line to introduce
hydraulic classification, and will be more complex than
the others. That it should be resolved to try mechanical
separation (which cannot be perfect) proves that the
management thinks little of the chemical and electrical
processes, or that the terras for the latter are not liberal
enough.
° * ♦ * •
The “special commissioner” of the Aoe and
Advertiser, w'ho wrote recently on “the Broken Hill
problem,” wound up by picturing the great things that
could be done with zinc. He showed that the consump-
tion had increased nearly 100,000 tons (roughly, from
280 006 to 380,000 tons) in 10 years, without a fall in
price. But, unhappily for Broken Hill, the European
zinc-production is practically in the hands of about a
dozen firms, who have agreed so well to limit production
that nearly the whole of the world’s increase mentioned
has taken place in America. In brief, from what the Eng-
lish and Continental zinc smelters have done, one cannot
in any way judge what they can do. To get out the zinc
is not half so big a problem as to keep up the price.
♦ * ♦ »
The great sulphide problem boiled down :
Millions of tons of Broken Hill ore consist of sulphide
of lead, sulphide of zinc, quartz and other gangue, all
very closely associated, and all carrying silver. The lead
averages, sav, 28 per cent. ; the zinc, say, 22 per cent. ;
silver, say, 20oz. It cannot be smelted in the ordinary
way because the zinc would go off as vapor and condense
in the flues, and would carry off much silver with it and
would require much fluing ; and it cannot be treated by
distillation for zinc because the lead would destroy too
many of the earthenware vessels. Two methods have
been tried, each with many variations in details-concen-
tration and lixiviation. Dr. Schnabel approved of the
latter (the use of sulphuric or sulphurous acid to solve
out the zinc), and the Ashcroft process is a variation of
that. Zinc is worth in Europe now £l3 a ton ; the world’s
annual supply averages now over 300,000 tons (374,00.3 in
'92). The concentrating method, adopted at Block 14,
Block 10, 1 South, Junction, and British, is, so far, only a
partial success by reason of the exceedingly intimate
association of the lead and zinc, each carrying silver;
therefore, though the sp 'cifio gravity of zinc-blend is 4
and of Rad (galena) 7’5, only about 60 per cent, of the
lead and 50 per cent, of the silver are saved, while as much
as 10 per cent, of zinc is retained, though the object is to
get the line out with the “middlings "or tailings. A
higher percentage of lead in the concentrates can be
Secured, but, so far, only by increasing the quantity of
'slimes; and the slimes are more difficult to handle
generally than the crude ore. The “middlings" and
richer tailings produced by the present processes may
eventually be treated for zinc, though for the present the
European buyers refuse them on account of the compara-
tively high percentage of lead that still remains. But
they often contain from 20 to 30oz. silver per ton, and as
this could be saved it w ill probably be a set-off against the
high percentage of undesirable lead.
Broken Hill Proprietary Co. is 10 years old this
week.

Arnold’s patent amalgamator is out with a
prospectus by which the vendor and inventor agrees and
binds himself to erect a machine with the proceeds of
£750 £1 shares. A great deal has been said of the marvels
of the amalgamator—based on plans and a model alone—
but the writer has seen so many good-looking models turn
out failures in practice that he reserves his judgment,
merely remarking that if this thing floats, it is some evi-
dence of its gold-extracting capability.

A reliable Coolgardie correspondent of The
Bulletin savs the Great Boulder is really a magnificent
mine and looks like 7 to lOoz. for 400 ft., with great width.
The Londonderry people have cut another bonanza; the
gold is well diffused, and the quartz looks like amber and
sparkles like champagne. “Don’t forget,” adds the
writer, “ that we are stealing a great piece of territory
out of the desert. This will prove a very wide-spreading
goldfield—Dariot to Dundas, over 300 miles longN. and S.
There are sure to be duffers in plenty, but there are
many rich mines which, if not over-capitalised, will pay
well.

dare give him employment—and dock his wages of that

A Coolgardie manager to The Bulletin : “I
Inspected the new find in the Londonderry at the 100 ft.
level. The stone is of a deep amber color, carrying fine
and heavy gold right through ; it has also some very
coarse gold, such as Bdwt. pieces. I don’t think anyone
not very intimately acquainted with the first stone raised
from the golden hole would be justified in saying whether
or not the present patch is richer or poorer than that was.
The stone is different altogether. It was first met with
35ft. from the main shaft at the 100 ft. level on the hang-
ing wall in the floor of the drive going south. As they
went through it the gold-chute gradually rose until, when
Bft. through it, it was half up the face and from foot of
hanging wall across a sft. reef. They are now through
the chute and are making a winze on the gold.”

Somebody attempted last week to jump the
Londonderry, on the ground that the rent had not been
paid, but tio such error had been committed, and the
application for forfeiture was withdrawn. But such a mis-
take might be made any day with any mine. It was a simple
error of this sort which has landed the Homeward Bound,
Yalwal, N.S.W., in its present litigation. Forfeiture for
non-payment of rent, whether accidental or not, is one of
the anomalies of Australian mining law applicable to no
other industry.

Tindall’s battery was started with much cham-
pagne and mutual admiration, but the oil-engine refused
to dance to the music and would barely lift five heads.
After a few days the whole battery ran for an hour or two,
then stopped, and has not worked since. Some serious
mistake seems to have been made in re power both of
engine andjaump. , * *
The rush to Lake Cowan, 65 miles south of
Coolgardie, looks like a fizzle. After reading wires of
big nuggets, of ozs. per diem per mm, and an enormous
rich ironstone deposit at the base of a hill, we are told
that over 1000 men are stranded there and that the whole
business has been proclaimed a fraud. One supposed
author of it has been captured, and has gone out - meta-
phorically at the end of a rope, no doubt—to prove his
statement.

Shareholders in the Bayley’s Reward living in
England are beginning to write fierce newspaper-letters
becans° of the paucity of news about the mine. No news
about Bayley’s or any other Coolgardie mine may be
regarded ns very bad news indeed. Things seem so bad
and beyond hope in Bayley s that the old game of putting
the rich side out is apparently abandoned. A recent
official report admits the extreme scarcity of gold in
the bottom level. How silent the Coolgardie wires are on
this painful subject !
Wilkie Bro.’s low tender for the Coolgardie
railway has been accepted, and a start is to be made this
week. The contractors promise to open a 30-mile section
within two months.
Dear Bulletin,—First crushing from the Leich-
hardt, 9 tons for 730 z. 2dwt. smelted gold—from Berry
and Doolan’s claim. This stone was not “ selected,” but
taken haphazard from the first 50 tons brought to the
surface. The defective crushing-plant at Cloncurry is
not, however, a fair test, and the value of the •* tailings "
should bring the crushing up to lOoz. In fact, the
machinery broke down thrice getting through the nine
tons. The ground at Leichhardt is barely scratched as
yet; the gold obtained is first-class—minimum price to •
date being £4 2s. (id. A general exodus has set in from
Croydon and Charters Towers, but if any rush is antici-
pated from the South just advise your readers to go vid
Normanton. Burketown is geographically nearer, but
the creeks and gorges make the route circuitous and
weary. I have just returned and must say the reef looks
well with every indication of permanency. As I have
myself been over several routes on my own business, my
strong advice is the Normanton route and no other, more
especially for new-chums. —Yours, Hermes. [From other
reports, there appears to be no ground for a big rush.]

The Democrat reef—the chief lode of the Glen
Wills field—was found by a sheep-dog. While burrowing
in a wombat-hole he threw out a piece of quartz—
which, as it chanced, fell into other hands than those of
the dog’s owner.
♦ » • ♦
The striking of the lode in the lower tunnel of
the Democrat mine, Glen Wills, and also later in the
United Brothers, carrying free, payable gold in each case,
is evidence that the chutes live in depth. At present
these two mines, with the Smile of Fortune, are the only
shows at Glen Wills that look really well. The rest are
merely prospecting.
Aluminium, the new metal which is to revolu-
tionise the world, exists everywhere, “the super-
abundant metal,” as a prominent constituent of common ,
clay, only waiting for the inventor to come along to
get it out cheaply. In 1887 it was worth SBs. per lb.—it is
now worth 38d. The total production in ’93 is pul down
at 340,0001 b. If aluminium can do one-half that is pro-
mised about it, the inventor who can get it out at 3d. a lb.
will be able to buy out all the landlords in the world,
and then .
• » * ♦
In a Maoriland mining case the other day it
was proved that certain breaches of the Act had been
committed, the penally for which was the forfeiture of
the lease. The Warden pointed out that the case was
clearly proved, but “in consideration of the fact that the
defendant represented British capital,” he would not for-
feit, but merely impose a small fine. Which practically
amounts to an admission that if the defendant had only
represented local capital the lease would have been for-
feited. Evidently British capital is a lot more important
than the local article, and it is the duty of the law to pro-
tect the British speculator as against the local miner, and
the British capitalist is a superior animal who isn’t
amenable to mere “ colonial ” law.

Practical experience in N.S.W. colleries goes to
show that there is plenty of air passing through most
N.S.W. coal-mines, but where the ventilation is faulty is
in the distribution of the air to the various working
places; while the “return” air-ways are simply the
u iderground travelling roads. So that, as a matter of
daily experience, the working miner is badly supplied
with air in many places while at work, and has to tramp,
heavily laden with tools, to and from work along pas-
sages choked with foul air, steam, and the smoke from
explosives. The air-distribution is solely in the hands of
the underground overman, whom the Act does not suffi-
ciently recognise ; the “ return ” air-ways are subjects for
definite legislation and regulation.

The celebration in Melbourne on the anni-
versary of the death of Adam Lindsey Gordon
was a dull and dismal function. A crowd of
polite persons with bare necks and white shirts
sat round in chairs, drank coffee, and ate indiges-
tible pastry, while a few absurd folks ranted Gor-
don verses like auctioneers selling second-hand
furniture. Several people, who professed to have
known Gordon, reminisced about him with bold
unveracity, and George Walstab shocked every-
body by telling the story of how Gordon told the
two bushrangers to go to Hades. A more un-
Gordon-like affair could not possibly have been
held.
Professor Thomas Harry Huxley, who
died on 30th June, aged 70, was one of the most
useful men of the century. He subordinated bis
capacity for original scientific research to his
faculty for scientific interpretation ; and his life
was devoted, in his own words, “ to the populari-
sation of science ; to the development and organi-
sation of scientific education ; to the endless series
of battles and skirmishes over evolution ; and
to untiring opposition to that ecclesiastical
spirit, that clericalism, which, in England as
everywhere else, and to whatever denomination it
may belong, is the deadly enemy of science.”
Huxley was a born literary fighter, with a gift of
strong, clear language, which he used with smash-
ing force in his continual controversies. Enquir-
ing minds eagerly absorbed his inspiration; and
the agnostic creed which he professed has been
adopted by thousands of thoughtful persons as
humanity's best and most reasonable attitude
towards problems of the unknown and unknow-
able.

The latest Sydney tragedy, involving the
slaughter of three children by their seemingly-,
crazy father, looks like furnishing another illu-
stration of the fact that the favorite outlet of
religious mania for its pious frenzy and oblique
veneration is murder with much blood-letting.
“ Without the shedding of blood, there is no re-
mission ” is a typical Bible text, figurative of the
crimson dye that saturates the warp and woof of
the Book.

The stony path of duty ! How we all
have to trample on our very hearts ! In view of
the more-than-brotherly affection known to sub-
sist between the Judges of the N.S.W. Supreme
Court, how bitter it must have been for Chief
Justice Darley, in his capacity as Acting-
Governor, to have to assent to the release of Dean,
tried so carefully and condemned to death with
such misplaced cocksureness by brother Win-
deyer ! And, considering how sweetly courteous
brother Windoyer aforesaid has ever been to bar-
rister G. H. Reid when they have crossed swords
in court, what unspeakable anguish must have
been experienced by Premier G. H. Reid in
giving effect to the report of the Dean Commis-
sion, bursting up and heaping ignominy on
brother Windeyer’s conclusions!
* # «
The situation of the released George Dean
is a curious study. It can hardly be supposed
that he and his wife will come together again and
live happily ever after; that would be too violent
a stretch of imagination. He has not deserted
his wife, nor has she deserted him in any legal
sense, therefore they cannot be divorced under
th.e desertion clause. There is no evidence of
cruelty, adultery, or habitual drunkenness on
either side, so that a divorce can’t be obtained
under any other heading. Presumably, they will
live apart, and the lady will insist on an allow-
ance from the man whom she so nearly succeeded in
hanging, and_they will go on like that indefinitely.
• # ■
The case reported in a recent Bulletin of
the lady who said an emphatic “ No ” at the altar
recalls a similar incident in a N.S.W. town two
or three years ago. The happy pair had arrived
at the altar after a five years’ engagement, and
the parson enquired in the usual course if she
would take this man, &c. She replied cordially
that she would not. “Why not?” asked the
flabbergasted preacher. “Because he swore at
mein the churchyard,” said the bride. “I fell
over a stone as we were coming in and he said,
‘ Hold up, damn you !’ If be swears at me now I
suppose he will swear at me all my life, and he
would find it cheaper to buy a dog and kick it
occasionally. Good-day.” And the lady retired
amid a deafening silence, while the bridegroom
went and bit a piece out of the font to relieve
his feelings.

by a Sydney firm of • printers. He was required

the post. It was given to a girl, eventually.

People behind the scenes of police work
know better than anybody the many material
reasons which, apart from principles of abstract
justice, make a Public Defender necessary. At
present “the authorities” diligently suppress
every circumstance that might tell favorably to
the prisoner. A notorious case in point. When
Deeming was being brought from Perth, it was
telegraphed and published in Melbourne papers
that lie had had a series of sham tits. As a matter
of fact the fits were very serious, and attended by
certain infallible but unmentionable symptoms of
epilepsy. Certain newspaper men wished to wire
full particulars, but were in the end persuaded by
officials to forward a sceptical version ; and re-
ference to the reports of the trial will show how
skilfully the delicate ground was skimmed over.
Had Deeming had the aid of Crown defence
officials, this important aspect of his case would
probably have got fuller treatment, which, if only
in the interests of science, it deserved.

A young Australian lady, nob unconnected
with an extremely wealthy Southern landholder,
is known in London as Miss “Gyle in the Bye”—
the reason being that it was with these words she
announced the fact that, on her recent voyage to
England, the ship encountered a gale in the Bay
of Biscay. There appears to be an excellent
opening for an interpreter in Anglo-Australian
circles in London.
A Melbournite, present in London at a re-
cent dinner to welcome a distinguished person
from “the kawlinies,” says that, when he saw the
men who gathered there, he trembled for Ana-
tralia. Every shark who has gone to London in
late years to float “dicky ” mines was present, and
seemed to have brought with him “seven other
devils worse than the first.”

his prisons. Chief Secretary Peacock was then

prisoned for? “Oh,” replied the wag, “it was
because he always refused to take off his hat when
the Viand played ‘God Save the Queen.’ ” When
the day arrived, the I.G. met the party at the
prison-gates, and, addressing Davitt, proceeded to
inform him how the “ Vagabond ” on one occasion
went through “ the stockade,” and dropped little
bits of tobacco at every opportunity. He also
hinted that visitors were not allowed to converse
with the prisoners. Davitt received these edify-
ing remarks with quiet annoyance, and then
said : “ I was not in gaol so many years without
understanding what prison discipline is.” After-
wards the evident inspiration of the speech was
explained to him, and he has since been laughing
with the rest.

ACTING-GOVERNOR DARLEY.
I am the very model of a modern Governor,
I patronise each charity, nor fancy one a bore ;
1 love the Roman Catholics, the Vatican’s ambi-
tion ;
And I am quite enamored of the Sydney City
Mission,
A Protestanic charity that always does disarm me;
And you’d scarce credit how I love the loud Sal-
vation Army.
I love each sect; indeed, Ido ; I dearly love them
all;
I’m ev’ry thing to ev’ry man, like our Apostle
Paul ;
I call him “ours” because he preached to Gentiles
—me and you ;
Yet, though I’m fond of Gentiles, I’m as fond of
any Jew;
I love the Hebrew, Papist, Protestant and Infidel,
The Spiritist, Theosophist and Buddhist, I love
well ;
The Brahmin and the Hoodooist, the bland Oon-
fucian,
The Mormon, the Zoroastrian, and grand Moham-
medan,
All share my love in equal parts—l wish each part
were more—
I am the very model of a modern Governor.
THE DANCE.
If she is there
The musio is like Rubinstein,
The floor perfection,
And every polished board a line
On which we float like air,
Past recollection,
If she is there.
If she is there
The rest may think me on the shelf,
A dull, old fogey,
Sans manners, sense, good looks or pelf,
A perfect bear,
To girls a bogey,
If she is there.
If she is there—
Alas ! too deep for idle song;
Her face is graven
Where secrets of the heart belong.
In the soul’.- fair
And quiet haven —
Yes, she is there.
Maoriland.

damages arising out of the terrible Redfern rail-
way-accident. The most serious case so far
decided is that of Dr. Odillo Maher, the distin-
guished Sydney oculist, who was so terribly
scalded as to render it doubtful whethrr he will
again be able to operate. The doctor, who had
risen to the very top of liis profession at the age
of 36, and was making an increasing professional
income of about =£3ooo a year for nine months’
work, sued for =£30,000 damages, and was awarded
=£6loo only—a capital which, under existing con-
ditions, will not produce more than about one-
twelfth of his admitted professional income.

* # *
A few years ago, before the
N.S.W. rabbit-inspectors were dis-
pensed with, a rabbit-inspector
drove up to a station in the Coola-
budgeree region. Inspector (log.) :
“ How do you do, Mr. Smith ? My
name is Jones. I am the rabbit-
inspector and have come to look
over your run.” Squatter : “ Oh,
how do you do, Mr. Jones ? Glad
to see you; come in. Here, .Tuck
(one of the men), take Mr. Jones’
horses, see that they are well fed,
and have his buggy washed.” The
run was duly inspected, and in
course of a week or two the squatter
got notice from the head of the
Rabbit Branch that the rabbit-
inspector had recommended that he
should put on 20 more rabbiters,
which recommendation would have
to be complied with. In a month’s
time the inspector again visited the
station, drove up to the house, and
found squatter Smith on the veran-
dah. R.I. : “How do you do, Mr.
Smith? My name is Jones —you
don’t seem to remember me. I am
the rabbit-inspector and have come
to inspect the run.” Squatter:
“ Oh, I remember you well.
Do you see that paddock? Well,
that’s my horse paddock and is pri-
vate land. Don’t you go inside
that fence. All outside that fence is
Crown lands, and yon can camp any-
where there you like. You want to
know if I have put on 20 more rab-
biters? You can find out all about
them the best way you can.” And
then squatter Smith turned round
and walked inside.
One old Australian railway-bridge has a
romantic history. The supervising engineer was
the contractor’s frequent guest. What more
natural than his strong admiration for the pretty
governess? The contractor winked hard and p%ft
up a bridge which is the terror of everyone in the
know. And it was passed without a word, for
the engineer’s wife was jealousy itself.
A cycling Melb. penwoman proudly exhibits
her calves —and not such bad calves, either— in
the show-case of a Bonrke-street photographer.
A clever and popular little Southern pen-
lady, whose obesity finally exhausted even her big
stock of philosophy, has experienced a result from
self-treatment which quite puts in the shade
H.R.H. Tummy’s performance in the same direc-
tion. She has brought herself down by 531 b. in
two months. At first her friends were alarmed
and feared she was going into a decline. But she
only smiled sweetly and kept her secret.

The wife of a newly-knighted magnate was
recently deputationised by a number of female
suffragists, requesting her to join their society.
The betitlcd one thought a moment, and then
replied that she would really like to, as she sym-
pathised very heartily with the movement.
“But,” she added, “1 don’t think I can do so,
because, you see, the position I have to keep up
now makes it necessary that I should be careful
what 1 lend my name to. Except for that I
would, don’t you know.” “ Well,” said a deputa-
tionist, “ it is just because of your position, such
as it is, that wo asked yon, and that’s the only
reason why we wanted your name.”

Judge Hamilton (Vic.) doesn’t take a
serious view of the little “presents” which a
husband makes to his wife. The learned Hamil-
ton, speaking feelingly, as from sad experience,
holds that Dresden china ornaments, &e., are pre-
sented to the home rather than to its mistress.
“ A man goes out with his wife in the usual way,
anil she takes an idiotic fancy to something, and
he buys it; that’s all about it.” Hamilton must
be either a widower or a recklessly-brave man.

• * •
Mrs. Dean, the Maoriland baby-farmer,
under sentence of death, was originally a school-
teacher, and is an accomplished musician. She
was the widow of a doctor when she married
Charles Dean, a wholly illiterate pick-and-shovel
man, and is described as having the appearance
and manners of a refined lady. At the time of
her arrest she had been in the baby-farming busi-
ness for five years. At the trial her pleasant face
and gentle manners made a good impression on
everybody, and if the evidence had not been over-
whelming she would almost certainly have escaped.
She took her sentence with perfect composure,
and departed smiling to die condemned cell.
Mrs. D. seems to have been a pleasant woman
who reluctantly killed infants for a living, because,
the earnings of her poor pick-and-shovel husband
did not suffice for any of the small refinements of
life; and she would probably make as good a wife
for a nice old man with means as any other aver-
age person of her sex.

Many Victorian sassieby people are read-
ing up the “Voyage of the Sunbeam ” —evidently
with a view to gushing about it to Lady Brassey.
on her arrival —quite oblivious of the fact that the
first Lady 8., and not the second, was the
authoress. The feelings of a lady when she is
gushed to about her husband’s first wife’s book
must be akin to those of Cain when he laid out
Abel.

TO—THE LOST ONE.
The whole wide world would love you
If once it saw your eyes,
Where diamond fancies glimmer
Like fireflies’ fairy shimmer—
No star in Heaven above you
With their cold beauty vies.
The whole wide world would love you
If once it saw your eyes.
The whole wide world were well lost
To hold you in my arms,
To watch—aside and slyly—
The rosebud and the lily—
Like trembling flow’rs wind-tossed—
Of your most matchless charms.
The whole wide world were well lost
To hold you in my arms.
The whole wide world is dying
Since you have gone from me.
Set in a frame of fancies
Your loveliness entrances;
But naught is left but sighing
And bitter ecstacy.
The whole wide world is dying
Since you have gone from me.

Hygiene teaches that nothing is so bene-
ficial to the human constitution as thorough
cleanliness a theory verified by modern revela-
tions of the d-adly microbes which infest the
flesh and blood—especially if we do not keep
ourselves in a clean condition, which ought to be
the first aim of every individual with any esteem
for himself. The use of Arnykos has proved to be
very important to this end, by its great sanitary
power over the skin, mouth, teeth, &c. If you
value yourself, never be without this excellent pre-
paration-

846 Maoquarie-street North, Sydney,
July 2, 1895.
Mv Dear Moorabinda—
The Working and Factory Girls’ Club ball (the
most purely fashionable Sydney pay-to-gb-in
dance of the year now that the School of In-
dustry ball is only an aristocratic memory) came
off at the Centennial Hall, the other Wednesday,
with much “ society ” success. In one way—if the
main object is to fill a bag—it is sometimes rather
fatal to have too much society, for quantity, not
quality, is the key to financial success. From a
fashionable standpoint, the W. and F.G. Club ball
was highly successful, Acting-Gov. Darley and
three daughters loomed large on the dais, and
though the accoutrements of the acting vice-regal
damsels were not up to bird-of-Paradise pitch,
still the dazzling furniture they had to sit on made
up for any sartorial smudgings about the patron-
ising party, which had a double-barrelled welcome
at the door and again at the dais. The ball
decorations were not overdone, the floor was ex-
excellent, the supper-table, with artistic floral
effects, “ groaned ” in the basement, and the only
thing I really didn’t like was the music, which was
rather flavorless. Even Dr. M'Carthy’s Lyceum
waltz, as scored for the full band, seemed quite a
different thing from its sparkling self in solo form.
Also, the bugbear of these amateur Compositions
is that the bandmaster always drones through the
long “ introduction” as if the poor audience was
loth to lose a quaver of it.
The most delicate-looking dress was worn by
the president—Mrs. Hutton —pale-grey and white,
mingled like a dove’s plumage. It at once sug-
gested that scriptural fowl —only doves don’t wear
diamonds, Mrs. Douglas Mackenzie (hon. sec.)'
wore cream-color brocade with heliotrope sleeves
and violets; Mrs. J. W. Flood spotted blue
brocade, the bodice veiled in black tulle ; Mrs.
Prendergast also wore pale-blue brocade, the skirt
covered with silvery stuff. A pretty girl, in
white satin modishly picked out with straps of
blue and silver, looked the double of the departed
Miss Kate Duff, and a plump matron from ’Ome
most unwisely wore a white toilet with a dollish
trimming of pearls and peacock-blue velvet points,
in which she looked to scale a ton. By the way,
travellers are never tired of scolding Australian
women for the loud way they dress in the street —
but I think they get equal with the soberly-clad
out-door Englishwomen when the two meet in a
ball-room. The belle from ’Ome out for the night
nearly always wears far less clothing up above
than does her Australian adversary, and her even-
ing tenue is frequently far more staring—even
vulgar—than the local article’s.
Not many uniforms lit up the festive scene, and
the sprinkling of dashing militaires in monkey
jackets was not enough to leaven the whole lump.
Talking of uniforms, I believe the committee of
the W. and F.G. olub ball are to be congratulated
on inaugurating a much-needed reform. I hear
they omitted to send great sheaves of compli-
mentary cards to the naval beaux in port—who
didn’t turn up in consequence. The naval man
in his glittering uniform who adds so much
dazzle to the mise-en-scene, always brings his
appetite with him, but contributes just nothing at
all to the cold-blooded balance-sheet, and it is time
ball-committees saw through him. His apologists
say that his pay is so trifling that you cannot
blame him—which looks (in this plutocratic age)
as if the over-trimmed sea-dog has been foisted
into a fashionable niche where he has no business
to be. Is it really possible that, pecuniarily speak-
ing, Captain Corcoran’s position is amongst the
“gods? ”
Much gape-seed grew round the Gaiety girls,
who plunged hot-foot into the dancing about
midnight. Neither Palotta (who gave a guinea to
the decoration fund) nor Maud Hobson turned up,
but Decima Moore (who has been out of the bill
with a severe cold for a week) arrived in cream
brocade with a demure fichu of white chiffon
which enhanced the pretty Puritan air she has
about her, and a gold chain wound twice round
her neck. She was, of course, squired by her only
Hope in faultless evening dress, and proved her-
self to be the best ball-room dancer among the
Gee-Gees. Claire Leighton, Blanche Massey’s
understudy, turned up looking rather like a panto-
mime queen in pink, with a silver waist-fringe
something like a Fijian’s petticoat (only the
nigger wears nothing underneath), the bodice
trimmed with silver in a one-sided way, a pink
Toby ruffle round her throat, and a sprig of pink
heather in her golden hair. Her strongest point
was her bouquet—spoils from the theatre—which
she used like a boxing-glove. Handsome Florence
Lloyd, in a black ball-dress with fuzzy black tulle
sleeves, looked exceedingly nice and just a little
naughty ; and Laura Kearney, in black sack with
big black sleeves, looked like the ghost of a
fashionable woman dipped in ink.
The clever Magpie Club, composed of society
amateurs, has seen fit to change its name. It is
nuw called theldlers —not very original, but much
apter than the Magpie, which used to suggest
that the members were blaok-and-white and with
thievish propensities. The Idlers _ purpose pro-
ducing an up-to-date comedy, “Going it,” early
this month.
I have received a couple of original musical
compositions, neither of which is a waltz, strange
to say. From Melbourne comes the “Lightning”
concert overture, dedicated to Lord Hopetoun by
Mdlle. Alice William?. The overture is a weird
musical ebullition with an odd picture on the
cover in which a full moon and a crescent moon
appear one above the other, while a double dart of
forked lightning bursts out of a clear sky. The
overture itself is peculiar, but easily played, and
would have a greater success, I think, if the com-
poser wrote it all over again as a galop. Miss
Lilian Lawless sends me the “Scotia Schottische,”
dedicated to Lady Duff. The “Scotia” has a
verra Scottish title-page, and is a lively rhythmic
schottische to be played with facility by anybody
who knows a sharp from a flat.
I hear of a very embarrassing episode concern-
ing a professional warbler lately arrived in Sydney.
He had been all the evening engaged in fervently
making love sotto voce to a Hebe in the cafe,
mumbling his amorosities so that the rest of the
youths should not hear, when suddenly, in a
momentary lull, the maiden’s voice rang out loud
and clear: “ I’ve told you I love you, and when
it’s my day out —so what more do you want?”
When the roar of laughter had subsided it was
found that he had fled. After all, men are really
ever so much more bashful than women.
The second of the shilling concerts organised by
that enterprising Sydney photographer, Mr. Eden,
to enable the majority of people to hear the Esty-
Marsh Co., drew a crowd of six thousand to the
Town Hall last Saturday. It looked as if a Mel-
bourne Cup crowd had got jammed under a roof,
and when the applause broke out, as it did after
every singing number, the noise was heroic. It
warmed the artistes to put in their best work
(though one would think a stuffily-dressed mob on
the very platform breathing down the singers’
necks would be far from pleasant), and, from a
managerial point of view, nothing could have been
more successful than the second pop. And, from
an artistic standpoint, too, for when the big
building’is 5.R.0.. the acoustics are splendid, and
the deadly Town Hall echo is sopped up. Alec.
Marsh, the star of the co., turned up in large
voice and with apparently the same scarlet button-
hole as of yore. Rival warblers assert that Mr.
Marsh’s success is due to the belligerent way he
cocks those lurid flowers at the audience, and cer-
tainly if good stage presence is worth anything,
Mr. Toreador Marsh must owe some of his pres-
tige to the way he fills the platform, no matter
how large it is. Mrs. Marsh, in a pair of big
green sleeves and other draperies, Madame En-
riquez, and Mr. Cunninghame, were also received
witli cheers.
Herr Vollmar —who played the ’cello like a true
artist—was not so enthusiastically welcomed.
The fact is that the passionate-sounding ’cello,
with all the pathos and none of the squawkiness
of the violin, is not yet popular in Australia,
where people want to know what it’s all about,
and would like to have words put to the “ Moon-
light Sonata ” so that a fat soprano in tight satin
could help Beethoven out. Widely speaking,
local musical culture cannot possibly rely on its
own soul to unravel the composer’s meaning—and
until some enterprising Philistine publishes the
“ words ” of all the string movements on the pro-
gramme I am afraid the ’cello will have a frosty
time.
“Praise everybody and everything” is the
motto of most singers who come to Australia, and
most of them get through very well on these
lines—all barring a recently-departed short, fat
tenor, who overdid the thing. His flattery was
immense. All the geese were swans, and he
would sit clasping the white hand of some parrot-
voiced songstress for hours in a spirit of reverent
wonder that such a marvel should have no greater
musical record than a huge success at an (admission
free) Band of Hope. Then he would wipe away a
tear, and, perhaps, reverently salute the lady on
the cheeks in an “ I dedieate-thee-to-music” kind
of spirit—after which he would almost formally
engage her for his great concert-season, and
vanish. The number of chortling women who
told their circles, in confidence, that they were to
be the stars of the So-and-so concert eo., and got
the photos, ready in advance, was legion, but not
one debut arose out of the whole thing. The next
anxious foreign tenor who visits these shores is
advised to praise these would-be-vocalists at a safe
distance.
Speaking of the noisy art, I think W, H. Paling
and Co.’s recently-issued musical catalogue is a
distinctly good notion. The catalogue is bound
in dark blue, with flesh-pink lettering, and con-
tains in alphabetical order the names of almost
every musical composition—from “ Sweet Marie ”
to a fugue—that there is on the face of the earth.
There are certainly no lists given of music for the
tom-tom or the early Greek lyre, but, apart from
this oversight, the catalogue is an invaluable
guide to the best tnusio for every sort of voice or
instrument.
Sydney Steamship Companies’ second annual
ball came off at Paddington Town Hall, last
Friday, with much success. The decorations
complimented the occasion. Life-buoys, flags in
profusion from the various steamship companies,
ferns, laurels, with a cosy corner of the cosiest
pattern, and the stage arranged as a drawing-
room, made the hall a scene of beauty generally ;
indeed, the only fault to be found with the
arrangements was that rather too exclusive atten-
tion was paid to the hall. Ball-goers do not live
by dancing alone, and the Paddington hall has a
wealth of nooks which a little carpeting makes
into admirable flirtation corners. These, how-
ever, the decorator had ignored, and people who
wished to “isolate themselves to exchange
thought ” had either to seek the icy balconies or
bawl sweet nothings at one another in the teeth of
the music. A fork-supper was set out cafe
fashion with much floral decoration, and a full
orchestra supplied the excellent music.
Several neat dresses took the flour. A well-cut
pale violet color brocade, with otf-the-shoulder
sleeves and shoulder-straps of violet, was
well 5 worn by a blonde. Another episode was
a white silk with a suspicion of silver
fringe in the diagnosis ; also, a well-cut black
silk tres decollete on a lady with shoulders
a la Isabelle Urquhart—both these were worthy.
One or two damsels had apparently gone to the
rag-bag for inspiration—especially one shingle-
sbaped maiden in black velvet weirdly adorned
with yellow roses. She was almost as unearthly as
the person whoappearson thetheatricalprogramme
as a “guest.” Mr. F. Burgess (U.S.S. Co.) was
hon. sec., and Mr. Harvio-treasurer, while Messrs.
E. A. Desjardins, Harivol, and E. Newman, made
the most attentive hosts you would wish to see.
The next nautical hop will be the historic East
Sydney Rowing Club ball, at West’s rooms, on
19th inst. Mr. P. C. Ternes is hon. sec. this year
to what is always one of the most spirited public
hops of the winter. But the hop of the month
will be the Tempe ball—the oldest and most
popular charity dance of the season, and one
which, for occult reasons, men uiake a point of
attending in great shoals. It is to eventuate in
Sydney Town Hall, on 24th inst., and the tickets
are selling like postage-stamps. By the way, a
Jonah’s-gourd sort of charity ball is to occur next
Wednesday, in Sydney Town Hall. Its object is
the relief of distress, but as the charge for the
Centennial Hall is necessarily high, and the
tickets are only five shillings, I don’t suppose
there will be much over for deserving indigence
after all the bills are paid. People who provoke
cheap charity Rtes generally hire a leas expensive
hall, where so much of the money taken hasn’t to
be paid away in exes. The object of this
spasmodic dance is a deserving one, no doubt —
only, what sized crowd would it have to be to
make the Town Hall pay at five shillings per
caput? Dancing would have to be impossible to
ensure financial success.
The first sacred utterance of Governor ’Ampden
has been cabled out to N.S.W., and I must say it
sounds reassuring—Gov. H. “expects to find the
colonists of N.S.W. as charming as the climate is
delicious!” The new Governor seems to have
got the hanky-panky very fluently already, and I
won’t say “Blessed is he who expects nothing,”
because the Viscount’s tinkling introductory
phrase doesn’t necessarily infer that he expects
anything of the sort. Poor Governor Duff’s first
gloomy remark cabled through was that lie was
going out to Sydney under social disadvantages,
as compared with Lord Jersey and Lord Caring-
ton.” What a difference !
The newspapers have a queer way of putting it
sometimes. In touching on Gov. Hampden’s
qualifications, a Sydney daily remarked : —“ The
new Governor has had Indian but not colonial ex-
perience, except that he has been a director of the
National Mortgage and Agency Co. of Maoriland,
Limited, for 14 years.” Yet 1 have no doubt the
genius who penned that paragraph laughs heartily
when the low comedian says he knows French—
his brother was a French polisher !
Three V.R. damsels ought to make the approach-
ing landing of Gov. Hampden (who expects to run
his flag up early in November) of more than pas-
sing interest to local johnniedom—if these re-
trenched youths can rouse up to take notice of any-
thing except the possibility of skulldragging a
fortune out of the bowels of Coolgardie, which
seems to be their absorbing topic. Even the
Gaiety girls fall flat, in a stage-door sense, and
complain of neglect. In London, America, Mel-
bourne, and Adelaide, the stage-entrance was
blocked with gilded youths, one of whom (in
Adelaide) did the Sir Walter Raleigh act up-to-
date by flinging his top-coat over a muddy cab-
wheel ! In Sydney nobody takes the trouble to
wait to see the Gee-Gees go in or come out —
though I am wrong there. The story goes that a
long, lean female with a Scriptural brooch hovered
about the Lyceum stage-door for hours one even-
ing, evidently bent on telling Maud Hobson that
all flesh is grass, or something of that sort.
Ministers of religion have given the Gaiety girls
the usual valuable advt. by imploring the Christian
public not to go near their wicked show. But how
the ministry come to know so much about it is a
matter that ought to be seriously sifted by their
churchwardens, it seems to me. The attitude of the
city, however, towards the frisky Gee-Gees is
much more hospitable. They have been received
with musical honors and fizz at Sydney Town
Hall, and the clergy’s horrified expostulations
evidently roll off Gog and Magog like water off a
duck’s back. At the same time, I think it reflects
rather frivolously on the Town Hall doges that
they should delight to honor the racketty team
which aims its toe at the ceiling, and leave the
Dacres—high-class artists as they are, without one
visible vulgarity in their well-chosen repertoire—
officially unwelcome.
The last nights of the “Gaiety Girl” showed
that if the G.G. cannot change her spots (if she
has any) she can at least juggle with her draperies.
In one way it rather cheapens a frock to set it
aside too easily—at any rate, you never see it
done with the mo; o expensive improvements on
Mother Eve’s fig-leaves. Maud Hobson, for
example, clings to her Madame Du Yan-ish
toilets button for button as at the premiere, but
Decima Moore altered to a blue erdpon gown over
a blue lining, with a narrow black sash and a hat
with black bows, in the first act. The easy change
suggested that Miss Moore doesn't mind |iow
many she buys of these sorts. Madge Rossell
burst forth in a new yellow silk gown in which
Monkhouse alluded to her as “ Colman,” though
“Coolgardie” would have been more locally up-
to-date—but the marvel of the latter nights of the
Girl was the crisp, just-back-from-the-wash aspect
of the bonnie frocks. No signs of a three-weeks’
run on their hems, though it was very apparent
on the nether edges of the “society girls ” unim-
pressive, neatly-patterned silks,
A seasonable item in a parcel of new goods from
Glen’s (Melb.) is Christahel’s “introduction to
the Art of Singing.” Veteran Christabel (who
is identical with Wickins, of the Vamping Tutor)
teaches most of the principles which Clifford
Halle is just now rubbing into Young Australia
from the platform, therefore anyone who feels
chastened by a Halle lecture will respect Chris-
tabol’s hints and exercises on articulation, voice-
production, breath-taking, and other matters that
gifted amateurs treat with lofty unconcern. Glen’s
latest shilling “ Federal ” book includes “Peace
or War,” from Mozart's “Magic Flute,” “When
Sorrow Sleepeth,” “ The Briar Rose,” and
five reliable old ballads into the bargain.
Fred. Oowen’s “ Where Shadows are Not,” a
graceful song with a pretty refrain, is another of
Glen’s recent publications. “A Prayer,” by
Mascagni, is excellent in Italian, but an English
translation of words doesn’t hitch on to the music.
Among several pieces for pianoforte the “Rick-
shaw Barn Dance” is good enough in its way,
and Theo. Bonheur’s “ Skipping-Rope Dance ” is
much better. Neither of these lively jingles
has the grit of Zelman’s “Gnome Dance,”
a quaint little weird production of the new Italian
school of architecture. Zelman, the shaggy old
captain of the V.R.C. brass band, seems to have
dipped into “Pagliaoci ” for his inspiration.
The Earl of Yarmouth, tiring of skirt-dancing,
has assumed a new pose. A song, words and
music composed by the “Girl,” was sung at
Elton’s benefit in Brisbane by Miss K. Hardy.
It is called “Dear Home Faces,” and twaddles
sweetly about dear mother and the family
generally, and expresses longing to return to the
same. So far as. can be ascertained, nobody in
Australia is trying to keep the Earl away from
’Ome.
Another step towards milliner-made male gar-
ments. The Princeof Wales, at the instigation of
the British Association of Silk Manufacturers,
intends to introduce for evening wear flowered
silk and brocaded vests. Indeed, a well-known
English fashion-paper talks gravely of a revival of
Sheridanesque Styles for fashionable men. *
In ri the rumor lately circulated regarding the
Misses FitzStubbs’ intention of starting a fashion-
; able millinery business in London, although the
Canard has been already contradicted, jt may be
as well, perhaps, to say that I have seen a cable,
gram of denial.

Messrs. Gollin and. Company, Australian
and New Zealand agents for the popular polisher,
Brooke’s Monkey-brand Soap, have established ail
office in Sydney, where they will conduct business
as general merchants and importers. The firm
has been trading for years past in Adelaide and
Melbourne, and is also largely interested in
Tolley and Co., Limited, Fremantle, W.A.,
whom it will represent in Sydney.*
The first electoral campaign meeting of the
N.S.W. Labor-party in Sydney was mostly riot
and miscellaneous football scrum. About nine-
tenths of the meeting consisted of enthusiastic
supporters, but the other tenth was v full of rum
and devastation, and sometimes as many as four
fights were in progress at once. Altogether it was
a great occasion, and one or two men had no
clothes worth speaking of when It was over. Evi-
dently a healthy interest is being manifested in
Sydney politics just now. ,
Scenes between barrister and Judges in the
Melb. courts grow monotonous. Purves, Q.C., is
the star performer as a rule. The Judge repri-
mands or admonishes Purves. on some point or
other, whereupon the learned counsel betrays
great emotion, and says that the imputation on
his professional character is too, too horrible, and
the Judge, chafing the corner of his eye with a
beautiful cambric handkerchief, suggests an
adjournment of the court until next day, and the
Philistines in the gallery wonder what would lie '
thought of grown-up men who behaved thus in
any less-learned department of life.
“ The tiger weeps !” said Voltaire, when,
with an exposition of tire evils of war, he had set
the King of Prussia a-sniveiling. The Tiger of
the Victorian Bar—blubbering over his wounded
self-importance—will be a joy for ever to thou-
sands the remorseless laceration of whose sensi-
bilities was to him apparently as much a labor of
love as of £ s. d. Prestige re-acts upon itself,
and in the game of bluff especially, nothing sue- |
ceeds like success ; but the most imposing edifice
of bluster, for all that, is never without an insidi-
ous crack of self-conscious weakness, which may
at any moment lay the whole'structure in maudlin
ruin. A bully is a bully, in fact, because he is
“ emotionally unstable ” at bottom. a|
Some St. Alban’s Chronicles.
The highest prices that yearlings bred at St.
Alban’s have brought at auction were: South
Hamilton, 1200 guineas; Petrea, 1550 guineas;
Avernus, 1350 guineas; Lord Randolph, 2300
guineas ; Garcon, 1400 guineas ; Red bourne, 1325
guineas ; Ascot Vale, 1050 guineas ; Pascarel, 1075
guineas ; Pounamon, 1000 guineas. Ail, with the
exception of Pascarel and Red bourne, won races
• # i - *
Eleusis, one of the imported mares at St.
Alban’s, is closely related to some winners of
classic races this year in England. Sir Visto,
winner of the Derby, is by her sire, Barcaldine,
while her granddam is full-sister to the dam of
Wisdom, who sired La Sagesse, winner of the
Oaks, and None the Wiser. The latter was sold
lately, at auction, for 7200 guineas—a great price
for a three-year-old filly, and only once exceeded. I
» # : *
Auraria, the Trenton filly, who won the prin-
cipal weight-for-age races in South Australia, is
out of Aura, who is regarded as one of the most
valuable mares at St. Alban’s. Leslie M‘Donald
had Footstep and Lord Wilton at one time of
their racing career, and never was satisfied till he
had one of the breed in the stud.
« « ♦ -vf. . • ’ •
Nordenfeldt (deceased) was a son of Onyx, who
was full sister to Robinson Crusoe, one of the
prizes in the St. Alban’s stud. Onyx was a very
indifferent performer, and was purchased by Mr.
Wentworth, of N.S. W., at the break-up of the
celebrated Maribyrnong stud, for 800 guineas. Mr.
Wentworth afterwards sold her to the New
Zealand Stud Co. »

La Tosca, winner of the A. J.O. St. Leger and
Adelaide Birthday Cup, is a daughter of Robinson
Crusoe ; and is what is described as being “on
the small side.” Near Geelong (Vic.) resides a
famous pony trainer who .one day called at St.
Alban’s to ask Leslie Macdonald if he might have
a gallop on the St. Alban’s course. Being
answered in the affirmative, the ponyite arrivedJ
with a good-looking prad. As there was only one
horse, Leslie asked the visitor why he sent him
alone, and was told there was nothing good
to go with him. “Shall I lend you one of our
best?” “ Right!” was the answer. “ Fetch out
the white-faced pony ” —and La Tosca trotted
out. “She’ll be no use,” said the visitor, “but
never mind ; let her go !” The dismay depicted
on the face of the ponyite at the end of that gallop
may be more readily imagined than described.
* * *

Matthew Bryant to buy her, us Simson was not
in good circumstances. Bryant turned the mars
out without trying her for racing, and whenever

Bryant did not forget to remind him tiiat “ there
was that d d weed you made me give .£7O for.

in foal to Lothair for 50 guineas. Boz won tm*
Caulfield Cup, and wound up a brilliant career <>y
carrying 10st. into second place for the New-
market Handicap. Until 1803 Hypatia had 1,0
foals that lived except the tluee mentioned. lb‘ r

garded as one of the best ever bred at St. Alban s,

A son of ex-Premier Dibbs is an engineer
on an ooean-going steamer leaving Sydney, and
much resembles his ex-Republican father in ap-
pearance.
Arthur Palmer, President of Q. Leg.
Council, grows very sere and yellow. His voice
now only carries a few yards, and he has to be
almost carried into the Chamber. The old man’s
devotion to duty and his salary moves his party
almost to tears.

“Phormi” : “ I am one
of thousands packed into Christ-
ohnrch (M.L.) Tattersall s listen-
ing to a portly polit cian bellowing
out how much he has ever loved
Christchurch and its people. It is
Hon. Richard Seddon, Premier of
M.L., and my memory reverts to
another scene. Kntnara, 1880 ;it
has been snowing heavily, and
snowballing is being indulged in
by old and young. A milkman
is passing an hotel, and the pro-
prietor, with a well-aimed shot,
knocks his hat off. The milkman
retaliates ; the combatants get
closer and closer, bombarding
each other with vigor. Finally it
is a hand-to-hand encounter, and
they are cramming snow down
each other’s necks. A crowd has
gathered by this time, and the
friendly duel grows very much in
earnest. However, just when the
elated crowd are expecting a
genuine go, the milkman capitu-
lates and the hotel-man retires,
white with snow and red with
triumph. The victor is ‘ Dick
Seddon. I had not seen him for
14 years, but he had not altered
two per cent.”
Squatter George Robertson, of Colac
(Vie.), has just died at the same age and on the
same day and month of the year as his brother
William. The brothers (Victorian natives, but
Cambridge University men) owned some of the
finest land and some of the best-bred stock m
Australia. George Robertson was an enthusiastic
and accomplished cricketer, and at one tune
captained a Victorian 18 which beat W. U.
Grace’s 11 by one innings.
A well-known Melbourne man, who in
four years lost wife, family, and fortune, drowned
his old dog the other day, not having the money
to pay the annual tax.
A magistrate named Mathias Davies heard
the police-court opening of the charges against the
extradited boomster, Jabez Balfour. The long
arm of coincidence again !
Wide Bay (Q.) News has made a sad mix
of the birthdays of Bismarck and Parkes. It
credits the latter with having received 120,000
letters on his natal day, along witli trimmings in
the way of a 20-yards-long sausage, a 2001 b. cheese,
and a wreath for his tomb.

The late R.C. Bishop Moran, of Dunedin,
spent the last hour of his life watching from the
window of his chamber the funeral procession of
his old sacristan. “I’ll be the next,” he said.

Vic., and now Mrs. Lindsay Miller) has just pub-
lished a new novel, “The Moving Finger,” and
has almost finished another Australian romance.
Her publisher has bespoken all her work on satis-
factory terms.

David Syme, of the Age, would have been
a Presbyterian parson had not life at a German
university somewhat shattered his belief in the
Westminster Catechism. Thus was some German
professor the cause of Australia’s biggest law-
case, Speight v. Syme.
A much-discussed S.A. millionaire lately
created a sensation on Adelaide block. The old
man had on a much-patched pair of pants, whilst
his daughter, who was with him, was decked in
the latest from Paris. However, like the family
fortunes, the old man’s pants had solid founda-
tions.
When Marcus Clarke’s novel, “For the
Term of His Natural Life,” was first published in
Massina’s Australian Journal, the circulation
of that paper, during its publication, decreased by
over 4000 an issue. The publication of the work
in book-form was a serious source of loss to Massina
at first.
Alexander Sutherland, M.A (Melb.), is
one of the very few Australians who has done well
by his pen—and such a prosaic pen ! In royalties
on his published school histories, geographies, &c.,
which he wrote while headmaster of Carlton Col-
lege, he receives well on towards <£looo per
annum.

on the Privy Council. At the opening of Q. Par-
liament “he was in knee-breeches and buckle-

black hat of the English Judges.” The Tas-

J. C. Stewart, once Melbourne’s Mayor,
is appointed its City Solicitor, vice Frank Stephen,
demised. Stewart used to be attorney to the
Presbyterian A-sembly, but gave it up because of
theological scruples as a follower of Pastor Strong.
He was succeeded by Mat Davies, who was as
orthodox as they make ’em.
Cole, of Melb. Arcade book-fame, now
runs “ the biggest bookstall in the world.” Some
30 years ago he ran a much smaller one—in old
Paddy’s market. It is now all electric-light;
then it was all slush-lamps. Cole ascribes his
prosperity to judicious advertising. That’s it.
Fortunes are founded, not on advertising, but on
judiciousness.
Parson “Jack” Goode, of Moonee
Ponds, Vic., whose straightforward and cant-
less style lias made him very popular, was
formerly a policeman. It would do every
prospective clergyman good to do a few
months’ night-duty on the Little Lonsdale-
street or Rocks beat and learn the grim reali-
ties of things in general. Another constable
who may be seen daily in Swanston-street,
Melb., is studying for the Bar.
Howard Willoughby, the Abgus leader-
writer, turns out the most undecipherable
“ copy” on the Melbourne press. One night
lately, wishing to illustrate the decline of local
trade, he wrote that the high tariff found
Melbourne a city of head offices, and left it
a place of branch establishments, just as (ad-
versely) “ Augustus found Rome brick and
left her marble.” As it appeared in print,
uowever, poor o’d Augustus was represented
as having “found Rome brisk and left her
miserable ! ”
Surgeon O’Hara, a mad wag, has in
his house a screen presented by a grateful
Jap. patient, and when the great Raymond
Blathwayt was visiting him one evening, he
admired it very much, and asked where it
had come from. “From the Mikado,” said
the surgeon, who added jokingly—“ I know
him very well.” Blathwayt, like the solemn
Cockney he is, took it quite seriously, and
said; “I’m going to Japan, and would be
glad of an introduction” —which O’Hara,
never suspecting that anybody in the world
could be so dull, took as a joke. But it
wasn’t a joke, fur Blatherwait actually wrote
from Adelaide asking for a letter to the Jap.
Emperor. This was altogether too much for
the sporting sawbones, who at onee sat down
and wrote : “ Dear Mikado, —Allow me to
introduce my friend, Mr. R. Blathwayt. Any
favor conferred on him will be received as
if you were obliging me.—Yours, etc., H.
O’Hara.”
An Oamaru (M.L.) bankrupt was lately
charged with not having kept proper books.
Wnereupon ha proudly turned up one of the
volumes and showed the inscription on the fly-
leaf: “Jacob Loudon, his Cash Book, by the
Help of God.” But the Court refused to be
moved even by this evidence of good faith.

Melb. imdergrad. who was constantly accom-
panied to her university-classes by a duenna.

Bent’s portrait still hangs in Parliament
House, Melbourne, alongside that of Peter Lalor,
and certain of “the authorities” are anxious to
have it removed. The picture of the Brighton Pet
is his own property, but he declines to take it
home. The Bulletin suggests that it be carted
into the cellar, and that the ex-Speaker be charged
storage for the time it remains there.
While Parson Haweis was tickling the ears
of his fashionable congregation at St. Paul’s,
Wellington (M.L.), the other Sunday night, and
causing his hearers to temporarily regard Chris-
tianity as a beautiful theory clothed in pleasant-
ness and melodious phrases, a recent importation
from “ th’ ould dare ” thus served it out at a R.C.
tabernacle. He drew a graphic picture of a lovely
spring morning “ wid de pretty burrds whistlin’
and singin’ in the bushes, when all at wanst yez
hear a moighty pale of thunder and 'lightnin’ and
the storrum rowles around yez in terrible and
furious blackness. ’Tis then yea’ll be afther
thinkin’ av yer God, take it from me.” As a big
smile lit up the face of the congregation he con*
tinued : “ And it isn’t anny manner av usethryin*
to collar salvation widoutan itfort, let me tell yea.
Yea’ll mver win Hivven in a walk-over, Oi'U
promise yez.”

E, W. Hornung, who poses in London M
an Australian writer, is another example of those
“ who travel on the game.” His first work, “ A
Bride from the Bush,” raised a good deal of indig-
nation. He is now to the fore again with “ The
Boss of Taroomba.” All that Hornung knows of
Australia was gained in the twelve months during
which he was tutor on Mossgiel station, N.S. W.
Michael Davitt was much puzzled by the
cablegram he got from Justin M'Carthy : “Need
urgent; elections immediately.” He did not
know whether this meant that the “ need ” of him-
self was “ urgent,” or that money was urgently
required. Knowing the Irish party, he suspected
the latter, and most likely he hit the mark. Any-
how, he is not starting for England for another
month,

commissioner, was onee asked to sign a voluminous
and lavish expense account for one of his sub ;
ordinates who had been on a Gilliesian official

The last poem of White Knight Fitzgibbon
(=£lsoo a year) the bard of the Melbourne board of
sewers and stinks, appears in the correspondence
column of the Argus. Says Gibbon ;
To the Editor of the Argus.
Sir, —With oracular faculty blest, Oriel says Crom-a-boo
is a “crest! ” In that he is as perfectly right as in whai
he towards me shows his spile. Is good done by such
prosing and rhymes? Would such e’er find a place in ths
Times? Yet, “Look out, great Times,” once was your
cry, though in days, alas, now long gone by. Better
’twere if you still aimed as high.— E. C. Fitzgibbon.
Poor old Gibbon has written much awful verse,
but he never got down quite so low as this before.
“We are all human, only some are more
human than others.” — Walter Berant,
“ Crom-a-boo,” the irresistibly funny and
rollicking Irish song of the Hoben Brothers, which
Sappho .Smith opined would prove “a terrible
success,” is already proving. Lonnen has taken
it up in London, and Lauri is to sing it in
Australia ; it is to be well introduced in America,
and in Maoriland. where that clever comedian,
Percy, of the Pollard Lilliputians, is making a
feature of it. It is also a universal success and
heard everywhere. Paling’s have already cleared
the first big edition, and have got out two others
in E flat and F. It is certainly the funniest Irish
football-song ever written, and the press every-
where has risen to describe it as the best comic
song of the day, and funnier than “Killaloe ” or
“ Ballyhooley.” It is just the thing lor an aspir-'
ing comique, pro. or amateur.*
PURVES’ TEAR.
Within a crystal phial lay
The liquid gem. Its lambent beam
Did with an amorous sun-shaft play,
And like a serpent’s eye its gleam
Could fascinate with wile supreme.
A man had caught it from the jaw
Of him who wept, and then with care
Had bottled it, for of the law
The grim deceits and humbug were
All glittering in essence there.

The Westralian Premier has posted a
notice on his door that he cannot see any more
office-seekers, and that the chance of employment
in the Government service is extremely remote.
Premier Kingston states that, because of
what they have done for the producers, there are
no six men in S.A. more cordially hated by the
commercial classes than the members of the pre-
sent Ministry.

Maori war, but that is recouped by the value of
the lands made available for settlement.” —Tkea.
Ward in London
The Vic. Upper House is upheld as an
assurance against hasty legislation. For ten years
the people have maintained a Lower House
pledged to legalise One-man-one-vote. It is not
law yet, and the Council is as fiercely opposed to
the principle as ever. Hasty legislation !
“ Hi never sor such a hunreasoning, hun-
hintelligent, pliant majority has the majority that
supports the Reid Ministry. . . . Ponies hin
’arness. You will hobserve that Hi scrupulously
refrain from using the hoffensive term ‘working
bullocks.’ They har’a very hobedient, pliant set
of gentlemen.”—Parkes.
W.A. Parliament, like Westralia itself, is
mainly composed of John Forrest. A return
shows that during last session the fat knight spoke
no less than 1025 times. The nearest approach to
the Premier s loquacity was that of Mr. George
Leake, a clever lawyer who sits in opposition.
He caught the Speaker’s eye 389 times.
* There are vague rumors abroad that the
N.S.W. Council may throw out the Loan Esti-
mates, in which over £1,000,000 worth of the
year’s ordinary public works expenditure is
charged to loans; and that dreary body is solemnly
warned by the Sydney morning papers that, if it
does so, it will cause a dreadful scarcity of work
through the winter, and reduce hundreds of people
to hunger and misery. The attitude is that, if
the Council doesn’t allow G. Reid to cook the
public accounts by charging his ordinary exs. to
posterity, G. Reid will shut down on providing
work for the poor, and the Council will be respon-
sible for the consequences.
The programme of the
Kingston (S. A.) Government
this session includes a further
reduction of the Governor’s
salary, a consolidation of the
public debt to save interest,
and the is ue of a State
paper currency. So far,
Queensland is the only Aus-
tralasian province which has
attempted the currency ex-
periment, and, for want of a
State Bank, it gets its notes
into circulation in a cum-
bersome and rather unsuc-
cessful fashion through the
private institutions. How
S.A. proposes to get over
the circulation difficulty is
not explained. Probably the
simplest method would be to
print notes to the extent of,
say, a million, and issue
them in payment of salaries
and other liabilities, and re-
ceive them in payment of
taxes. By this process a
good portion of the issue
would be kept in constant
circulation, and would
gradually wear away, as all
paper money dies in the
long run, through fire, flood,
rats, and other causes. By
strictly limiting the quantity
in circulation, and accepting
the State paper in payment
of taxes, it should fie pos-
sible to keep it at par with-
out a gold reserve. And
some day in the dim future,
when many drunken swag-
men had lighted their pipes
with the State notes, and
population bad largely in-
creased, the circulation
would become so visibly re-
stricted as to justify the
issue of another million on
the same basis.

Some time last month, so says Fiji Times,
a block of 9356 acres in Baa district, island of
Vanua Leva, was sold for .£3OO, or about 9d. per
acre, for the freehold. Good old Crown colony !
A block of that size is no fleahite in such a limited
territory as Fiji. Things are better managed in
the neighboring Tongan group, where the freehold
is nominally vested in the king and never parted
with by the State.
The N.S. W. Council rejected the Jennings-
Dibbs Ministry’s land-tax in 1886; the Parkes
Cabinet’s land-tax in ’88; the Dibbs Cabinet’s
income-tax in ’93 ; and finally the Reid Govern-
ment’s land and income tax in ’95. Under these
circumstances, the pretence of the Sydney morn-
ing papers that it is the Protectionist party in the
Council which did the latest rejection is a hollow
affair. The Upper House is “agin” land and
income taxes at all times.
Although adult suffrage was granted to
S.A. by, last session’s Parliament, this year must
close ere the other sex can vote, as it is necessary
that the names shall be on the roll fur six months
before the franchise can be exercised. If the
Kingston Govt, is defeated and a dissolution
granted within the next three or four months, the
ladies, therefore, may be practically shut out from
the ballot for another three years.
Under the Dibbs tariff, says Premier Reid,
the wage-earners of N.S. Waffs paid £1,913,000,
and the propertied and professional class only
.£832,000 per annum, total £2,745,000. Under
the new Freetrade and land-tax, the wage-earners
are to pay £1,060,000 and the propertied class
£1,026,000; total £2,086,000. Question : How
are the figures arrived at, and who is to pay the
£659,000 which is unaccounted for?
At the very bursting-point of the N.S.W.
political crisis, the editor of the Australian
Star (which journal was the “ organ ” of the late
Dibbs Government) sat him down and wrote a
non-committal leading-article on “The British
Farmer.” A sign more significant than any other
of the odionsness of the Protectionist party being
compelled to pose before the country as mates
with and champions of the Legislative Council.
Melb. Argus and Age bandy customs
figures valiantly in the tariff issue. The weary
old fallacy that imports and exports added
together constitute the trade of a country is re-
iterated daily. If a Freetrade country exports
£lOO worth of wheat and gets back £lOO worth of
woollens —the transaction swells the customs re-
turns. If in a Protectionist country £lOO worth
of locally-made boots are exchanged for £lOO
worth of locally-made hats—the transaction stays
out of the customs returns. Therefore, Freetrade
is, etc. Q. E.D.
Borrowing is “in the air” in Maoriland.
After years of abstention tbe province has once
more listened to the voice of the financial charmer,
and the success»of its 3 per cent. Joan has roused
a vague inclination for another vigorous Public
Works policy, and another boom. The feeling is
that borrowing is a curse, but that when the curse
can be obtained at so amazingly cheap a rate it is a
pity not to take advantage of the opportunity.
And Seddon, who seems to be only a large,
bounceable opportunist after all, is apparently
lending his capacious ear to the idea.

and the money received from him under this head
is to be “ earmarked ” and devoted to liquidating
