Smoke Eagle Cigarettes,
The Turner Ministry has so far persisted
in its resolve to enforce payment of the S' lectors’
arrears, despite trie bitter wail of the Fat Men of
both Houses that such a policy would utterly ruin
the “ poor farmer.” A considerable amount has
already been collected, and payments keep coming
in steadily. The result, so far, is to show that
nearly all the selectors in arrear are people who
have sold out to banks, finance companies, and
Fat Men generally. The'aggregate Stunt Person
of Victoria had simply left the properties standing
in the names of the previous holders on the
assumption that the State wouldn’t unduly press
the “poor farmer” for fear of losing his vote.
The real selector seems to have paid up very well
on the whole, and now Fat Man is also beginning
to send in his cheque with many groans, and
means to get level at next election.
WALLACE, by Carbine, out of Melodious. One of the prizes in St. Alban's distribution.
Photo. by Talma.
According to law, all N.S.W. public works
costing over £20,000 have to be authorised, in the
first place, by the Works Committee. A number
of works costing over £20,000, winch have not yet
been authorised, are, however, included in Reid’s
present Loan Estimates, and are being rushed
through Parliament. This brings them under the
eye of the country in time for the general elec-
tion, and the constituencies where the money is to
be spent will “ feel good ” towards the Ministry
in consequence. If the usual course had been fol-
lowed they couldn’t have been included in the
present estimates, and the constituencies wouldn’t
have had the same reason to vote straight in the
noble cause of the importers. The Works Com-
mittee may, of course, reject them later on, but
the election will be over by that time.
F. : Murray Smith, the undismayed cham-
pion of a discredited cause, is a heroic figure iu
Vic. politics. Mistaken though he is on Aus-
tralia’s fiscal needs, his partners in error, the
Staughtons and Maddens and Carters, appear as
ludicrous pigmies beside him. The senile Argus,
like the common scold it is, now slangs this one
man apparent in Freetrade ranks—for what? For
moving in the Legislative Assembly to give effect
to the alleged platform of the alleged tariff-
reformers. Murray Smith, honest himself, thought
his party organ honest, and moved to give effect
to the Wilson and Mackinnon policy. His motion
became the mock of the House because the Argus
lie that the House was pledged to a 25 per cent,
maximum in tariffs was —a lie. Next day, instead
of repudiating the lie, the Argus repudiated
Murray Smith, who, among free-raiders, is the
one Freetrader found.
Melb. Age and Sydney Telegraph are
throwing huge quantities of shipping statistics at
each other. The Telegraph declared that the
trade of Victoria was dead, and that Sydney had
become the great shipping port of Australia. The
Age replied by pointing out that the inward ton-
nage at Sydney had risen in ten years from
1.608.000 to 1,959,000 tons, while that at Mel-
bourne had risen in the same period from
1.513.000 to 2,038,000 tons, and thus showed not
only a larger total, but a larger and steadier
increase. The Telegraph then pointed out that
500.000 of the Melb. figures were practically bogus;
tbe mail steamers were reckoned when they called
on their way from England to Sydney, and again
when they called en route from Sydney to Eng-
land, while, as they made only one call at Sydney,
they were reckoned at that port only once. There-
fore, it deducted the 500,000 tons, and showed that
Sydney was ahead. Then the Age responded by
declaring that at Sydney vessels were reckoned
whe,n they arrived from over-sea, and when they
came back from coaling at Newcastle they were
counted a second time, and that 540,000 tons of
Sydney’s record was bogus in consequence. The
Telegraph blankly denies this, and that is as far
as the fight has got just now.
In Maoriland all Crown witnesses are paid
by the Treasury, therefore everybody who knows
anything against the accused, and many people
who know nothing at all, come forward to testify.
Witnesses for the defence are not paid at all,
therefore even the people who have evidence to
give on the prisoner’s behalf often fail to come
forward. Recently a man was accused, by a girl
of shady repute, of criminal assault, the alleged
offence having been committed in a distant town.
The Crown brought its evidence along, but six
witnesses, who could prove a conclusive alibi, re-
fused to appear unless their travelling ex.’s were
forthcoming, and the aceuse.il was absolutely
stone-broke. He was just getting along favorably
towards a long term of penal servitude when a
kindly bloated capitalist put up money enough to
meet the emergency, and an acquittal resulted.
If it hadn’t been for the capitalist he would
have been convicted, probably with a full know-
ledge of the circumstances on the part of the
“Crown” aforesaid. Wanted, a Public De-
fender.
Steff Cassander : “ Talk about the Chinese
living cheaply and doing no good for a place.
You should see the Calcutta Indians in Fiji. The
coolies, after they have served their time, do not,
as a rule, return home, but go in for vegetables,
Chinese fashion. Tin y grow all the vegetables
at Levuka and Suva, and are among the milkmen
of the place. What would keep a Chinaman a
month would keep a coolie a year. Never coold
more wretched specimens of humanity be sees
than in these two Fijian towns.”

The Ministers’ Union (Q.) has decided too
recommend the various pastors to bring the
question of the lax enforcement of the Sunday-
closing laws before their congregations. A year or
two spent in the godless North, among the bearded
mining Sabbath-breakers, turns the hair of the
average sky-pilot prematurely grey—or the con-
science.
H. : “ Radicalism has everything to gain
by the substitution of a Tory Cabinet, in Eng-
land, at this juncture. A Liberal Ministry is in-
variably a delusion, because it has such excellent
pretences for showing how not to do it, whereas a
Tory one has to under-bid its rivals. As witness
Disraeli, who, as far back as 1859, advised the
slash he accomplished in ISG7, but which, mean-
while, Palmerston, Gladstone & Co. staved off.”
In a case of alleged larceny heard at the
current Sydney Quarter Sessions before Judge
Docker, the jury was locked up Wednesday
night, Thursday night, Friday night, and from
Saturday till Monday. Now, had this been a
gallows case before a gallows Judge, the hearing
would probably have been Mount-Rennied, in
order, if you please, that the jury should not be
locked up over the Sunday. That, by the way.
was one of the coercive arguments judicially used
in the case of George Dean. But, in a simple
case of larceny, where no human neck is demanded,
the tender feelings of Justice are not concerned as
to the comfort or inconvenience of the jury.
With the beginning of the really cold
weather in Melbourne it is strange to see the lead-
ing drapery houses sacrificing their winter goods
to make room for an early show of Spring fashions.
Such is the case, however ; and it is marvellous
th is winter to note the extreme manner in which
the prices of articles which one would have
thought would just be in the greatest demand—
such as furs, sealskin j.-* ke s, winter mantles,
dress stuffs. &c. —are reduced at Messrs. Hicks,
Atkinson & Sons’establishment in Collins-street,
during their existing stock-taking sale.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The V.R.C. committee has been asked to state
definitely its official sentiments on the totalizator ques-
tion The “tote” discussion meeting takes place next
week (IO'.V. If some of the secret adherents of Ikey Mo
declare themselves on the side of the tote, for the sake of
appearances, the tote’s real friends will carry a vote of
confidence on the machine and arrange a petition to
Govt. . rty the way, how many members of the V.R.O. are
under Ikev Mo’s thumb?
?»♦ * *
Should Vic. Tote Bill pass in its present form
some of the proprietary clubs will .likely go under, a
clause providing that they shall be debarred from using
the machine. The tote, when in full swing under legal
sanction, will cause the raising of stakes all round, and
meetings of clubs outside the pale will be scarcely re-
garded as even bread-and-butter fixtures should they
survive.

If Dreamland has progressed in the way he
should go since Nov. last, that handsome son of Trenton
and half-brother to Mentor is a likely winner of Melh.
Cup Dreamland has fine breeding, fine appearance and
courage, and a fine two-year-old performance on his side.
There is a solid excuse for his failures of last season. The
horse was knocked out in the early spring by a Ijad
attack of strangles, and would have got a long rest if his
rapid recovery hadn’t tempted Scobie to make a desperate
effort to win the Derby with him, after all. The handi-
capper has weighted Dieamland on his form— 121 b. below
Harvester—and his chance for the Cup is good, if he s
thoroughly himself again. In the meantime it would be
quite good enough to bet even money against any parti-
cular horse starting for any particular race four months
hence. .

Joe Thompson has had 1000 tickets for St.
Alban’s lottery sent him, and under English law is liable
to prosecution if he sells them. So, as Joe doesn t see
the force of giving them away to his pals, it is likely he
will s°nd them back to Brisbane again. “ You may
swindle the public over a bogus mine, but it is not legal
to run a thoroughly honorable lottery in England.”

“Meston,” the member of Sydney Tattersall’s
who was considered a cert, for the billiard tournament,
and was b. ked to win thousands, went down before
. bookmaker Kurts last week. “Meston’s” downfall was
not so much due to Kurts’ superior play as to the fact that
he was badgered and chivvied “ off hishead ” by a section
of the leather-lunged who thronged fie room. Coming
on top of the Pearl Powder-Merry (I rl scandal, it has
rather disgusted manv of the private members, who,
now recognising that you can’t touch pitch without being
defiled, talk resignation.
And by the way, the books at one time decided
not to pay out over “ Meston, as they 7 considered him
“ rung in,” but they were evidently only waiting to see
how he fared for a round or two before showing their
hand Under these circumstances, Meston and his
backers have a good case for not paying up. It is a rule
of wagering that it is no bet if there is no possibility of a
win. And if Meston hai won, some of his backers had
“ Buckley’s show ” of a settlement.
The ambition of the wealthy Mr. S. H. Gollan
to ride a horse of his own as an amateur and win a big
steeple has at last been achieved at Hawke’s Bay (M.L.),
where he bested a good field over 34 miles on the cham-
pion. Norton. Next year Norton will be nominated for
Liverpool (Eng.) Grand National, in which his owner
hopes to ride him. , * *
The automatic poker-hand machine at Melb,
Stock Exchange Club is well-nigh worn out, seemingly.
It has got into the way of absorbing a threepenny-bit
every now and then, without throwing up a poker hand
in return and the bar-tender (the machine stands beside
the bar-counter) is quite accustomed to being asked for
another coin in place of the one which missed fire. No
machine can be expected to swallow “ thnpmes and
vomit five cards all day long until the crack of doom.
The strain upon the Stock Ex. Club machine has been
something awful. It takes as much as £3O a day down
the slot-2400 droppings of the coin and lightning changes
of the cards: 240 per hour for 10 hours-4 every minute.
No wonder the poor works want greasing. Even
if it plays fair as far as the cards are concerned the
machine doesn’t lay fair odds. A pair of pictures entitles
the lucky investor to one cigar, and a straight-flush wins
fifty or seventy-five cigars, say the latter. Seventy-five
sixpenny cigars to a thripny-bit-150 to 1 against picking
up a straight-flush. Thus far, the machine has produced
one s.-f. from about a quarter-of-a-raillion attempts.
» • * *
A co. in which the Rothschilds are interested
has taken up the patent pneumatic billiard cushion, in-
vented by one Ives. The new cushion seems bound to
supplant the best of those now in use. Cold or heat makes
no difference in its condition ; it is absolutely true, can
be tightened up or relaxed—made fast or slow with-
out trouble ;it is cheaper than other cushions, and will
last four times as long, if not for ever. All this on the
testimony of John Roberts, who has sampled hundreds of
hew billiard inventions in his time, and almost invariably
found them not worth mentioning. The champion swears
by the pneumatic cush. It has another virtue, which
ought to recommend it strongly to unskilful performers.
The edge of the cush. is Jin. lower than the lowest manu-
factured hitherto, and the rail is Jin. lower. You get a
Clear half of the ball above the cushion when tucked
up," and don’t have to make an agonising effort to
•trike it. . .

Woodcock, the Leicestershire bowler (writes
Tub Bulletin’s London correspondent), is faster than
Richardson, though only a little fellow. On ms day he is
almost unplayable, and he and Ppugher will bring the
hunting county well to the front this season. All jour
late visitors seem to be getting into good form and are a
great draw wherever they play. Lockwood appears to
have been forgiven his sins.

Stoddart is anxious to see an Australian eleven
visit England in ’96. piloted by Ben Wardill or Phil Sheri-
dan, and captained by Geo. Oiffen, and has written Oiffen
to that end-suggesting that the team’s arrangements in
England be left to the Middlesex C.O. At the banquet
given him by the Hampstead Club recently, Sloddart
said he would like to pilot another team to Australia in
’97 if his private affairs so allowed.

Melb. cricket opinion favors the selection of the
level-headed Blackham (although in his cricket decline)
as captain of the next Australian eleven, instead of the at
times over-confident Giffen.
* ♦ • ♦
The Sydney and country footballers of N.S.W.
met on Sydney Cricket Ground last Saturday. The
country men were picked from the New England, the
Northern, and the Western Unions, and the way they
played in combination shows that they study football as
a science. Abbott, the best three-quarter back on the
Sydney side, lacked his usual vim. Whiteside, too, of
whom much was expected, fumbled like a raw schoolboy
at times. But, though one or two individual men were
thus disappointing, several of the Sydney forwards, and
Wickham and Galloway played in best form. Wickham
got a try after a remarkably clever run right through the
opposite side, and Lane, after getting one try would
have got another but that his boiler burst within ten
yards of the goal-line and he couldn’t foot it fast enough
to outrun the opposing back. The game was up and
down, down and up, until the whistle went, and the
Sydney men, by individual merit and good luck, came off
victors by 14 points to 11.

John Gillespie and Co. v of Bathurst-st ,
Sydney, oriental merchants and manufacturers ;
importers of rice, spice, coffee ; oat and barley
millers, began their Australian career many years
ago with an excellent name and an excellent pur-
pose. That name, backed by ability and honest
enterprise, has pushed this extensive concern into
the van of prosperity. Other businesses have
fallen before the depression, but this well-managed
institution still progresses. If N.S.W. could be
proportionately prosperous a new era would begin
with the happiness of a nation.
Imitation is the sincerest flattery. The
best tilings are generally copied. Messrs. Staer-
ker and Fischer, proprietors of the renowned Ster-
ling Stainless hosiery, with the diamond brand,
have been granted an interim injunction restrain-
ing Messrs. L. Stevenson and Sons Ltd,, of Melb.,
from infringing their trade marks, or colorably
imitating them. This is not the first action which
has had to be taken in the same connection.
Owing to the superior quality and dye of the Ster-
ling Stainless hosiery the temptation to trades-
men and importers to copy the celebrated brand
is very great, and the proprietors have frequently
to act on the defensive. A “ public notice ” ad-
dressed to warehousemen, dealers, and the public
generally, in connection with this latest trouble,
appears in another portion of this issue.

Sydney photographers.
Those who are troubled with coughs,
colds, bronchitis, asthma and similar complaints
will be glad to hear that a new remedy, Anti
koffyn, is now available. It consists of pastilles
which are burnt in the Antikoffyn inhaler, the
fumes being drawn into the nose or mouth, iln
new remedy is cheap, simple, according t<
many enthusiastic testimonials —highly efficient.
The patentees and manufacturers are Reed and
Bury, of 8 Bridge-street, Ballarat (Vic.), and ot
Clarendon-street, South Melb.

“DIAMOND BRAND STERLING STAINLESS HOSIERY.”
Staerker & Fisher v. L. Stevenson S Sons Limited.
is hereby given, that in an action by Staerker and Fischer, manufacturers
and exporters of hosiery in Germany, and carrying on business as warehousemen
in Flinders-lane, Melbourne, against L. Stevenson and Sons Limited, warehousemen,
Melbourne, an interim INJUNCTION was, on the 24th of June, 1895, GRANTED by
the Supreme Court of Victoria restraining the defendants, their servants and agents,
from infringing the plaintiff’s trade marks numbered 3028 and 3826, and from colo.ur-
ably imitating the same, and from doing anything calculated to enable the defendants
to pass off their hosiery as and for the hosiery of the plaintiffs, or to enable the
defendants’ hosiery to be sold or passed off as the hosiery of the plaintiffs, until judg-
ment in the action or further order.
Warehousemen, Drapers and Others
are hereby cautioned against manufacturing, importing, selling or offering for sale
hosiery other than the goods of the plaintiffs bearing the diamond device or any device
or combination of words infringing the registered trade marks of the plaintiffs, or color-
ably imitating the same in such a manner as calculated to deceive.
Dated this 27th day of June, 1895.
C. J. BARROW, 3t9 Collins-street, Melbourne, solicitor for plaintiffs.

The other day a Melbourne man, convicted
of trying to shoot somebody with a pistol
which missed fire, was told by Judge Williams
that there was no doubt he intended to com-
mit murder. The Judge didn’t ask for proof
of this murderous intent-he took it for
,e man had been an ex-
M.l. travelling on an invalid railway-p.,?s, the
Orovvn Law authorities would have required
pvooj' that he intended to defraud the revenue,
and, by the same token, an ex-member accused of
attempting to shoot ” would probably escape
prosecution on the ground that nobody cojild
swear he meant to do any harm. But an unknown
man is judged by the laws of common-sense.

Herbert Gladstone proposed a
Government grant for a statue to Oliver Cromv ell
at Westminster, a score of little Tory newspapers
railed against the “ monstrous joke.” Neverthe-
less old Noll is to get his statue, and the bulk of
the necessary funds has been subscribed by the
House of Commons. Also, the Government did
put the proposed grant upo'n the estimates, and
only backed down out of respect for the feelings
(alias votes) of the Irish-party. So much for Tory
cackle.

Ten cab-loads of prisoners were arrested
down south recently at a police-raided Chinese
gambling den. About a dozen others amongst
them the son of a widely known divine —retreated
per skylight to the roof, where they froze through
the bitterly cold night till early morning.

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “ Alien and
Ginter’s Straight-out No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “Pure Gold ” and “Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales fur the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
cc THREE CASTLES ” and “ CAESTAU. 77
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would riot be to the interest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“Three Castles” and “ Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HEYDE, TODMAN & CO. {Agents for W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Ltd.)

The Dean Case.
What is known throughout Australasia as
“the Dean case” came to an apparent ending
on June 28, when George Dean, convicted at
Sydney on April 6 of attempted wife murder,
and awarded sentence of death—afterwards
commuted to penal life-servitude—was released
from prison. No criminal trial has so agitated
this continent since Frederick Bailey Deeming,
the Rainhill and Windsor murderer, was
arraigned and executed at Melbourne in 1892.
* • •
George Dean, the central figure in the pro-
ceedings, is 27 years old, and is employed as
master of a ferry-steamer plying between
Sydney and the marine suburb of North Shore.
Kis appearance and character are singularly
manly and prepossessing. On several occasions
he has saved others’ lives at the risk of his own.
The unanimous testimony of employers, friends,
and acquaintances declares that he is the last
person in the world to commit the crime with
which he was charged. The police, prompted
to an unusually searching scrutiny of his past,
could only say that when a boy' of 13 he had
been (illegally) convicted by a country bench of
magistrates of using, without the owner’s per-
mission, a horse which he rode to the races in
company with another boy, and that he had
been sent to gaol for three months because
unable to pay the £5 fine imposed. This,
indeed, shows how brutality and ignorance
combine to manufacture criminals ; but it can-
not be held to stain a career which from then
till now seems to have been a model of indus-
trious integrity.
* * *
Contrasted with the character of George
Dean, that of at least one of his accusers stands
in keenest opposition. Caroline Seymour, alias
Gaynor, alias M‘Ewan, alias Asbury, alias (?)
-the mother of Mrs. Dean—is a ripe and
sinister fruit of the infamous British convict
system. At 63 years of age she suggests one
who might have taken part in the awful incan-
tation of “secret, black, and midnight hags ”
which lured Macbeth to his doom. The mind
pictures just such another dancing in the grim
cave while the weird sisters
Round about the cauldron go ;
In the poison’d entrails throw.
Originally transported from England to Tas-
mania; an almost lifelong thief or consort of
thieves, with a number of convictions tallied
against her, while behind the veil of that infernal
past creep shadows of malignant things dimly
guessed and hinted at ; Caroline Asbury—
once, perchance, more sinned against than
sinning—-steps into the light of publicity cast
by the Dean case a woman bold, vengeful, and
cunning; an adept in all the intricacies of
crime; a creature with as little sense of moral
responsibility as an adder.
• # #
To this woman, on May 16, 1874, at Mel-
bourne, was born an illegitimate female child—
registered as Caroline Gaynor, afterwards
known as May or Mary Seymour, now Mary
Dean. In the N.S. V\. country township of
Narrandera, 150 miles away, George Dean was
then a little boy of six. And the streams of
circumstance, bearing two dawning lives into
all but fatal conjunction, were steadily con-
verging.
• • •
Caroline Gaynor’s father, Denis Gaynor, a
frequently-convicted thief and a criminal- of
the worst reputation, was in gaol when his
daughter was born. The child was two years
and nine months old when her mother, Caroline
Asbury, was sent to prison for 12 months for
shop-lifting. Soon after her release, Mrs.
Asbury came to Sydney, where she was
known to the police ns a thief and har-
bourer of thieves. Thus the future Mrs.
Dean’s parentage and earliest associations con-
nected her with the lowest strata of crime ; and
when charges of crime became recently linked
with her name the past was remembered against
her. “Do men gather grapes of thorns?” it
was asked, “or figs of thistles?”
• • *
In 1881, when her child was seven years old,
Caroline Asbury seems to have made an effort
to loosen old criminal ties. Between mother
and daughter there has always existed a close
affection, honorable to both ;‘and it is reasonable
to suppose that Mrs. Asbury wished to give her
girl a chance of growing up to a purer life than
she herself had led. Taking the name of Mrs.
Seymour, she set up a greengrocer’s business,
which was carried on continuously till about
the end of 1893. She did not, however, relin-
quish old friendships. Victorian criminals
coming to Sydney made her home their
rendezvous. Women of immoral character
lodged there ; and Mrs. Seymour took part in
the management of an adjacent house of ill-
fame.
* • •
Tothis house Mrs. Dean swearsshe never went.
Several witnesses swear she went there more or
less frequently; and there is some evidence that
she was acquainted with immoral women, and
visited other immoral houses. Giving all due
weight to the statements on both sides, it is
abundantly clear that, considered as influencing
the formation of character, the later immoral
environment in which Mrs. Dean grew to
womanhood was almost as degrading as her
earlier criminal environment and her unfortu-
nate criminal parentage.
* * *
Mrs. Seymour’s house, in the middle of
1893, came George Dean, who had met Mary
Seymour at a picnic. His father, an ex-police-
constable, had died in his boyhood, leaving his
mother with six young children to provide fdr.
George, the eldest, after, working some time
with a blacksmith, came to Sydney, where he was
at first assistant to a butcher, then deck-hand
and subsequently master of a ferry-boat. Mary
Seymour was good-looking and attractive, and
Dean fell in love with her at once. Nothing
occurred to rouse his suspicions as to the
character and antecedents of herself or her
mother, and after an eight months’courtship be
married her on March 9, 1894. A child was
born on Dec. 26, 1894. Mrs. Dean states that
she was unkindly treated by her husband after
a month of married life, and her mother corro-
borates this. Dean denies it, and alleges that'
the only misunderstandings were caused by
Mrs. Seymour, who came to live with them, but
whom he presently ordered out of his home.
Independent witnesses state that Dean was
uniformly kind to wife and child. There is no
doubt that after some months Dean and his wife
lived unhappily together ; and that his antagon-
ism to Mrs. Seymour was keenly resented by
herself and her daughter.
* * *
The gathering storm burst on March 14 last,
when Dean was arrested and charged at North
Shore police-court with administering poison to
his wife with intent to murder her. He was on
23rd March committed for trial by the stipen-
diary magistrate, who, though he expressed
grave doubts as to the good faith of the
evidence against accused, could not himself
take the responsibility of dismissing the
case. The trial commenced at the Cen-
tral Criminal Court, Sydney, on Thursday,
April 4, before Judge Windeyer and a jury of
twelve. It ended late on Saturday night, 6th
April, when the jury returned a verdict of guilty,
with a strong recommendation to mercy on
account of Dean’s previous good character ; and
the Judge passed sentence of death. This was
commuted by the Executive Council to penal
servitude for life.
* * *
The trial presented many remarkable features,
of which three in particular are noteworthy.
r l he first was the grotesque assumption by
prosecuting counsel —to some extent supported
by medical evidence, and acquiesced in by
defending counsel, Judge, and jury that
strychnine and arsenic are mutually antidotal.
Mrs. Seymour and Mrs. Dean swore that Dean
administered to the latter various articles of
food and drink which made her purge and
vomit; the doctor attending Mrs. Dean testified
that her symptoms agreed with those to be
expected after strychnine and arsenic had been
taken in certain quantities ; and analysis of
liquors said to have been partaken of by Mrs.
Dean, and of fluids and solids alleged to have
passed through her body, showed that these
contained arsenic or strychnine, or both to-
gether, in more or less poisonous quantities.
8o much arsenic, and so much strychnine, were
found, that it became a question how Mrs.
Dean could have swallowed the quantity she
wasalleged to have swallowed and survived; and
in order to remove any doubt, prosecuting coun-
sel stated that “ fortunately, with the arsenic an
antidote in the shape of strychnine had been
administered, and although a sufficient quan-
tity of the former poison had been found in the
medicine to kill several persons, fatal results
did not ensue.” r lhe verdict of the jury was
doubtless influenced by this false assumption,
which is justified by no evidence known to
medical science. It is possibly true that the
stimulant effect of strychnine may to some
extent counteract the generally deadening in-
fluence of arsenic on the heart’s action ; hut
that the counteraction has antidotal value where
poisonous doses have been taken is an assertion
a priori improbable and unsupported by a single
shred of proof.
• # #
The second remarkable feature of the /rial
was that the jury’s verdict appeared to very
many unbiassed persons to be quite against the
weight of evidence. Allowing for the anti-
dote fallacy, allowing for the circumstance
that the characters and history of Mrs. Dean
and Mrs. Seymour were imperfectly made
known to the jury, and passing the points
that Dean was not shown to have obtained or
possessed poison, and that he was an extremely
unlikely person to commit such a crime ; there-
remained the curious fact that the jury lingered
in indecision until Dean entered the witness-
box and gave the only evidence which under
the circumstances an innocent man possibly
could give, the very evidence which under the
circumstances a guilty man certainly would not
give, and then made up their minds that he was
guilty. So many people received a contrary
impression, that it appeared a fair inference that
either the jurymen or a majority of the public
were incapable of comprehending the value of
evidence.
* *
The third and most remarkable feature of the
trial was the conduct of the presiding Judge.
He deprecated what he termed the attempt to
blacken (!) Mrs. Seymour’s character, and
summed up strongly against the prisoner. The
jury retired at 12.25 p.m., and as at 8.35
p.m., according to the newspaper reports, they
had not agreed upon a verdict, the Judge
recalled them and said that “the issues were
so clear in the case ... that it was almost
a scandal upon the administration of justice
that they could not come to a conclusion.”
Then he pointed out that “unless the jury
agreed upon a verdict, they would have to
remain incarcerated in their room until Monday
morning, as the law did not permit him to do
anything else.” The significance of this in-
decent hint to hasten—indecent, seeing that a
man’s life was at issue—was not missed by the
jury, who apparently took it as a menace of
60 hours’ imprisonment ; and in about seven
minutes they returned with their verdict.
Then the Judge (who had hinted to the jury
that it would he open to them to recom-
mend the prisoner to mercy while finding him
guilty) in passing sentence, used this extra-
ordinary language to the prisoner : “ I am as
satisfied that you poisoned that woman, your
wife —not once, or twice, hut on every occasion
that she fell sick—l am as satisfied of it as if I
had seen you put the poison in her food.” Con-
sidering the circumstances, considering the evi-
dence, warrant for this statement will in vain
be looked for. (The Judge’s notes, hy-the-way,
omit all mention of the fact that he re-
called the jury to express surprise at their
delay in coming to a verdict, and state that
the jury returned at 9.55 p.m.)
# # «
All these things combined to make a deep
impression on the public mind, and an agitation
for the release of Dean was immediately com-
menced. It gathered strength so quickly and
so markedly that on Bth May the Government
yielded to its urgency and appointed a Commis-
sion to investigate the case, and decide whether
Dean should serve his sentence or be released.
The members of the Commission, which sat
at intervals for several weeks, were Mr.
F. E. Rogers (president), Dr. Sydney Jones,
and Dr. F. N. Manning. The appointment of
a lawyer as president was perhaps inevitable ;
yet many doubted whether any practising
lawyer, however able and honorable, could
wholly resist the immense social and legal influ-
ence which was being brought to bear in order to
uphold the conviction, or could come to a per-
fectly unbiassed conclusion. The reputations
of the presiding Judge, of the Attorney-General
and other prosecuting counsel, of the Police
Department—which had carefully gathered the
evidence which told against Dean, and had
strangely omitted to bring forward the evidence
which told in his favor—were more or less at
stake ; and all the forces of officialdom leagued
to keep Dean behind the walls of Darlinghurst
gaol.
* * #
But the Citizens’ Defence Committee—fight-
ing with funds raised by public subscription
against the bottomless purse of the Government
Law Offices ; fighting a bureaucracy supposed to
be impartial, but pricked by the spite of wounded
vanity into venomous party aggression—brought
forward a mass of new evidence which should
have been produced by the prosecution at the
trial, or before the trial ; evidence on which the
foregoing narrative is chiefly based ; evidence
which impelled a majority of the jurymen to
say publicly that, had it been presented to
them, Dean would never have been convicted.
Mrs. Seymour’s character was slipwn to be the
worst possible; Mrs. Dean’s character was
seriously impugned ; and the question for deci-
sion became practically whether their oath or
the oath of George Dean should receive credence.
• * •
The effect of the whole body of evidence left
little doubt as to the verdict of the Commission.
The Commissioners were asked to say whether
George Dean should be released from prison.
It appeared from the enquiry that it would
have been more to the point to ask why he was
ever sent there. Dean was accused of poisoning
his wife ; and his guilt or innocence is possibly
even now a matter of conjecture. But Justice
does not act on conjecture. Justice demands
that, before a man is imprisoned, his guilt
should be proven ; and Dean’s guilt has never
been proven. It was not proven to the jury
which convicted him ; still less was it proven
to the Commission. And as it is legally and
justly held that a man is innocent till he is
proven guilty, for the purposes of the enquiry
Dean was innocent and deserved instant release.
• * *
This conclusion a majority of the Commis-
sioners seem to have had no difficulty in reaching.
They did not need to consider whether Dean is
actually innocent, or whether he could afford
proof, or even reasonable presumption, of his
innocence. That would he too much to expect
of any man. Suppose, for example, that some-
body living under the roof of the legal President
of the Commission—who is understood to have
dissented from the verdict of his colleagues—
took a dose of poison from whim, or malice, or
“ that tired feeling,” or in a crazy fit, and then
turned round and said the President poisoned
her. How would he prove his innocence, sup-
posinghecould not prove her previous possession
of poison ? Her word would be as good as his ;
her innocent baby-face would appeal to an
impressionable mate jury more than his ; and if
he happened to be tried by a clairvoyant Judge
who, in his mind’s eye, saw him brewing a jug-
ful of poison—the President’s neck would be in
grave danger.
• • «
Several theories were put forward as being
capable of more or less plausibly fitting
the evidence. It was suggested that Dean
poisoned his wife ; that Mrs. Dean poisoned
herself; that Mrs. Seymour and Mrs. Dean
together administered the poison ; that Mrs.
Seymour administered poison without Mrs.
Dean’s knowledge ; that Mrs. Dean never took
poison at all. But the point was that the Com-
mission was not concerned with the relative
plausibility of these theories ; it had to consider
only whether the first one—that Dean poisoned
his wife—was not only plausible, but proven ;
and the medical members of the Commission,
Dr. Sydney Jones and Dr. Manning, decided
that it was not proven, and recommended the
release of Dean. Mr. Rogers, the legal member
of the Commission, dissented.
* # *
The Executive Council endorsed the recom-
mendation of the majority, and on June 28, after
three months’ and two weeks’ imprisonment,
George Dean stepped, a free man, into the air
of Liberty. Compensation for the suffering
unjustly inflicted upon him has still to follow.
The daily press publishes a cable about the
proceedings of the New York . suicide chib. A
great crowd assembled outside the club’s room on
the occasion of the annual pow-wow. No speeches
were made by the clubmen, but lots were drawn
and the doomed man withdrew without emotion.
The message, of course, is “ borak ” on the face of
it, and was evidently sent by the hanging-re-
porter who always writes that the condemned
“ walked to the gallows with a firm step.”

Re barrister-doctor Todd’s letter to the press
explaining that Dr. Newmarch is not respopsir
ble for the erroneous theory, acted upon at the
Dean trial, that a poisonous dose of strychnine
and a poisonous dose of arsenic would be
mutually antidotal, Judge Windeyer’s notes:
report the witnesses as follows
Dr. Newmarch: Supposing a person took a dose of
arsenic and then took strychnine it would be an antidote
to jt. Arsenic deadens the pulse, but strychnine would .
quicken It. . . . Strychnine would have a bitter:
taste, and supposing she had taken some of it would ac-
count for her having a strong pulse supposing she Had
been suffering from arsenical poisoning. . . , The
taking strychnine would the better enable her to go
about.
Dr. Rennie ; “ I agree with Dr. Newmarch. The symp-
toms described are those of acute arsenical poisoning.
Strychnine, taken with arsenic, will account for the
steady pulse described. Arsenic deadens the heart's
action ; strychnine stimulates it. . . . Her being able
to move about in the afternoon would be accounted for 1
by her taking strychnine with the arsenic.”
After Mr. Meagher’s address :
Mrs. Dean, at the request of juror, then shows how
much syrup she put in the glass.
Hamlet says the quantity is a little over two ounces. . •
The strychnine would modify- the arsenic, to which it i»
antagonistic and would counteract it.
1. Will these witnesses admit that these notpsr
are correct? 2. Whether they admit it or hot,
are the notes correct? The Judge is, in the
eyes of the law, absolutely infallible as a re-
porter, His notes must not, in any circum-
stances, be impeached. You may question the
correctness of his law, but you must not sug-
gest that he made a mistake in his report of the
evidence. The situation as here shown is
indeed an edifying one.
Premier Reid’s Programme.
Premier Reid announced his policy and that
of his Government to a small meeting of Sydney
electors last Monday night. There are some
notably good things in the programme, and
some conspicuously bad ones, and some that
are not distinctly good or bad, but only mixed.
In this respect it is like all other programmes,
and, under the system of Party Government,
the country has to take or reject all these
things, and Mr. Reid also, as well as many other
things and persons not mentioned, in a lump.
G. H. Reid promises to reform the Council by
making it a nominee body of 60 members, of
whom 12 will retire every year, so that the
tenure of office will be only five years. The
proposal is not theoretically perfect by any
means, nor is it distinctly democratic, but it
must inevitably make the Upper House much
more progressive and up-to-date than it now is,
or than it can be under existing conditions, so
The Bulletin cordially approves of the idea
until a better one comes along. The Referendum
is to be introduced in a limited fashion, the
people being made the final court of appeal in
cases where the Council and Assembly are un-
able to agree. This also is the beginning of a
great and urgent reform. The land-tax, with
the same exemptions as already proposed,
is to be persisted in, and the Premier’s
firmness in resisting the demand for an
all-round tax, with no exemptions whatever—a
demand which was put forward under a bogus
pretence of fairness and equality—is commend-
able. New South Wales wants a graduated
land-tax, and a much heavier one than that
which the Council recently rejected—partly for
revenue purposes, and in still greater part to
prevent the ruinous iniquity of big estates
taking root in the country. These are the dis-
tinctly good features of G. H. Reid’s policy.
On the other hand, he does not purpose, so far
as yet appears, to abolish the present unwork*
able system of Party Government, by making
the Ministry an elective body, with a fixed
term of office. He professes a belief in Free-
trade and Federation—in other words, he in-
tends to hasten the union of the six provinces
of Australia by increasing the number of their
differences, and to make agreement easy by de-
parting as widely as possible from the policy of
his neighbors. It is still the cardinal principle
of his creed that local industries should be
sacrificed to the interests of the Sydney import-
ing ring, and that the local white man should
go unemployed in order that the country should
be flooded with the cheap products of the
Asiatic, who works 15 hours per diem, and the
hard-driven serfs of Europe, and the Queens-
land Kanaka, and other human flotsam and
jetsam. Aiso, he gives no promise to reform
his ruinous financial methods, and still retains
the proud pre-eminence of being the worst and
most politically dishonest Treasurer his province
has possessed within the memory of the present
generation. The country has to lump the Govern-
ment policy, and decide whether the land-tax
and the other reforms are, or are not, too dear at
the price. Also, it has to form its own con-
clusions as to whether the feeble Premier, who
has accomplished nothing at all so far, except
the shameless “ cooking” of the public accounts
and the sacrifice of Local Government to the
demands of the Fat Man interest, is capable of
carrying out the programme, and if he really
means to carry it out. And if the country has
doubts on these points, which it probably will
have, the other party will most likely be also
willing to promise Upper House reform and a
land-tax—at a time like the present the average
party will promise anything; and the com-
munity may sit down and try to decide which
of the two is more likely to carry out its pro-
mises, oris best able to carry them out, supposing
either of them really desires to do so. And
may the Lord have mercy on that community’s
soul!
F. W. Ward, of Brisbane Courier, is cer-
tain that Northern Senarationists are prepared in
case of their obtaining legislative autonomy’ to
take the risk of Labor domination. According to
the Courier editor, just back from a Northern
trip, the Separationists have watched the proceed-
ings of the Labor, members in Maoriland and
South Australia, and find nothing very horrifying
therein. J

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melh., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : —l, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books; 3, Stationary ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6. Toys; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures; 9,
Artist Materials ; 10, an Ornament Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery ; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long.

""A recent Vio. sporting swindle. A suburban
racecourse proprietor gets telegrams entering two or
three cr.ck horses for a small handicap. They are
weighted and put on the card, and although they don t
coin" mar the course no one appears to scratch them.
Consequently they are made favorites, anything else
being Borloto 1. Toen the cl -ver ones come in and get
their nion- y on at a good price about their special gee-
gee a horse which, but for the presence of the cracks,
would h - about 6 to 4, and, in fact, is qu >ted at that as
soo ias the gam is discovered. Quite lately a prominent
Melh. owner was fined for not scratching his horses for a
certain event. He objected and denied having entered
the nags, hut was shown a telegram authorising the club
to make the entry.

Among English thoroughbreds a sire, and dam of
the same color will reproduce that coloriu their offspring
586 times out of 1000. Two Arabs of the same color will re-
produce it in the foal 837 times in 1000. A white Arab
mare, mated with a stallion of different color, will have a
while foal 729 limes out of 1000. But, of course, the
number of recorded instances is too small to make this
rati > much more than guesswork.

* • • •
Mr. W. A. Long has always been both a keen
judge of a horse and a lover of racing, and, when an
undergraduate at Cambridge, devoted a good deal of ins
time to his favorite sport. Naturally he had his penchant
for certain horses, and, if I mistake not (writes The Bul-
letin’s London correspondent), he thought that Asteroid
was the best horse he had ever seen. Asteroid, it will be
remembered, won the Chester Cup under the heavy bur-
den of 9st. 41h., being then a five-year-old. Mr. Long
may, therefore, be pleased to know that Kiisallaghan, the
hero of the Chester Cup of this year, is a grandson of old
Asteroid, being by his son, Astrologer, out of Gipsy, by
the American-bred Brown Prince, a son of Lexington,
whose stock are so highly esteemed in the States. Kii-
sallaghan seemed to be the only horse in the race able to
stay the distance, hut I fancy his success was not quite
expected by either Capt. Machell or the astute Jewitt, or
he would not have started at 20 to X.

♦ * ♦ ♦
Baldheads, footballers, cyclists, cricketers; men
who fight, who love to talk fight, to watcli fight; came
in a living avalanche the other evening to George
Seale’s smoke concert at Sydney Amateur Athletic Club.
The songs alternated wdth three and four-round boxing
matches. Mr. “ Paddy ” Lane (wing forward to Wallaroo)
in shirt sleeves and pipe in mouth, presided ; the tem-
perature was above record, and a dense cloud of smoke
changed one singer into bacon before he had half sung his
song. Several good men boxed, and a finishing bout
between Messrs. Lane and Seale drew from a delighted
American lover of the game the exclamation that Seale is
the sort of man that is seen once only in a lifetime.
• • • •
Zimmerman’s courtship and marriage was quite
a Young Ladies’ Journal novelette. She came out of the
convent to travel, and, on her return trip on board
steamer, she met the dashing cyclist. It was “ love at
first sight,” and, until they were married, Zimmerman
wrote to her every day. They were married quite unex-
pectedly in April, and Mis. Z. has said already that
“ Arthur is not to race any more.”

W. G. Grace’s wonderful cricket form this
season must surely be the last flare-up of the expiring
candle. Or perhaps they have been bowling just a littl j
bit soft to die W.G.O. M. in order to feed public enthusiasm
re the national testimonial. Anyway, ids record of five
“centuries” within five weeks, 30 years after ins
first appearance in an All-England Eleven, is a wonderful
thing, a feat that no small batsman can hope to rival.
Grace is one of the good big 'uns who are always better
than the good little 'uns in athletic sports, as long as they
take care of the mselves. A fat man, too. The champion
who slowlv fattens on muscular exercise is the stayer.
The Bulletin would have a deeper respect for George
Giffeu if lie weighed another 211 b.
» » » •
The suggestion of the N.S.W. Rowing Associa-
tion that the question of Dawson’s amateur status should
he referred to an English tribunal, has mightily amused
Vic. rowing men. It isn’t to be expected that tribunals
which have ruled that a man who measures off a yard of
tape in a shop is not fit to he classed as a bona-fide
amateur, and that to retain amateur status you must
wear sleeves to your jersey, would decide in favor of the
result of the last race remaining unaltered. But, apart
from prejudice, it is hard to see how anyone could fail to
uphold the contention of ttie Sydneyites. Three months
before the race the N.S.W. Association announced in the
press that it objected to t he inclusion of any cash amateur
cyclists. Nevertheless, Victoria placed Dawson in her crew
without special warning to N.S.W. or Queensland. And,
as for the interpretation of the rules, Dawson was in-
eligible, no one who has won an “ open competition (not
an open rowing-competition) for money” being regarded
as an amateur. On the technicalities Victoria should
certainly lose the last inlerprovincial contest ; on the
merits of the case the result should remain as it was,
“ first past, the post.”
Years ago the chief authorities of the Victorian
police were ardent devotees of cock-fighting and dog-
fighting. At West Melbourne l.ek-up there was a cock-
fighting field-day fortnightly, and one of the treats
provided for the Duke of Edinburgh by Chief-Commis-
sioner Standish was an afternoon’s cock-fighting on
premises not very far from the Melbourne Club. When
one of the cocks got over the fence, out went the Duke in
chase of it.
• * * •
At a recent English cricket-match a professional
nearly run out was so pleased at the umpire’s
favorable decision that he joyousty jumped in the air.
The wicket-keeper, who still held the ball, knocked down
a stump (because the bails were off) while the man was in
the air, and appealed again to the umpire. The profes-
sional was then given “ out.”
• * * •
Apropos of the shooting-party which has just
gone from Palmerston to tackle the buffaloes on Melville
Island, doesn’t it seem a pity to slaughter the beasts
(estimated at about 20,000) for the sake of hides, which it
is calculated will only net two or three shillings apiece?
Melville Island is right in the track of shipping and could
soon be cleared of buffaloes by a smart party. Surely the
island could easily be proclaimed a preserve under Govt,
supervision.
« * * «
W. Hamilton, of Horsham (Vie.), has put up an
Australian record by chopping a 2ft. 6in. block in 4min.
32Jsec. —a quarter of a second under the previous best.
Hamilton’s opponent was Alf. Clarke, of Tas.

Prices at a Coolgardie scrapping-match : —Stage,
£2 ; chairs, £1; front seats, 10s.; back, 6s. When Jack
M’Aulifle, a Port Fairy (Vic.) boy, sent out Jack Dunne
in seven rounds for a £l2O purse and the gate, the latter
realised £240.

Mr, D’Arcy Wentworth, of the South Ken-
sington College of Science, a son of Mr. Fitz Wentworth,
won the mile and a half handicap at the A.A.C. meeting
at Stamford Bridge, Eng., in such style as to warrant the
belief that he is both a good stayer and a plucky runner,
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent. He was
on the 150yds. mark against sueli men as H. W. Thomas
at 130 and Munro at scratch.
• * * *
The Melb. Waterloo Cup is to be run this year
on Maribyrnong Plumpton, the Vic. Coursing Club having
funeralled. The well-known Mr. Cox is the promoter.
A few years ago Cox, by way of encouragement, added
1000 sovs. to a stake run for at Moonee Valley.

Many doctors advocate cycling for “nerves”
and general depression. Sydney will shortly witness a
cycling spectacle—its fattest doctor (20st.) pedalling
merrily day by day.

First sport: “ Well, how did you get on at the
races to-day?” Second ditto: “Not bad. I backed the
five first winners, and would have backed the sixth if I’d
had any money left.”

A Wonderful Cure.
Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Dear Sir, —In the year of 1871 I was eight days in the water sheep-
washing, and contracted rheumatism and stiffness in the joints, and
have tried all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney I had n
great difficulty in walking, and went to the Turkish baths, which did
no good. Passing your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E. W.
O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as I had, which led me to
try your Remedies. On Thursday before Good Friday I took the first
of your Pills; since then 1 have used 4 boxes Special Powerful Alfaline
Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with
Liver and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline Eucalyptus Oil,
and lam now, with the blessing of God, as well in health as I was 23
years ago. I can with great confidence recommend your Remedies to
anyone suffering from the same complaint. May you long be spared
under God to be a blessing lo suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully, JOHN KEAY.

PROSPECTUS
OP THE
Westralia Extended
Gold-mining Company
(No Liability),
Coolgardie, Western Australia
(To be registered in Victoria as a No Liability Company).
Capital, £27,000, in 27,000 Shares of £1 each.
7,000 Shares paid up to ss. are reserved for the
Vendors in part payment for the property,
who also subscribe for 7000 additional shares.
13,000 Shares are offered to the public at 2s. 6d.
per share on application, and 2s. fid. per share
on allotment.
Out of the proceeds of the sale of these 20,000
shares, .£2500 is to be paid to the Vendors • the
balance of <£2500, less brokerage and flotation ex-
penses (not to exceed .£400), will be placed to the
credit of the Company.
Directors:
(To be appointed at the first meeting of Share-
• holders,)
Bankers:
The Bank op Australasia.
Brokers:

bers.
~ Herbert W. Day, Moorabool- street.

~ A. W. Clark, 369 Coilius-street.

rpKIS Company is being formed for the pur-
I. pose of acquiring and working Mining Leas s
No. 1151 and 1639, containing 25 acres and 18 acre*
respectively or 43 acres in all, and known as tie
Westralia Extended Claim. These leases an
situated about 7 miles North of Coolgardie, ai d
immediately adjoin the Westralia Proprietary
and New Victoria mines.
The workings of the Westralia Proj rietary
Company on a continuation of the New Victoria
reef extend almost to the boundary of the two
leases. From the North end of their ground the
Westralia Proprietary Company have obtained
some wonderfully rich stone. This mine was
recently sold to an English Syndicate for .£15,000
cash and a one-sixth fully-paid-up share, equal in
all to .£32,000, and the New Victoria mine has
during the month been sold to Dr. Simon on
behalf of a French Syndicate for £40,000 cash.
It is the opinion of practical men who know the
locality, that the New Victoria reef will run right
through one or other of the leases.
A small gully which crosses the leases is rich in
alluvial gold, nuggets up to 4£oz. having been
found.
The principal work has been done on lease
1151 on what is known as the Western reef of the
New Victoria mine. This reef has been exposed
for a length of three chains in an open cutting,
and for that distance the lode averages four feet
wide. The reef is a very compact one, well
mineralised and having gold evenly distributed
throughout, and is estimated to average 2oz. per
ton.
At a distance of 60 feet to the East, of the
cutting a main shaft has been sunk to a depth of
85 feet. A drive was put in West 20 feet, and
the reef cut. Since then, the reef has been driven
on North and South, averaging 3 feet 6 inches in
thickness, and carrying gold in equal quantity to
the surface formation. Two other shafts have
been sunk on the reef to the North of the.cutting,
in both of which good gold has been found.
The New Victoria Company has crushed 140
tons from a continuation of this reef (taken from a
point where the reef outcrops on the surface, 12
feet wjde), for a return of 140 oz. 7dwts., which,
taking into account the imperfect crushing appli-
ances, is highly satisfactory.
A cross reef running from the Western reef
carrying coarse gold and averaging about 2 feet in
thickness has been traced for a considerable dis
tance towards the Eastern boundary of lease No
1151, which will be well worth further develop
ment.
On lease No. 1639 a shaft is being sunk to strike
the New Victoria reef, which, as before stated , h as
been worked 3 chains of the South
boundary, and it is expected the lode, will be
struck when a depth of 100 feet is attained.
The promoters have been working these lease
for nearly 12 months, and now offer them to tb
public so that capital may be raised to develop
the mine systematically. e
Mr. Tiros. Fowler, late manager of the New
Victorian mine, reports more favorably on the
property, and as he has a thorough knowledge of
the ground, and is so favorably known both in
Coolgardie and Victoria, his report, which can be
seen at the brokers’ offices, can be thoroughly
relied on. r
There is an abundance of timber for fuel and
general mining purposes, and water is attainable
at a depth of about 150 feet.
Intending investors will note there are no Paid-up
Shares, and that the promoters, in addition to
taking Contributing shares in part payment for the
property, are subscribing for 7000 additional
Shares on the same terms as the balance of 13,000
Shares are offered to the public, thus practically
showing their confidence in the future of the ihine
The promoters submit this property as a legiti’
mate, well-proven concern, having every coufi
dence that under judicious management it wil*
soon be on the list of dividend-paying mines. 1
Early application is necessary.
A telegram has just been received from Cool-
gardie stating that a large quartz formation has
just been struck in the shaft on the New Victoria
line of reef in lease No. 1639,. showing gold, and
that it has not yet been passed through.
This should have an important bearing on the
prospeots of the Westralia Extended.

Mar Lodge, the Duke of Fife’s home in
the Heelands, had the proud privilege of being
burnt out under the eye of England’s
royalty. The cablegrammer mentions with a
gratified smirk that ‘'the conflagration was wit-
nessed by Her Majesty,” who probably sat at one

relative was fully covered by insurance.
The early death of Miss Violet Yarley,
which elicited so widespread an expression of
sympathy throughout Australia, has been grace-
fully commemorated by the issue of a new song
specially written to illustrate the career of the
deceased songstress, and accompanied by an excel-
lent portrait of her. The song is named “In an
Old Wardrobe,” and is published at 2a., post free,
by Messrs. W. H. Glen and Co., of Melb. The
charming musical setting is the work of Mr.
W. J. Turner.
Within a few years the name of Dr. :
Jaeger has become a household word in the Eng- |
lish-speaking world; especially in these colonies 1
hardly anyone will be found who has not heard j
the name and who will not think at once of the !
natural woollen under-clothing that bears his
stamp. It is not so widely-known that the famous |
doctor himself attributes equal importance to the
question of the outerclothing. At present, many
people wear outer-garments made up from good
woollen materials, but the benefit to be derived
therefrom is lost by the insertion of cotton linings,
also of interlinings and paddings of questionable
materials. “Man should clothe himself in accor-
dance with the dictates of nature, which provides
animal fibre for his covering.” It took a long
time, until the heretic tailors were converted to
the new gospel, but the demand for the woollen
linings has steadily increased. The London
Jaeger Company opened the other day a special
depot in Conduit-street, London \V.,and arrange-
ments have been made at the Sydney and Mel-
bourne branches to supply these linings, which,
at a small additional expense, ensure to the wearer
the greatest comfort and increased immunity
against the climatic changes.

His Mother’s Mate .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The haggard woman sat on a step under the
electric-light, by the main entrance of the theatre.
She had a child on one arm, two more beside her, a
pile of papers on her knee, and a cigar-box, full of
matches, boot-laces and bone studs, on the pave-
ment by her foot.
A gentleman stepped out of the “ marble bar ”
opposite, stood for a moment on the kerb, glanced
at his watch and then across at the theatre. He
crossed over, and put his hand in his pocket as he
reached the pavement.
“Paper, sir?” cried the newsboy. “ Here y’are,
mister—News, Stau.”
But “mister” had noticed the woman and
walked towards her.
“Paper, sir! News—Stab !” cried the boy,
dodging round in front; then, with a quick glance
from “ mister’s ” face to the news woman.
“ It’s all right, mister ! It’s all the same—she’s
my mother. . . . Thanks.”
H.L.
The Sultan of Turkey has developed
“peripheral neuritis” over the Armenia worry.
Now, wasn’t it arsenic?
Professor Leonard, the man from Chicago
who shone in the Mein, firmament for about 48
hours and then vanished, described himself
as “a veritable key of destiny, unlocking the
doors that lead to the temple of ease, health,
wealth, and influence.” The wizard couldn’t
expect to do much for himself in that line of art.
II - is probably in search of a widow with capital.
A tine figure of a wizard stands a good show with
any susceptible female who comes along to enquire
whether she has a husband waiting for her.
“ Behold him at your feet,” &c., &o. Great
business. •
The Bishop of Salisbury was somewhat
shocked during his recent Australian tour by
“the struggle and competition and the commercial
spirit which infected communities of our brethren”
in the church and chapel trade. Seems as though
mother Salisbury must have travelled incognito
in Sunday morning trains, from which the church-
wardens look out of window at. the weather, and
say “ VVe ought to have a good collection,” or “It
looks bad for the plate,” as the case may be. To
get a, fair idea of, say, Melb. religion and its
inner workings, one must travel with the frock-
coated, dissenting-bearded gentry in the Sabbath
morning trains. The shepherd is paid a good
salary, and the management prays that the rain-
clouds will hold off until the tabernacle is
crowded.
A tine bush-horror is reported from Toora-
weunah (N.S. W.). A man made a fire at the foot
of a tree, and camped for the night. Naturally,
the tree burned through and fell on him, pinning
him to the ground. It kept on burning, the fire
approaching him almost imperceptibly. For
hours he yelled in hoarse discords for assistance,
hut the people who heard him assumed that it was
only a man in the horrors, and, apparently, men
like that are common at Tooraweenah, for nobody
came near him. He scratched up the soil franti-
cally with his nails and threw it at the fire, but it
still burned on. At last it reached him and
burned a place about the size of a dinner-plate—
burned it “almost through to the intestines”
cheerfully remarks the local journalist, and then
the anguish nerved him to a superhuman effort,
and he lifted up the tree and writhed out. The re-
port ends with the off-hand statement that he is
“doing well,” as if he were merely an interesting
family event of the Gamp description. They
take their horrors in good part “ out west.’’

Amongst the presents received by Bis-
marck on his 80th birthday was a sausage 20 yards
long. It probably came from France. The one-
time man of blood-and-iron has become the man
of blood-pudding-and-irony.
A weird new disease has just reached
Europe from Russia—whence the influenza came.
Pathologists believe it to be the “ foot-and-mouth
disease ” of cattle, probably transmitted to the
human subject by diseased milk. It is a fierce
fever which attacks the month, causing the loss of
all teeth in a few hours ; after which it kills, or
departs, leaving its victims toothlessi

Another death-bed story, its hero a wicked
(M.L.) publican who lived and died a joker. Two
rival parsons claimed the right to administer to
his last moments, and he was asked which he
would prefer. His back was to them, but he had
just strength enough to turn his head and faintly
say : “ Well, if I must have one of them, I think
I should prefer the old fellow with the red nose.”
And so he died.

Various Verses .
Quacks ; Unregistered—and Otherwise.
In “Dr, de Lion, Clairvoyant” (published by
Angus and Robertson, of Sydney), the anti-
spiritualistic Dr. Knaggs discourses, to use his
own rather bumpy phraseology, “upon the sub-
jects of Medical Clairvoyance and Quackery, as
the pernicious outcome of certain defects in
medical legislation in New South Wales.”
The proverbial difficulty of proving a negative
doesn’t hamper Dr. Knaggs with the quacks as
it did with the spirits. It is but seldom
that the sceptic gets the chance of demonstrating
that a spook is no spook ; whereas, with the
quack, the matter is simple. “ Ho isn’t numbered
and labelled ’--away with him ! ”
It is easy to believe, as the author further
assures us, that “every incident herein depicted is
founded on fact.” The story in which Dr. Knaggs
embodies his animadversions consists mainly and
evidently of descriptions of actual occurrences —
strung upon a thin thread of crude melodrama,
embellished with a few really first-class horrors,
and presented with a blundering directness of
style which is not without a certain bizarre merit
of its own. Dr. Knaggs is, at all events, well
worth reading for his incidents, which, besides
being “frequent and painful and free,” are such,
in most cases, as could only be made use of by a
man who thoroughly understood what he was
talking about. There is no need for wild surprise
at the resultant translation of the gentleman who
thought to re-oxygenate his blood with 4oz. of
chlorate of potash to half-a-pint of water, but
only a medical man would have ventured to repre-
sent a drunken skipper as “ picking himself up ”
with a wincglassful, each, of spirits of chloroform
and sal-volatile! His must, indeed, have been
the dura marinorum ilia, and Dr. Knaggs reaches
his highest literary level in his description of the
after-apprehensions of the tyro in posology who
should have instructed this marine phenomenon
to make 20 hourly instalments of what he paid
into his astounded stomach in one tremendous
dose.
About quackery in general Dr. Knaggs has
little to say, except that—like the power of the
Crown, according to a famous House of Commons
resolution “it has increased, is increasing, and
ought immediately to be diminished !” “Medical
clairvoyance ” is the mark at which the doctor
aims most of his bounding rhetoric, though the
“ clear-seeing ” fraternity should nevertheless be
not a little obliged to him for selecting as its
fictional exemplar “a man of the highest mental
power and culture”—a person who, according to
the author’s own showing, made excellent use,
under pretence of clairvoyance, of the medical
knowledge which he actually possessed. Dr.
Knaggs, like most enthusiasts, makes the tactical
error of proving too much, and it is a curious
“backing of his friends” to relate how—after
numerous duly-ticketed practitioners had suc-
cessively mal-“ treated ” a woman for liver, heart,
lungs, brain, and spleen—the pretended clair-
voyant, by the exercise of a little common observa-
tion, was able correctly to diagnose her ailment
and afford her the relief of which orthodoxy was
incapable. If any laugh comes in here, it is clearly
not against the “ quack.”
It is curious, moreover, that a man who, like
Dr. Knaggs, would so fiercely cast into outer
darkness the doctrine of clairvoyance, should have
so far taken into his bosom the not very dis-
tantly-related notion of “hypnotic suggestion” as
to seek therein an explanation of the easily other-
wise comprehensible influence of the villain of the
story upon a girl who, having taken to drink and
opium, timeously turns up in Sydney as a peg
whereon to hang a crowning catastrophe, ihe
nebulosity of the hypnotic notion according to Dr.
Knaggs, however, appears in the fact that whereas
the hero of the story is represented as having been
at one time hypnotically a mere automaton in the
hands of the villain, this same villain—when even-
tually the injured and vindictive hero proposes to
assassinate him with entirely new and original
effects—is apparently unequal to the very simple
and convenient “suggestion ” that said hero should
forthwith deliver himself over to the police as “of
unsound mind and unfit to be at large.” But the
hypnotic suggest!vism in which Dr. Knaggs be-
lieves resembles exactly, in this respect, the upiri-
tualism and clairvoyance against which he is con-
tinually crying aloud and sparing not—it goes
hopelessly “bung” the moment it is asked to do
anything of any practical use to anybody except
the professional converters of credulity into cash,
The central figure of the story—the adventurer
who modifies his name of Dillon into “ de Lion,”
and who might just as well have made it “ Dan-
de-Lion ” while he was about it-is evidently, in
many places, the mouthpiece of the author; in
which capacity his words are by no means
always those of either wisdom or good taste,
The airy complacency, for instance, with which
he brackets homeopathy with “faith-healing,
spiritualism, and other humbugs” is much on a
par with the smug superciliousness of the anti-
quated Melbourne .ZEsculapius who “believes there
is a man up-country who thinks he has discovered
in strychnine an antidote to snake-poison !”
Cheap oracularism of this sort is certainly not
characteristic of the real chiefs of a profession in
which the prizes do not always fall on the best
men, and which has no lack, within its sacred
enclosure, of be-lettered pretenders to a skill which
they do not possess.
The question of quackery, after all, is largely
one of degree. Whatever strides surgery may
have made, medicine proper is s ill little better
thanapok.ng m the darkand not oneaymanm
10,000 has any suspicion of the frequency of the
diagnostic and therapeutic blunders winch the
profession, for the sake of its collective repute
studiously endeavors to conceal. A medical
degree, mfact-whifeitmay mean,on theone hand,
that its holder is a born physician who has happ y
arrived, upon prescribed lines of study at is
natural vocation—may merely indicate, upon the
other, that its possessor, with perhaps a fine original
genius for butchering, or baking, or candlestick-
making—has barely scraped through an examina-
tion which entitles him to write himself Sangrado
instead of Dulcamara. But your Sangrado, of
the two, is the greater danger to the community,
and the plain man—bearing in mind such matters,
for instance, as the cheerful alacrity with which
the heroine of one shuddersome Sydney affair was
certified to as intacta, and the delightful toxico-
logical incertitude of the Dean ease—will be in no
hurry to confine the term “quack ” to those whose
mistakes are not secundum artem, and whose in-
competence is undisguised by any corporative im-
primatur.
All of whioh, however, furnishes no reason why
New South Wales should remain for ever the
paradise to which, from the ends of the earth,
resorts the medico-swindler of every degree—or of
no degree, and Dr. Knaggs will have rendered the
lieges a solid service if his book shall succeed in
opening the eyes of that astonishingly large
number of them who, while reasonably or un-
reasonably distrustful of the “ Art and Mystery
of Medicine,” deliver themselves over with
fatuous credulity to the mystery without the art !
It is a popular fallacy that the treadmill
used in British prisons involves the expenditure of
a large amount of physical force without the
smallest possible result. As a matter of fact, it
either pumps water or grinds flour for the prison.
All the same, it is a cruel and wanton torture,
prolific chiefly of varicose veins and vexation of
spirit.
The “ other-siders” are growing impudent,
not to say offensive, in Westralia. At Beverley,
lately, a small party of them, by way of a bitter
comment on the ways of the Westralian mail-
train, borrowed a railway-trolly and kept just ahead
of the train for seven miles, while they consoled
the driver and fireman with a wild chords of “ ’E
duuno where ’e are.” The locomotive made the

tioian, whose brusque, uncompromising demeanour
has robbed him of the Speakership, illustrates the
objections to him by the story of a friend who
travelled in N.S. Wales, Hie friend met a party
of local notables, and the talk was about certain
English Judges, and then about a certain Recorder
of London. “ I don t think much of him, said
the silent man of the party ; he s a very clever
man, but I always thought him far too senteu-
tious.’ (It was the Recorder who had sentenced
that particular notable to transportation.) And,
adds Mr. Courtney, “ I suppose that was my
fault as Chairman of Committees I was too sen-
tentious,”

From Sydney Evening News :
Large numbers of unemployed arriving in the north to
geek employment at cane-cutting. If cane-cutting will
reduce the number of unemployed, that is one more
reason against interfering with an exceptionally circum-
stanoed trade.
Why, certainly 1 Cane-cutting will obviously re-
duce the number of unemployed. And every
other industry which now exists in N.S.W.
through Protection, and is likely to be destroyed
under the new tariff, will also, if not interfered
with, either reduce the number of the unemployed
or else prevent large additions being made to their
number. And they are all “ exceptionally situ-
ated.” The Evening News constantly asserts
that Freetrade will_ lift a great burden off the
country and make it rich, and that Protection
keeps it poor. The unemployed are the natural
consequence of poverty. Then, if the News has
any semblance of faith in its own creed, let it
howl its loudest for absolute, unalloyed Freetrade
so that these unemployed may be relieved ; and
not sit miserably on a fence and try to get down
on both sides of it at once.
THE BIOGRAPHER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The great man’s soul ha» now forever flown
The scenes of every vict’ry and defeat,
While those whom he in life had never known
Exchange the maudlin mournings in the street,
Reciting loud his record from the roll
Type-filtered and distorted to the view ;
All cry for wordsmiths to inscribe his scroll —
But who ? But who ?
Not you, pen-prostitute, with eager ink
To flow where’er your hirers may direct,
Concerned in naught beyond what they may
think;
Your wish—but to supply what they expect,
To eulogise or censure as ’tis told ;
You care not what may be his rightful due
So long as you can trade your words for gold.
Not you. Not you.
Not you, with heart by loveless life made sour,
Joined without love and with desire still less,
Tliat he might taste the dead sea-fruit of power,
You—'advertise your grief with costly dress.
Too weak to boldly love and bear the gibe,
Children you bore to one you never knew ;
No less a stranger was the hireling scribe.
Not you. Not you.
Not you, with name of God as stock-in-trade
Threatening the weak and cringing to the great,
Lying to all by whom your wage is paid,
Claiming all good on earth to regulate;
Neat sentences of fulsome praise you turn
With copious tears well placed in public view
Commensurate with guineas that you earn.
Not you. Not you.
Not you, esteemed companion of his life,
Who fought ahd stole and lied with him who
died,
Who, midst the storm and sweat of daily strife,
Took manfully your welcome stand beside,
Helping him onwards, upwards to the goal.
Still! ’twas at best the husk of him you knew ;
You never saw the secrets of his soul.
Not you. Not you.
But you, who felt his passionate caress,
Beside whose true, fond love he reckoned
naught
The puerile plaudits of a paudar press,
The cheers of craven crowds for whom he
fought.
You, to whose eyes his heart he did unveil,
Who knew each virtue there and every blot;
You are the only one to tell his tale —
And you will not.
Melb.
MONTAGUE GROVER.
OMAR’S POTS.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
We are but earthen pots, and at thy will
We took the shape thou gavest—good or ill;
And if the wine from some small flaw outflow,
We cannot save it, but must let it spill!
If we are square, ’tis as thy hands did frame ;
We.wist not if to make us round thine aim—
Thou didst not ask us would we have it so :
Each fault we have is thine —and thine the blame!
