The Squatter .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We m u t him at Pierson'* private bar during the
wool-sales, and he talked of sheep, and clips, and,
above all, of “ his place.” The man’s exterior did
not give the impression of the owner of countless
flocks and herds ; but then, as the doctor remarked,
“These great pastoralists often dress as meanly
as swagmeu.” lie carried a large pocket-book,
whose plump proportions he would often refer to
in a casual way ; but it chanced on more than one
occasion, when a big score for drinks had to be
paid for, that he found his cheque-book had, by
some unaccountable oversight, been left at his
hotel.
“ Great men are often so forgetful,’’ the doctor
explained to me, as he cheerfully paid the reckon-
ing.
“You must come down to my place,” the squat-
ter would say as he parted from us ; “I am getting
tired of asking you, but if you want to have a
month’s real pleasure, wallaby drives, kangaroo
Stalking, and a genuine country home of the right
stamp, then come along. Send me a wire at any
time and my drag shall be at the station to meet
you.’’ This was how it came about that the
doctor said to me :
“ Let us go and sp-nd Easter with the squatter.
We shall have a bit of sport, a spell from the
grind, and a royal reception from our old friend.
1 am sure he will be offended if we deeliae his
invitation any longer.”
A long journey by a western train, and we were
deposited at a bush siding. No trap was there,
no hotel or township, nothing but the wild bush,
a weatherboard accommodation-house, and a rail-
way porter.
Evidently our wire had missed delivery, but we
were cheered to learn that the mail-coach would
take us within half-a-mile of Johnston’s place.
An hour later, like two Robinson Crusoes, a
gun on each shoulder, we left the mailman and
plunged into the bush-track he had told us to
follow. Our bulky belongings we had hidden in
the scrub.
A sound of chopping ahead, and we came to a
familiar figure astride a fallen apple-tree.
“Well, this is good of you,” exclaimed the
squatter joyfully as he extended towards us two
hands smeared with honey. “ Welcome to
Wooltergatta ! You are just in time to taste
some of my honey;” and he handed us each a
piece of comb from the hollow of the tree. “Now,
that is what I call honey—none of your Sydney
glucose about that, hey ! But come along ; you
must want some lunch.”
The squatter in moleskin pants and flannel
shirt, bare-headed and bare-footed, led the way
with a step of lordly hospitality.
“ Why did you not send a wire? I would have
had one of my fellows to meet you. Came by the
coach, I suppose? Beastly shaky old trap—a dis-
grace to 4 the district; but come along up to the
house.”
A three-acre clearing dotted with stumps, a
bark humpy and a lean-to shed.
“How is that for a homestead now? Look at
the view 1 Look at the prospect! I call that the
finest site for a residence on this continent. Leave
your guns there, under the verandah, and I will
take you up to the lavatory. You must want a
wash after your long journey ; then I will present
you to Mrs. Johnson—she will be as delighted as
she can be.”
The squatter led the way to a water-hole. Here
we “ sloushed ” our hands and faces, taking for a
towel some loose stringy-bark.
“ Nature’s fountain, my boys. The bath of the
gods. What do you think of that? None of
your Menzies’ hotel about my place, eh 1”
cheerfully agreed with him as we vainly
endeavoured to free our skin and clothes from the
g brftg
M s. Johnson, a red headed lady in a print
skirt and a slip body of flour-bag material, greeted
us on our return to the hut. “ You must wait a
* J ' t > misters, till I get you a bite of tucker; you
must be real hungry to be sure.”
Qn fcbe table there waB boiled p Urnpkini damper.
a bone u f corned beef, and empty jam-tins filled
with black tea. The doctor found a gin-case, I a
stool, and we drew up next to an assorted crew of
shock-headed youngsters.
“Now, this is what I call a bit of beef,” said
the squatter, flourishing his knife. “ I never kill
a beast over four years old ; that’s the prime age,
and I like it prime, you make no mistake.”
“ Why, dad, that’s a bit of the cow the dingoes
killed in the gully,” interjected one of the young-
sters.
The squatter did not heed the remark, or alter
for a moment his bland, benevolent smile,
“Now, try those pumpkins, you will find them
genuine—grown in my own garden—l can recoin-
m*-nd them. Make yourselves quite at home;
from the cellar to the garret, all is at your dis-
posal.”
For some minutes the doctor and I wrestled nn-
successfully with the slices of hide or hoof the
squatter had hacked off for us, drank the nauseous
tea made with creek-water, and chewed the heavy
chunks of damper.
“If you have finished your snack, gentlemen,
come and see the new house.” He led the way to
a cleared hill-side. “ How is that for a residence,
now !” and the squatter waved his hand as though
in front of a majestic building. “This is the
terrace with the marble steps—you will notice that
the drawing-room opens on to it with French case-
ments. The entrance hall is wide and spacious—l
like room to turn round. But come along this
passage to the billiard-room. There now ! Did
you ever see an apartment better lighted ? I have
given an amount of study to this room you would
hardly credit.”
We looked on inamazement. A few wooden pegs,
stuck here and there in the ground, were the only
substantial parts of the regal structure the
squatter conducted us over.
“Yon are not going home again? Why, I ex-
paotad you to stay a month at least; it will take
yon that time to thoroughly test the sport on my
run. Well, if you must, you must; I will force no
man. Let me call some of my hands to harness
up the sulky.”
But for some mysterious reason Bill, Tom and
Harry did not come when they were called. The
sulky, too, was unfortunately undergoing repairs
at a neighboring town, and the quest ended by a
freckled-faced boy catching an old horse and
harnessing him up to a wood-dray,
“Take your seats, gentlemen! Take your
seats !” The squatter was on the off shaft. “They
may say what they like about coaches and car-
riages, and, of course, a man in my position has to
keep them ; but for real solid comfort give me a
well-built dray. Now I know this dray, every
stick of it ; there is not a better in the country ;
you are real safe once you are aboard, that I will
guarantee. Now don’t she ride light? Fancy you
were in a feather-bed on a spring-mattress. When
I have a dray built ”
There was no end to the squatter’s talk. Even
when he had seen us safely seated in the returning
mail-coach, he was full of pressing invitations for
us to come again.
“ You should come when you have more time.
You have not seen my wool-shed, and the irriga-
tion plant alone would be a real lesson to you.”
We drew a sigh of relief as the coacli drove on.
“What is he?” enquired the doctor of the
coachman, nodding his head towards the squatter,
who was standing upon the dray, waving an adieu.
“ Harmless, quite harmless!” replied the driver,
throwing his nose in the air.
N.S.W.
HENRY FLETORER.
Perhaps the best epigram in “An Ideal
Husband’’: “A question is never indiscreet:
answers always are.”
Eugene, the ex-bookrnaker and prophet,
who foretold the total destruction of Maoriland
and induced a number of people to fly with him to
South Africa for safety, has been heard from. His
party of believers took the train from Cape Town
as far as the railway goes, and then hired wag-
goners to hump their belongings right out into the
dim infinite of the North. It was an indefinite
sort of contract, and after four weeks travelling it
was closed by the waggoners up-ending the sticks
of the believers on the banks of the Mashona
River, 230 miles from anywhere, and starting for
home. The- pilgrims settled down there and
waited for the promised cataclysm. They lived
by shooting while their ammunition held out, and
when it was finished they overtook slow animals
and hit them with the butt-end. Then they
somehow discovered that the convulsion hadn’t
arrived and Maoriland remained where it was,
and thereupon they, decided, as all such pilgrims
do, to get home again, and proceeded to pound
their gaunt mules along the interminable back-
track to the Capa. The prophet, however, was
left behind on account of “ some trouble about a
horse,” and by this time has possibly been eaten
by a hyaena—at least hopes are entertained to that
effect.
A Maoriland syndicate is making another
effort to recover the ,£140,030 in gold which went
down along with the General Grant among fne
Auckland isles in 1857. The General Grant had
87 passengers on board—mostly of the roaring
“luoky-digger” species, and one windless, pitch-
dark night a mysterious current drifted her into a
cavern among the islands. Her masts scraped
along the roof of the cave, and brought down a
rain of rook fragments which killed many people,
and maimed many more. Then she stove herself
in and sank peacefully many fathoms deep. Four-
teen men and one woman got ashore, and settled
down to lead an idyllic existence on fish and hope,
until something would turn up. But it was long in
turning up, and after six mouths four of them went
away in the solitary boat to look for Maoriland.
and were heard of nevermore. The others waited a
year longer, and then a whaler from Bluff harbor
found them and took them off. As for the afore-
mentioned £140,030, it is supposed to be still lying
around at the sea-bottom, and possibly a million
tons of sand have gathered on top of it by this
time. Anyhow, in spite of many attempts, not
one copper has been recovered —so far as anybody
is aware. As the person who recovered it would
probably say as little as he could on the subject,
however, for fear of claimants, it is quite likely
that the money has been found and drunk long
ago, and that the syndicate is getting its feet wet
on a fool’s errand.
The Age triumphs over the with a
charge of plagiarism from London Spectator,
which it clearly proves with parallel columns.
About a year ago, the Argus unearthed a similar
plagiarism on the part of the Age from Sydney
Smith, and an AGE-man was dismissed. The
heroics of the accusing journal in such cases are
preposterous. The reasonable inference, of course,
is that the accused journal has been swindled by
one of its staff, not that it is a swindler itself.
The hopelessness of the Turkish Empire
is almost pathetic. At the present time the
Sultan is under orders from “the Powers’’ to
provide the Armenian provinces with a certain
number of Christian officials. The end of Moham-
medan supremacy in Armenia must be very near
to the end of the Sultan’s sovereignty.
Ex-Commandant Tulloch, who tells them
at ’Ome that Victoria is damned beyond redemp-
tion by State Socialism and Payment of Members,
and a general want of veneration, shuffled two of
his sons into the army—one as a nominee of Melb.
University, the other as a nominee of the Vic.
Govt., whilst he occupied the position of Military-
Commandant down South. Evidently Tulloch
knows what he is talking about when he sneers at
the style of administration in vogue by the balmy
Yarra;
Countess Wachtmeister, the present Theo-
sophical missionary to Australia, maybe termed a
ghost of Mrs. Besant’s spiritual self. The
Wachtmeister discourses begin and end with the
philosophy she has adopted, whereas her famous
friend often treated of social questions from
worldly standpoints, whilst Theosophy lay dark
off the weather-bow. Theosophy, in Mrs. Besant’s
lectures, used to wait for an opening before sail-
ing right in, and sometimes it was 9.30 p.m. ere
the opening presented itself. Countess Wacht-
meister is a Low-Church Theosophist. She sticks
closely to the Word of Blavatsky. But this
plump, self-contained lady, who is more feminine
than Annie Besant, will gain converts as
she goes along. The doctrine of reincarna-
tion is not unimpressively preached by
Countess Wachtmeister. She knows her philo-
sophy well, although she doesn’t make the ;
m >st of it on the platform. There is a want of
oouseoutiveness in her sermon. She seems to fire
off each long sentence as it comes to her memory,
without a critical regard for its bearing upon the
previous announcement. Each sentence, how-
ever, has been carefully prepared, and if the
countess’s oratory proceeds jerkily it can’t often
be accused of wobbling. Moreover, all Theosophy
is good, a corrective to the fundamentally im-
moral isms of this world, and the adult inhabi-
tants of Australia are herewith recommended to
buy at least one packet of the Wachtmeister
specific and apply it to their immortal souls. It
can do them no harm, it may make honest, high-
thinking people of them. This exhortation is parti-
cularly addressed to regular attendants at places
of worship.
